---
layout: post
title: CS 32 Notes
---

Below are my notes from CS 32 during the Winter 2014
quarter at UCLA. I hope that current CS 32 students
find them useful. I also highly recommend the notes
and practice problems by [Andrew Forney](http://web.cs.ucla.edu/~forns/classes/winter-2014/cs-32/cs-32.html), one of the best CS TAs at UCLA. 

To read the notes offline:

* [Markdown version](https://ucla.box.com/s/avfnz8cwsyhgaajhk8x6eojdxw8p7gvq), best read in [Sublime Text](https://www.sublimetext.com/3), with the [MarkdownEditing](https://packagecontrol.io/packages/MarkdownEditing)
package installed. 

* [PDF version](https://ucla.box.com/s/hlutu0sewz1dvcae3tpc7i3iwddwm0vq)

[Open a pull request](https://github.com/KyCodeHuynh/kycodehuynh.github.io/compare) if you find any 
errors, and I'll gladly correct them. 

Happy studying!

---

<h2>
<a id="user-content-title-cs-32-notes" class="anchor" href="#title-cs-32-notes" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>CS 32 Notes</h2>


<ul>
<li>Lecture 1: 6 January 2014

<ul>
<li>A Little About Carey</li>
<li>Thoughts on Teaching</li>
<li>Time for CS</li>
<li>Algorithms

<ul>
<li>Algorithm Comparison</li>
<li>Data Structures</li>
<li>Data Structure + Algorithms</li>

</ul>
</li>
</ul>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-thoughts-on-teaching" class="anchor" href="#thoughts-on-teaching" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Thoughts on Teaching</h3>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-time-for-cs" class="anchor" href="#time-for-cs" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Time for CS</h3>

<p>Fiboannci sequence, where first two numbers
are 0 and 1 and successive numbers are sum of
previous two numbers. </p>

<p>0, 1, 1, 2, 3, 5, 8, 13, ...</p>

<pre><code>bool isFib(int array[])
{
    for (int d = 3; d &lt; 20; d++)
    {
        if (array[d] == (array[d-1] + array[d-2]))
        {
</code></pre>

<p>A girl named McKenna got it. </p>

<p>Solution: </p>

<pre><code>bool isFib(int arr[])
{
    int j; 

    if (arr[0] != || arr[1] != 1)
        return false; 

    for (j = 2; j &lt; 20; j++)
    {
        if (arry[j] != arr[j-1] + arr[j-2])
            return false;
    }

    return true; 
}               
</code></pre>

<p>On our exams, we'll have problems harder
than this with less time. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-algorithms" class="anchor" href="#algorithms" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Algorithms</h3>

<p>Algorithms are a set of instructions/steps
to solve a particular problem. </p>

<p>They receive <em>input data</em>, and produce
an <em>output result</em>. </p>

<p>They can be classified by <em>how long</em>
they take to run on a particular input
and the <em>quality</em> of their results,
e.g. the results of a path-finding algorithm, 
e.g. N cities would take N! steps for a 
perfect solution to the traveling salesman problem. 
Instead, we do approximations. </p>

<p>We want to be able to compare algorithmic
efficiency and quality. </p>

<p>Our isFib() was of perfect quality,
but would take N interations/steps. </p>

<p>How many steps for a N amount of input?
N steps? N! steps? </p>

<p>Bad algorithms can kill a program. They make
the difference! </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-algorithm-comparison" class="anchor" href="#algorithm-comparison" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Algorithm Comparison</h4>

<p>Example: Our program must guess
the word a user guessed. </p>

<p>Possible algorithms:</p>

<p>1: Randomly pick words from the dictionary
   and ask the user if that word is correct.</p>

<p>If there are N words in our dictionary, our
algorithm would find it in N guesses. Why not N/2?
Because <em>we might guess words twice</em>! </p>

<p>// He pulled out a massive dictionary from his backpack!</p>

<p>2: Search linearly from top to bottom and ask user. </p>

<pre><code>repeatedly:
    j = 0; 
    Ask if word j is the wordj++;
</code></pre>

<p>// He keeps selecting </p>

<p>This one will take N/2 steps because words
will in the first half or last half of the dictionary.
The distribution based on loation would center
around the center. This one is twice as fast
as the original. </p>

<p>3: Binary search. </p>

<pre><code>SearchArea = the entire dictionary

while (guess not correct)
{
    Pick middle word w in SearchArea
    Ask user
    If done, yay!

    Else, ask "Word comes before or after w?"
    {
        If before
            SearchArea = first half of current SearchArea
        If after
            SearchArea = second half of current SearchArea
    }
}
</code></pre>

<p>How long would this take? log2(N). </p>

<p>In the worst case, we keep dividing search area in half until
it contains just a single word. </p>

<p>Ex: N = 16 words, how many times to halve till we have
just one word left? 4 steps, which is log2(16), as it's
2^(?) = N.</p>

<p>Even for N = 131,072 in 17 steps, log2(N), the number of times
we can divide N by 2 until we reach a value of 1. </p>

<p>N = 1 billion? log2(1 billion) = 30. </p>

<p>Compare them! N vs N/2 vs log2(N) steps! This is the power
of a good algorithm vs. a bad one. Example, if we had to
search through a names database. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-data-structures" class="anchor" href="#data-structures" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Data Structures</h3>

<p>// Carey offered a bumped-up grade if we win one
// of the top three spots in the Code Day hackathon. </p>

<p>A data structure is the data that'ss operated on an
algorithm to solve a problem. </p>

<p>Sometimes, an algorithm can operated just on the 
data passed into it. The data structure alone is
the input data. </p>

<p>Other times, we'll need to create a <em>secondary data structure</em>
for solving a problem. </p>

<p>Example: a data structure that lists friendships. </p>

<p>If we went relationship-by-relationship in an 
unordered list, it would take forever. </p>

<p>A different way to do it, we assign user a number,
and alphabetize by user name. Again, we could use
binary search, which would take log2(N) steps. </p>

<p>Once we have both users' numbers,
we use them and a 2D array to represent
relationships. </p>

<pre><code>0   1   2

1   *

2       *
</code></pre>

<p>We would typically want it to be symmetric,
meaning both people are reflected as being
friends with each other.</p>

<p>To check if a friendship exists, we
get look up numbers, check (A,B) or (B,A),
and see if they're buddies. </p>

<p>HOWEVER, the number of friendships are less
than the number of people. Building a 1 bill by 1 bill
array would be ludicrous. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-data-structure--algorithms" class="anchor" href="#data-structure--algorithms" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Data Structure + Algorithms</h3>

<p>Data structures and algoirthms can become very complex,
and leave another programmer completely confused. </p>

<p>So every time we create some, we also create
simple functions that hide the messy details,
e.g. <code>bool areFriends(string user1, string user2)</code>,
<code>becomeFriends()</code> and <code>breakUp()</code></p>

<p>This is known as an <strong>inteface</strong>. 
They allow us to use and interact with very complex
things simply and easily. </p>

<p>The algorithms and data structures are designed together, 
then create an interface.</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-abstract-data-type" class="anchor" href="#abstract-data-type" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Abstract Data Type</h3>

<p>Data structures + algorithms + interface = ADT,
an abstract data type, used for a particular problem. </p>

<p>In an ADT, the data structures and algorithms are
private/secret (the details are irrelevant to the user
or other programs).</p>

<p>To solve big problems, we build sub-solutions and 
put them together. </p>

<p>In C++, classes can be an ADT. </p>

<pre><code>class Name
{
    public: 
        // Interface

    private: 
        // Secret algorithms

        // Secret data structures
}
</code></pre>

<p>We build our program from collections of classes. </p>

<p>If every part of our programs knew how every
other part worked, then they would be
very complex to write, never mind change. </p>

<p>We can change implementation while leaving 
interface alone. </p>

<p>Example: switching out engines in a car, but we
can still drive the same way. </p>

<p>Example: the uniform interface of power outlets;
the source of the power doesn't affect us </p>

<p>// Hehe, he's got an air cannon for waking up sleepers</p>

<p>This is the power of <strong>abstraction</strong>, closely
related to <strong>encapsulation</strong>. </p>

<p>We literally break down big problems into little ones. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-object-oriented-programming" class="anchor" href="#object-oriented-programming" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Object-Oriented Programming</h3>

<p>OOP is based on the ADT concept. </p>

<p>In OOP, programs are constructed
from multiple, self-contained classes, 
interacting only through public interfaces. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-c-review" class="anchor" href="#c-review" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>C++ Review</h3>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-c-structs" class="anchor" href="#c-structs" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>C++ structs</h4>

<p>We use structs when we want to group 
related pieces of data into a single variable. </p>

<p>This is defining a whole new type of variable/data. </p>

<p>Syntax: </p>

<pre><code>struct Name
{
    // Members/fields
};
</code></pre>

<p>Don't forget the semicolon! </p>

<p><code>struct</code> declarations are normally found
in header files (.h); in source files (.cpp),
we can <code>#include "header.h"</code> to use the 
definition we just wrote. </p>

<p>Keep in mind that all scalar variables
have a random, garbage value when uninitialized.</p>

<p>// Haha, the PPT makes fun of David Smallberg,
// in this case declaring that Carey's GPA &gt; David's</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-c-classes" class="anchor" href="#c-classes" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>C++ Classes</h4>

<p>Classes and structures in C++ are almost
identical (difference was the reversal of private/public
by default), but <em>by convention</em>, classes 
have member functions in addition to member variables. </p>

<p>Lingo: <code>ClassName r, s</code></p>

<p>r and s are "objects" or "instances" or "variables". </p>

<p>Even the primitive data types can have
"instances".</p>

<p>In a class, the data are "member variables"
and the functions are called "member functions"
or "methods". </p>

<p>Variables defined inside a function are known
as "local variables" and used for temporary needs.</p>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-lecture-2-8-january-2014" class="anchor" href="#lecture-2-8-january-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lecture 2: 8 January 2014</h2>

<p>// We have MLK and President's day off, which is
// why we have another lecture</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-c-classes-review" class="anchor" href="#c-classes-review" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>C++ Classes Review</h3>

<p>By convention, data members are <strong>always</strong>
private. </p>

<p>Also, by default, in <em>classes</em>, everything is
<code>private</code>. But in <em>structs</em>, everything is <code>public</code>
by default. Otherwise, structs and classes are identical
in C++. </p>

<p>Public members are accessible by anything anywhere else
in the program, but private members are accessible only
by members of the class. </p>

<p>"Just remember like how you only touch your own privates."</p>

<p>Heh, then we'll discuss the use of <code>friend</code>. </p>

<p><strong>Encapsulation</strong> means we'll hide the details
and present only a clean, public interface. </p>

<p>Every class is a self-contained problem-solving
unit, an ADT. The rest of the program does not
(and should not) know how it works. </p>

<p>In software dev, we'll start with simple versions
of classes, and improve them with time, even as
the rest of the program doesn't have to change. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-constructors" class="anchor" href="#constructors" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Constructors</h4>

<p>Constructors automatically initialize new variables.</p>

<p>Syntax for declaration: </p>

<pre><code>ClassName
{
    // Constructor
    ClassName(parameters);
    ...
};
</code></pre>

<p>So now when we construct new objects, those arguments
must be passed to the constructor (unless we have a default
public constructor that doesn't take any parameters). </p>

<p>Syntax for definition: </p>

<pre><code>ClassName::ClassName(parameters)
{
    m_member = parameter1;
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>There is <em>no return type</em> for a constructor.</p>

<p>The return statement can be used in a constructor,
but it just terminates the function. It cannot
return a value; it's used to end the constructor early. </p>

<p>Example: </p>

<pre><code>ClassName::ClassName(parameters)
{
    ...
    if (condition)
        return; // End early

    // Don't execute the remaining code below
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>Also, like other member functions, constructors can
be defined inside or outside of the class. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-default-parameters" class="anchor" href="#default-parameters" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Default Parameters</h4>

<p>In the parameter listing, default parameters
can be given:</p>

<pre><code>CSNerd(int PCs, bool usesMac = true)
{
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>This allows: </p>

<pre><code>CSNerd Ned(5); 

// One argument passed; other one uses default
</code></pre>

<p>But: </p>

<pre><code>CSNerd Alan;
</code></pre>

<p>Will still give an error. </p>

<p>Also, <em>default parameters must be right-justified</em>.
No left-side or middle parameters may be default.
All can be default. If something is default,
everything to the right of it must also be default
(or that parameter needs to be moved to the right).</p>

<p>What if we have two defaults and we pass one argument?
It'll go to the left-most parameter, even if both
parameters accept the same data type. </p>

<p><strong>We pass arguments in the order of the parameters!</strong></p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-overloading-constructors" class="anchor" href="#overloading-constructors" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Overloading Constructors</h4>

<p>We can have multiple constructors, as long as
they <em>differ in the number of or type</em> of parameters.</p>

<p>Otherwise, we have <em>ambiguity</em>. </p>

<p>Question: Will the error happen at compile-time or when
we actually try to construct an object with ambiguous arguments?</p>

<p>If no constructors are no defined, a default constructor
is implicitly defined that does not nothing at all:</p>

<p>"I do nothing at all. I'm like a USC student!!!!!!"</p>

<p>Keep in mind, this means that member variables are left
uninitialized. </p>

<p>If <em>we want an array of a class type</em>, we <em>must have</em> a
<em>default public constructor</em> that takes no arguments!
Alternatively, we can have a constructor that has
default arguments for everything. C++ just needs some
constructor that doesn't require arguments.</p>

<p>When we define that array, the constructor will run 
to construct objects for every element in that array. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-initializer-lists" class="anchor" href="#initializer-lists" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Initializer Lists</h4>

<p>WE can do intialize members with the constructor,
OR we can do an <strong>initializer list</strong></p>

<pre><code>CSNerd(int PCs, bool usesMac)
    : m_numPCs(PCs), m_macUser(usesMac)
    // No semicolon after the list
{
    // Any other constructor stuff
}
</code></pre>

<p>Syntax: </p>

<pre><code>ClassName(parameters)
    : member(value), member (parameter)
</code></pre>

<p>The initializer list is with the constructor
body definition; don't leave it in the header
while putting the body in the source file; that'd
be weird. </p>

<p>If we really wanted to, we can mix the two
initialization techniques together. </p>

<p>Also, the curly braces are still needed, but
they don't need to contain anything. </p>

<pre><code>class CSProf
{
    public: 
        CSProf(const string&amp; name, int age = 39)
            : m_name(name), m_age(age)
        {
            // EMPTY
        }

    private:
        string m_name; 
        int m_age;
}; 
</code></pre>

<p>OR: </p>

<pre><code>CSProf(string name, int age = 39)
{
    m_name = name; 
    m_age = age;
}
</code></pre>

<p>OR: </p>

<pre><code>CSProf(string name, int age = 39)
    : m_name(name)
{
    m_age = age; 
}
</code></pre>

<p>OR: </p>

<pre><code>CSProf(string name, int age = 39)
    : m_age(age)
{
    m_name = name; 
}
</code></pre>

<p>Also, these should <code>const string&amp;</code>,
for efficiency and to maintain error-checking
since we don't need to change the passed string. </p>

<p>Also, all of these can be done outside of the class 
too: </p>

<p><strong>Default parameters go in the declaration/prototype</strong></p>

<pre><code>class CSProf
{
    CSProf(const string&amp; name, const int age = 39);
    ...
};

CSProf::CSProf(const string&amp; name, const int age)
{
    m_name = name; 
    m_age  = age;
}
</code></pre>

<p>The PBCR isn't needed for primitives unless
we want to avoid changing variables being passed
into the constructor. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-when-constuctors-are-called" class="anchor" href="#when-constuctors-are-called" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>When Constuctors are Called</h4>

<p>We do not call a constructor when
we only create a pointer to an object. </p>

<p>When we do call the constructor:</p>

<ul>
<li>new command</li>
<li>New object</li>
<li>Array </li>
</ul>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-destructors" class="anchor" href="#destructors" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Destructors</h4>

<p>There may be only <em>one</em> destructor in a class. </p>

<p>It de-initializes a class variable once it goes
away, i.e. passes out of scope. This will happen
automatically. </p>

<p>Syntax: </p>

<pre><code>// In-line, in-class definition
~ClassName()
{
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>OR:     </p>

<pre><code>ClassName::~ClassName()
{
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>NO return-types or parameters allowed. </p>

<p>// Haha, he embedded the Simpsons theme
// song in his PowerPoint</p>

<p>When we don't have a destructor, we
never free up needed resources. </p>

<p><em>Destructors can call member functions.</em></p>

<p>When are destructors needed? </p>

<ul>
<li><p>System resources are allocated (e.g. with <code>new</code>)</p></li>
<li><p>We open a disk file (we need to close it)</p></li>
<li><p>We open a network connection (we need to disconnect)</p></li>
</ul>

<p><em>There is no way to way to manually call the destructor.</em></p>

<p>When are destructors called? </p>

<ul>
<li><p>When local variables pass out of scope (a code block ends)</p></li>
<li><p><code>delete</code> is called on dynamically-allocated variables
(don't forget the <code>delete</code>!)</p></li>
</ul>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-class-composition" class="anchor" href="#class-composition" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Class Composition</h3>

<p>This is when a class contains one more other
classes as member variables. </p>

<p>Example: class Car contains a variable
of type GasTank. </p>

<p>This can embed deeply. </p>

<p><em>A class may not have a member of its own type,</em>
<em>but it may have a pointer to a one of its type.</em></p>

<p>The outer class may call the public functions
of its inner class member, and use them anywhere
in the outer class, including in its constructor
and destructor. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-initializer-list" class="anchor" href="#initializer-list" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Initializer List</h3>

<p>Before, we've seen constructors be used to intialize
a newly-created instance of a class.</p>

<p>They worked like this: </p>

<pre><code>ClassName::ClassName(_type1 arg1, _type2 arg2)
{
    _type1 m_var1 = arg1;
    _type2 m_var2 = arg2;

    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>Another way to do this is with an initializer list:</p>

<pre><code>ClassName::ClassName(_type1 arg1, _type2 arg2)
    : m_var1(arg1), m_var2(arg2)
{
    // A body is required, even if empty
}
</code></pre>

<p>These initializer lists will allow us to handle
class composition and inheritance. They also allow
us to have const and reference member variables,
which must be initialized on the same line that 
they are declared. Trying to do assignment via
a regular constructor body is too late.</p>

<p>Example of what won't work:</p>

<pre><code>#include &lt;iostream&gt;
#include &lt;string&gt;
#include &lt;math.h&gt;

using namespace std;

class Test
{
    public: 
        Test(int i)
        {
            m_test1 = 1;
            m_test2 = i;
        }

    private:
        const int m_test1;
        int&amp; m_test2;
};


int main() 
{
    Test t(10);
}
</code></pre>

<p>Clang fires back an error that "the constructor
for Test must explicitly initialize the member
variable m_test2"</p>

<p>For m_test1, it notes that a read-only variable
is not assignable, indicating the const property
is in effect before we attempted to initialize;
it's "initialized" with junk data instead. </p>

<p>This can be fixed with:</p>

<pre><code>#include &lt;iostream&gt;
#include &lt;string&gt;
#include &lt;math.h&gt;

using namespace std;

class Test
{
public:
    Test(int i) : m_test1(1), m_test2(i)
    {
    }

private:
    const int m_test1;
    int&amp; m_test2;
};


int main()
{
    Test t(10);
}
</code></pre>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-construction-order" class="anchor" href="#construction-order" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Construction Order</h4>

<p>But what happens with the constructors
for the classes? </p>

<p>Example, we construct a Nerd object, 
which contains a Stomach object. Which constrcuctor
runs first? </p>

<p>Hint: If the outer class' constructor uses
the inner class-type object, the object of the inner class
type must be constructed first. </p>

<p><strong>Construction order: inner classes --&gt; outer outer</strong></p>

<p>Another example: Valve --&gt; Heart --&gt; TinMan</p>

<p>Also, note that any members that of a class-type
(e.g. strings) will also be constructed first. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-destruction-order" class="anchor" href="#destruction-order" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Destruction Order</h4>

<p>A Nerd destructor may use the Stomach object,
so we can't afford to destruct it when it's still
needed. </p>

<p><strong>So destruction order goes outer --&gt; inner</strong></p>

<p>Note that destructors will automatically run
as long as the variables are automatic ones. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-advanced-class-composition" class="anchor" href="#advanced-class-composition" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Advanced Class Composition</h3>

<p>What happens if we have a member for an outer
class, and this member is of a class type 
that requires parameters for its constructor? </p>

<p>Then, to make this work, we have to pass
parameter to the inner class constructor. </p>

<p>Even if Nerd's constructor passes
values to its Stomach member variable, 
that won't be done in time. It has to
be done with an initializer list. This list
can take arguments given to the outer constructor
and pass it to inner constructor, e.g.</p>

<pre><code>CSNerd(int farts)
    : belly(farts)
</code></pre>

<p>I don't think C++11's support for default values
in the class declaration will work either. 
We'd have to do an explicit constructor call? </p>

<p>What if we have default arguments? Then the
full initializer list not required, but we may still have it. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-include-directives" class="anchor" href="#include-directives" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>#include Directives</h3>

<p>For headers that are not pre-included as part
of the Standard Library, like ones that we create,
we need to:</p>

<pre><code>#include "HeaderName.h"
</code></pre>

<p>Compare this to the syntax for pre-included headers:</p>

<pre><code>#include &lt;header&gt;
</code></pre>

<p>Note the "" and the .h (which is omitted for library
headers, except for a few deprecated ones like string.h).</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-include-guards" class="anchor" href="#include-guards" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>#include Guards</h4>

<p>There's actually a problem with the above
Watch class example: what if we tried to #include
Watch.h multiple times? The answer is an error.
C++ does not allow us to redefine something; one of its
rules is the <strong>One Definition Rule</strong>. </p>

<p>Remember that we can avoid this with things declared
in different scopes or namespaces (which we'll see shortly).</p>

<p>Avoiding this manually would be tricky. Header files can
call upon another; this layering can be very deep indeed.</p>

<p>The solution? Guard our headers.</p>

<p>If we do something like this:</p>

<pre><code>#ifndef HEADER_H
#define HEADER_H

// Class declaration

#endif
</code></pre>

<p>Then the compiler will check if HEADER_H has
been defined on a list of "code already seen". 
If it is, it will skip to the next #endif, smartly
placed <em>after</em> the end of the class definition. </p>

<p>Otherwise, we #define ("Yes, compiler, this class
has now been seen and declared.") and declare
the class. The #endif would be ignored in that case. </p>

<p>The #define can "define" any non-keyword, though
convention is to use the header name in all caps,
with an underscore in place of the dot.</p>

<pre><code>#ifdef // also exists and does the obvious
</code></pre>

<p>These macros, as they're known, can be used outside
of header files as well. </p>

<p>Additional ones:</p>

<pre><code>#if         // IF
#elif       // ELSE IF
#else       // ELSE
#line       // Used as #line &lt;number&gt; "New file name"

// Any error that follows this #line directive
// will have a line number that uses the specified number
// as a starting point and the containing
// file name as specified by the directive

#error      // Followed by an error message, no quotes
            // Hint: use it in combo with control flow

#pragma     // Specifies compiler options; compiler-specific
            // Ignored if the specific #pragma statement is
            // not supported by that compiler, though stricter
            // warning flags will note the ignoring
</code></pre>

<p>There are also predefined macros like:</p>

<pre><code>__LINE__
__FILE__
__DATE__
__TIME__
__cplusplus // Gives an integer indicating the C++ standard
            // that the compiler conforms to; 201103 indicates
            // C++11

__STD__HOSTED__ // 1 if the local C++ implementation includes all
                // standard header files; 0 otherwise
</code></pre>

<p>All of these statements with a # preceding them
are directives for the <strong>preprocessor</strong> which will
follow these directives before compilation. Note
that they are not followed by a semicolon and that they can
only reside one line; they are considered ended by the first
newline character following them.</p>

<p>To have directives stretch across more than one line:</p>

<pre><code>#directive\
second line of directive
</code></pre>

<p>For details: <a href="http://www.cplusplus.com/doc/tutorial/preprocessor/">http://www.cplusplus.com/doc/tutorial/preprocessor/</a></p>

<p>Returning to our watch example:</p>

<p>// Watch.h</p>

<pre><code>#ifndef WATCH_H
#define WATCH_H


typedef unsigned short ushort;

class Watch
{
    public: 

        // Self-explanatory functions

        void setHour(ushort hour);
        void setMinute(ushort minute);
        void replaceBattery();

    private:
        // This implementation has no need for
        // negative numbers

        ushort m_hr; 
        ushort m_min;
        ushort m_perc_batt;

}; // Don't forget the semicolon!

#endif
</code></pre>

<p>Note that #include guards are not needed for implementation
files; they implement, not declare (though they might
<code>#include</code> other headers--which should already be guarded!).</p>

<p>Even with #include guards, headers should not be included
unless necessary.</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-include-etiquette" class="anchor" href="#include-etiquette" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Include Etiquette</h4>

<p><strong>Never</strong> include a source file (.cpp) in another
source or header file (.h). All sorts of stupid linker
errors will result otherwise. </p>

<p><strong>Never</strong> put <code>using namespace</code> in a header file.</p>

<p>This is known as <em>namespace pollution</em>: any file that includes
that header is forced to use that namespace, which can
lead to all sorts of errors. Instead, any such statements
should be in the implementing source file instead. </p>

<p><strong>Always</strong> include header files whenever they are directly
needed. <strong>Never</strong> assume that one header file will include
another one. Better safe than sorry, especially since
include guards should be implemented for all headers. </p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-when-to-include" class="anchor" href="#when-to-include" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>When to #include</h5>

<p>We don't actually always need to <code>#include</code>.</p>

<p>When we <em>do</em> need to #include:</p>

<ul>
<li><p>We create an object of that class type</p></li>
<li>
<p>We use such an object in any way:</p>

<ul>
<li>We call its methods</li>
<li>We return the object itself from a function</li>
<li>etc.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>

<p>When we do <em>not</em> need to #include:</p>

<ul>
<li>
<p>When we have a class-type object as a</p>

<ul>
<li>Parameter for a function</li>
<li>Return-type for a function</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><p>When we point or refer to a class-type object</p></li>
</ul>

<p>We can still #include, but we do not need to
(and indeed, should not, so as to avoid infinite
including-type problems, which we'll see in a moment).</p>

<p>Instead, simply declare the class' existence
(before it's needed, a <strong>forward-declaration</strong>):</p>

<pre><code>class UsedClass;
</code></pre>

<p>This also speeds up the compilation process,
as header files can be quite large. </p>

<p>NB: If we only declare the class' existence
but do not include it, and then try to do something
that requires the full class definition,
then we will get syntax errors.</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-self-referential-headers" class="anchor" href="#self-referential-headers" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Self-Referential Headers</h4>

<p>If two classes both refer to each other,
and both try to include each other's headers,
we'll get a compile error. </p>

<p>Why? Because the two files will keep including 
each other!</p>

<p>Example: A.h includes B.h and B.h includes A.h</p>

<p>Then:</p>

<p>A includes B which includes A which includes B
which includes A...</p>

<p>Avoid this via the fact that at least ONE
of the two should not need to include the full
class!</p>

<p>NB: Class declarations cannot have full inline
definitions for any functions that use another class
in a way that requires their full definition.</p>

<p>Those function definitions will have to go
in the implementing source file instead. </p>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-lecture-3-10-january-2014" class="anchor" href="#lecture-3-10-january-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lecture 3: 10 January 2014</h2>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-pointers-review" class="anchor" href="#pointers-review" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Pointers Review</h3>

<p>Note that an array name by itself 
acts like a pointer (printing it out
would give the memory address of the first
element) but it is NOT a pointer itself,
which is a variable (with memory allocated
for itself) that holds the
memory address of another variable.</p>

<p>A pointer to an array can be used just
as if it were the array itself. </p>

<p>Don't forget that pointers + some number
means to move forward in memory by the size
of the data type that the pointer is meant to point to. </p>

<p>We do not move forward by bytes but instead
by doubles or ints, etc. Eg, move forward
two ints and it might go from 9240 to </p>

<p>Set-up:</p>

<pre><code>int nums[3] = {...};

int *ptr = nums;
</code></pre>

<p>Equivalent:</p>

<pre><code>ptr[2];
*(ptr + 2);
</code></pre>

<p>The pointer arithmetic:</p>

<pre><code>ptr[j] --&gt; *(ptr + j);
</code></pre>

<p>This is adding a pointer and
an index, but we cannot add
pointers together. </p>

<p>Pointer subtraction is defined,
with the result being how many integers
are between them (if both point
into the same array), NOT the number
of bytes between them. </p>

<p>Also, commutativity:</p>

<pre><code>j[ptr] --&gt; *(j + ptr);
</code></pre>

<p>What happens if we </p>

<pre><code>cout &lt;&lt; nums + 1;
</code></pre>

<p>nums is an address, so + 1 would
print out the address 1 int away. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-passing-arrays-to-functions" class="anchor" href="#passing-arrays-to-functions" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Passing Arrays to Functions</h3>

<p>When we pass arrays, we just pass
the address of the start of the array, 
not the whole entire array itself.</p>

<p>That's why we can have these as equivalent: </p>

<pre><code>void function(int array[]);
void function(int* array);
</code></pre>

<p>This means we can do something like: </p>

<pre><code>function(&amp;array[j]);
</code></pre>

<p>This means that anything meant to act
on an array can be made to act on 
a later subset of the array instead. </p>

<p>Note, however, that the function 
will not know the array's size
unless we pass in another argument
meant to represent that. </p>

<p>-1 is also usable as a subscript value.
It'll move backward that many types in memory.</p>

<p>Note that the passed array must have 
base type matching the expected parameter. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-copy-constructors" class="anchor" href="#copy-constructors" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Copy Constructors</h3>

<p>We have a problem with the way we're currently
designing classes. </p>

<p>Whenever they dynamically allocate memory,
open files, open Internet connections, etc.,
a certain amount of those resources are allocated
to the object of that class type.</p>

<p>The problem is if we have a function that takes
one of those objects by value, or if we make 
a new object based on an old one. </p>

<p>By default, C++ implicitly defines a
default copy constructor (just like how it 
defines default constructors and destructors)
that does a <em>shallow copy</em>.</p>

<p>The new object's members are copies of the old
one's. However, this extends to pointers pointing
to anything dynamically allocated, meaning the new
object will think the old's dynamic members are its own.</p>

<p>So we need a copy constructor that does what
we intend: make a copy, but one that has its own stuff. </p>

<p>Syntax: </p>

<pre><code>// This is still a constructor, so no return-type

// const as we don't need to change the old one
// PBR for efficiency, as objects can have many
// members

//  UPDATE (14 September 2015): 
// According to http://www.cplusplus.com/articles/y8hv0pDG/
// the copy constructor must have one of these signatures:
//      MyClass( const MyClass&amp; other );
//      MyClass( MyClass&amp; other );
//      MyClass( volatile const MyClass&amp; other );
//      MyClass( volatile MyClass&amp; other );
//
// Otherwise, if we have this: MyClass(MyClass other), 
// we're calling the copy constructor as part of the copy constructor!
// Infinite loop! Note that while the pointer versions of these
// could work, they are not considered valid CC's by the standard

ClassName(const ClassName&amp; old)
{
    // 1. Allocate new resources for the new object
    // 2. Copy over the appropriate values from old to new
}
</code></pre>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-lecture-4-13-january-2014" class="anchor" href="#lecture-4-13-january-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lecture 4: 13 January 2014</h2>

<p>Carey is out sick! Every previous year,
he has somehow pulled through and made it to 
class. </p>

<p>Instead, Smallberg will be teaching today
--for 6 straight hours, haha. He's picking up
where we left off last lecture. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-pointers-review-1" class="anchor" href="#pointers-review-1" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Pointers Review</h3>

<p>Pointers pointing to structs or class-type objects
work too. Use <code>*</code> to get to the object via the pointer, 
the <code>.</code> operator to access its fields/members. </p>

<p>Or do it all in one go with <code>-&gt;</code></p>

<pre><code>ptr = &amp;Nerd;

(*ptr).numZits;

ptr-&gt;numZits;
</code></pre>

<p>Smallberg commenting on Carey's use of 
<code>void</code> for functions that don't take any parameters,
a holdover from C that is avoided in C++, haha. </p>

<p>If we have a function that takes a pointer
to something, do the call with an address-of:</p>

<pre><code>functionCall(&amp;argument);
</code></pre>

<p>This can be avoided by defining the function
as taking the parameter by reference. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-dynamic-variables" class="anchor" href="#dynamic-variables" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Dynamic Variables</h4>

<p>Traditional, <code>local variables</code> are like
rooms in a house. But then we run out of space
with so many visiting relatives, and we call up
a hotel, which reserves some rooms, much like
<code>dynamic memory</code>. The overflow relatives
will be placed there instead. </p>

<p>Don't forget to free up the allocated space. 
Tell the hotel that your relatives went home! </p>

<p>What happens if that relative forgets
something in the room? Will that object
still be there? Maybe, maybe not. Someone
else may have taken the room. </p>

<p>Dynamic memory that is released, and we
follow the pointer to it may give the original 
value, or something totally different altogether.</p>

<p>Don't dereference dangling pointers!</p>

<p>With the <code>new</code> command, we can have dynamic arrays:</p>

<pre><code>cin &gt;&gt; size; 

int *array = new int[size]; 
</code></pre>

<p>Make sure the pointer type and array base type
match!</p>

<pre><code>array[0] = num1; 
array[1] = num2; 

*(array + #) = ...;

...

delete [] array;
</code></pre>

<p>Use <code>delete []</code> for anything allocated as an array. 
Use <code>delete</code> for anything allocated as a single item.</p>

<p><strong>Remember: do not <code>delete</code> automatic variables</strong></p>

<p><em>Do not <code>delete</code> anything twice!</em> </p>

<p>(Unless it's a nullptr; deleting a nullptr is harmless). </p>

<p>There's no need to <code>delete</code> anything that was not
dynamically allocated.  </p>

<p>Delete means that we're done with memory at some
location; the amount of memory used (starting at
that location) is handled by the OS's memory allocator. </p>

<p>Just remember that the pointer is left alone (its
memory address still points to storage is now deallocated). 
Change it to null and have functions check for null
(before using their pointer parameters) as a safety measure. </p>

<p>Remember that declaring a pointer to an object
does not create that object unless we actually
do the <code>new</code> command!</p>

<p>Don't mix-up the two versions of delete. 
Just check how the item to be deleted
was allocated (it may not even need delete,
as with automatic variables). </p>

<p>If we use the wrong delete, it is <em>undefined behavior</em>,
and able to cause a crash.  </p>

<p>What if <code>new</code> calls for too much memory? 
e.g. we want 32 billion bytes of memory (4 billion doubles). </p>

<p>The runtime will throw an exception--and our code can
try to catch it somewhere, but if we don't: the program will crash. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-dynamic-class-members" class="anchor" href="#dynamic-class-members" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Dynamic Class Members</h4>

<p>A handy way to customize objects is to have
their constructors take arguments
that are fed (in some form) to an internal
implementation that dynamically allocates
stuff to that specified size. </p>

<p>This means we'll only use as much memory
as needed (and not have to waste memory 
trying to plan for the maximum amount 
likely needed). </p>

<p>Just note that constructor arguments
are in the scope of the constructor definition 
ONLY. To use those arguments in other member functions,
store them as data members. Otherwise, they'll go out
of scope once the constructor is done running. </p>

<p>Don't forget to add a destructor as well. </p>

<p>Because <code>delete</code> should not be called on something
that is uninitialized (that pointer will lead
to who knows where), be sure to initialize
member pointers to nullptr (e.g. with Pets
that <em>may</em> have a favorite Toy). </p>

<p>There are some technical issues with <code>NULL</code>,
and so C++11 introduced <code>nullptr</code>. As long as 
the compiler supports nullptr, we should use it. </p>

<p>Also, remember the big three of memory management: 
if we need a destructor, then we probably need
a copy constructor and overloaded assignment operator
as well. </p>

<p>Yes, we can dynamically allocate and deallocate
structs and classes. </p>

<p>That is a two-part process.</p>

<p>First, memory is allocated, then second, 
the object's constructor is called to create it. </p>

<p>Deletion would mean the reverse:
the destructor is called to clean up any
internal dynamic member variables, then
the memory is declared as available to the OS. </p>

<p>A destructor can be used to do something right
before a class instance is about to disappear. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-namespaces" class="anchor" href="#namespaces" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Namespaces</h3>

<p>In C++, all code is always in a namespace. </p>

<p>Across different namespaces, the same
names/identifiers might be attached to totally
different things. </p>

<p>Consider how we might a friend named Bob
and a boss named Bob. Among our friends, who 
do not know our boss, it's very unlikely
that they'll think we're talking about our boss
when we say "Bob". </p>

<p>On the other hand, our coworkers would think
in the opposite manner. </p>

<p>The meaning of the name depends on context. </p>

<p>With C++, we strictly control what and how
everything is defined in a particular context,
as well as control what contexts exist and 
when they're used. </p>

<p>Previously, we used <code>using namespace std</code>. 
This meant that all the names defined for
the C++ standard library were being used.
Whenever we said <code>cout</code>, we meant the one
found in , not some other version
that we designed ourselves. </p>

<p>If we did not use an explicit namespace,
then our code resided in the <strong>global namespace</strong>.</p>

<hr>

<p>We can use multiple namespaces,
but we can never use more than one
at a time.</p>

<hr>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-creating-a-namespace" class="anchor" href="#creating-a-namespace" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Creating a Namespace</h4>

<p>To place some code into a new namespace,
place it into a <strong>namespace grouping</strong>.</p>

<pre><code>namespace cool 
{
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>The names in that code now reside in the
namespace <code>cool</code>.</p>

<p>To make these names available to other 
code, use a using-directive (<code>using namespace</code>)
or a using-declaration (<code>using cool::</code>)</p>

<p>Note that we can have any number of namespace
groupings for a namespace, even across multiple
files, and all those names will still be in the
same namespace. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-using-directives" class="anchor" href="#using-directives" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Using-directives</h4>

<p>To use a different namespace:</p>

<p>We can have a <strong>using-directive</strong>:</p>

<pre><code>using namespace &lt;space&gt;
</code></pre>

<p>This makes all of the names in that
namespace available, and, crucially,
it does not allow any other use of
those names.</p>

<p>Its scope is from the directive's 
declaration to the end of a block
of code, or, if it was declared in
the global namespace, till the end
of the file.</p>

<p>It is highly recommended that we use
a list of using-declarations rather than 
using-directives, <em>especially</em> at the global level.</p>

<p>Any using-directives should be limited
in scope to specific blocks.</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-using-declaration" class="anchor" href="#using-declaration" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Using-declaration</h4>

<p>Sometimes, we only want specific names
from a namespace, or we have two namespaces
that use some of the same names. </p>

<p>To cleanly intermix the two (that is,
not use the same names from different
namespaces at once), we can use
a using-declaration:</p>

<pre><code>using &lt;space&gt;::
</code></pre>

<p>Its scope is from the declaration's 
appearance to the end of a block
of code, or, if it appeared in
the global namespace, till the end
of the file.</p>

<p>Now, we make only specific names
available and can switch between
namespaces as needed for overlapping
names.</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-qualifying-namespaces" class="anchor" href="#qualifying-namespaces" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Qualifying Namespaces</h4>

<p>Having a using-directive or using-declaration
isn't strictly necessary.</p>

<p>For example, many of the functions found
in the std namespace will have parameters
like this:</p>

<pre><code>void function(std::type parameterName);
</code></pre>

<p>This means that we're using the name from
the std namespace.</p>

<p>Having a <code>using namespace std</code> or <code>using std::</code>
would just mean we could omit the <code>std::</code>,
but then we could not define that name.</p>

<p>Thus, everywhere we've seen <code>cout</code>, where
it's used as found in , we could have
written <code>std::cout</code>.</p>

<p>We’re also able to use this qualification
technique even when another parameter has the 
same name:</p>

<pre><code>void function(std::name parameter, name parameter);
</code></pre>

<p>The first will use the name found in std, the
second the name found in the global namespace
(or in the namespace given by a preceding
using-declaration or using-directive). </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-copy-construction" class="anchor" href="#copy-construction" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Copy Construction</h3>

<p>What happens when we code something like: </p>

<pre><code>Circle b(...); 

Circle a = b; 
</code></pre>

<p>This means that we created an object <code>b</code>
of class type Circle. </p>

<p>Then, we created another object <code>a</code>, also
of type Circle, which is meant to have the 
values of B. </p>

<p>This is construction that is based off an
existing class-type object.</p>

<p>We will be <em>copying</em> it. </p>

<p>The problem is when we have dynamic members. 
The memory addresses stored in the member
pointers (pointing to those dynamic objects)
will be copied verbatim. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-syntax" class="anchor" href="#syntax" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Syntax</h4>

<pre><code>ClassName(const ClassName&amp; existing)
{
    m_member = existing.member;
    ...

// In general:

    // 1. Allocate memory for the new instance
    // 2. Copy values from existing to new instances
}
</code></pre>

<p>So here's a cool thing. We're accessing 
the member variables of another object. How?
A class has access to all its members! </p>

<p>We can talk about private class members
within that class. A copy constructor is
a member function that can access private
class members. There is no additional rule
that we can only access the object we're currently 
operating on. This is precisely so that we can
do stuff like copy constructors. </p>

<p>This allows to have calls like:</p>

<pre><code>// Similar to any other constructor call
ClassName b(a);
</code></pre>

<p>Equivalent to: </p>

<pre><code>// I actually think assignment operator
// with this. It's a good challenge to 
// identify when = is used, and when
// the copy constructor is called

ClassName b = a; 
</code></pre>

<p>This means "initialize <code>b</code> based on <code>a</code>".</p>

<p>This is the old syntax we've seen as
an (awkward) alternative: <code>int a(16)</code>;</p>

<p>Normally, we use this parantheses syntax
for initializer lists instead (which requires them). </p>

<p>Remember that compilers are checking what was passed to
functions and looks for what's needed among the pool
of defined ones. </p>

<p><strong>A copy constructor is a member function used</strong>
<strong>to create a <em>new</em> variable from an <em>existing</em> one</strong></p>

<p>Note:</p>

<pre><code>ClassName(const ClassName&amp; existing)
</code></pre>

<p>We will almost always PBCR. We don't want
to change something we're simply copy (unless
we have a data member that counts how many times
something has been copied, for example) and we
want to pass objects by reference for memory efficiency
reasons. </p>

<p>The funny thing is if we PBV, which would involve
calling the copy constructor for itself? Recursion!</p>

<p>Note that a default one will be provided which copies
member variables from the old instance to the new instance.
However, this implicit default could be inefficient,
doing a <em>shallow copy</em> that copies everything,
even if it doesn't need to be copied (e.g. an array with
unused elements). </p>

<p>To be more precise, we copy the <em>value</em> of every member
variable from the existing instance to the new instance. </p>

<p>// Haha, Smallberg loves seeing the animated robot's head spin</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-deep-copying" class="anchor" href="#deep-copying" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Deep Copying</h4>

<p>The problem is when we have class type that involves
dynamically allocated stuff. </p>

<p>Then, a shallow copy would copy over the contents
of pointers to objects; meaning two objects shared
the same dynamic variables!</p>

<p>Thus, if a destructor with <code>delete</code> statements is
called on one of them and the other one
tries to use that dynamic variable, we'd be following
a dangling pointer!</p>

<p>There's all sorts of logical errors that could spring up
too: objects that aren't initialized correctly, etc.</p>

<p>To solve this, we need a <em>deep copy</em>, where we copy
the what is being pointed to, rather than the pointers
themselves. This means that we'll be setting up a new 
object with new pointers to newly allocated dynamic
variables, and then giving those variables values
identical to the ones of the old object. </p>

<p>Again, any time a class allocates things in memory
dynamically or accesses system resources (e.g. opening
a file), it must have a copy constructor implemented!</p>

<p>This copy constructor then will have to create a 
a new object that has the same amount of dynamic 
memory allocated and then copies over values
into those dynamic variables. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-the-importance-of-ccs" class="anchor" href="#the-importance-of-ccs" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>The Importance of CC's</h4>

<p><em>Copy constructors are used *anytime</em> we make a new copy
of an existing class instance. We do that when we 
PBV to functions! </p>

<p>If we say "copy" while describing the operation of something,
we're probably calling the copy constructor as part of the
process. We call a function that takes its parameters by value:</p>

<ol>
<li>PBV function is called. </li>
<li>Copy constructor is called. </li>
<li>CC creates temporary class instance that is a copy of the argument</li>
<li>The function acts upon this temporary copy </li>
</ol>

<p>What happens when we have dynamic data members? </p>

<p>We might have a PBV function that takes a class instance, 
which calls the CC. The function then acts upon that copy.</p>

<p>The problem: we pass out of scope, and that copy goes out
of scope. A destructor is called. The dynamic members are deallocated. </p>

<p><em>However</em>, the existing instance used as a basis
for that copy is also modified. Why? Because the shallow
copy had the member pointers pointing to the same locations
in memory; both instances thought a particular section of 
memory belonged to them. This resulted from the dumb
copy of pointer to pointer. Both stored the same memory address. </p>

<p>This alone is a problem. </p>

<p>However, that destructor run means that the existing
instance is left in an <em>invalid state</em> with a dangling
pointer to dyanmic members that it thinks still exist.</p>

<p>Once we have dynamic variables, we must write our
own destructor and copy constructor. This is also true
for if we open system resources (files, network connections, etc.).</p>

<p><strong>Always ask</strong></p>

<ol>
<li>Do I need a destructor?</li>
<li>Do I need a copy constructor? </li>
<li>Do I need overloaded operators (especially for assignment)? </li>
</ol>

<p>"A copy constructor should create an object that looks
like the original object, but is independent of it."</p>

<p>We want clones of a person; we don't want the original person
and their clones sharing internal organs!</p>

<p>Thus far, we have not needed CC's, because it hadn't made
sense (or wasn't necessary) to create a new Zombie, Player,
Arena, etc. based off of an existing one. </p>

<p>Instead, when we did need something like that,
e.g. with strings, the Standard Library took care of
that for us. Dynamic members of library types had
their own copy constructors called! This means
that once we properly design and implement classes,
even if they use dyanmic storage, we can stop worrying 
about it. </p>

<p>Also, if we return a class-type object from a function
that has a non-reference return-type, then the copy constructor
is called for that return statement. We'd be returning something
that's getting destructed otherwise. </p>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-lecure-4-14-january-2014" class="anchor" href="#lecure-4-14-january-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lecure 4: 14 January 2014</h2>

<p>// Smallberg is subbing for Carey again
// The original slated sub also got sick
// I do not envy such long teaching hours</p>

<p>// Carey must be really sick</p>

<p>// A lot of the following notes are from
// Winter Break, when I had lots of time
// to study ahead in-depth. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-operator-overloading" class="anchor" href="#operator-overloading" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Operator Overloading</h3>

<p>Time to make operators work
with our class types!</p>

<p>Note that operators can be overloaded
in a class, outside of it, or as a friend</p>

<p>Today's serial killer was Ed Gain,
inspiration for Hannibal Lector, Norman Bates,
and Leatherface in Texas Chainsaw Massacre.
He was most definitely <em>not</em> a programming language
inventor. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-basics" class="anchor" href="#basics" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Basics</h4>

<p>Operators like +, -, *, /, etc. are really just
functions with a different syntax. </p>

<p>Their arguments (often called <em>operands</em> in this context)
might fall on either side of them instead of being 
passed in enclosed in a pair of parantheses. </p>

<p>Just like with functions, we can overload operators,
which allows us to tweak their behavior to accommodate
the class types that we define. We get control over
what it means to "add" two objects of type Country together,
as an example. </p>

<p>Most (but not all) operators can be overloaded 
(and some that can, should not be). </p>

<p><strong>At least one operand to an overloaded operator must be</strong>
<strong>of a class type!</strong></p>

<hr>

<p>Don't overload <code>,</code> or <code>||</code> or <code>&amp;&amp;</code></p>

<p>The associativity of the first and the short-circuiting
of the latter two will disappear otherwise. </p>

<p>Also, only existing operators can be overloaded;
we cannot create new ones.</p>

<hr>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-syntax-for-overloading-a-binary-operator" class="anchor" href="#syntax-for-overloading-a-binary-operator" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Syntax for Overloading a Binary Operator</h4>

<p>The general syntax is this (for a binary operator): </p>

<pre><code>const ClassName operator +(const ClassName&amp; one, const ClassName&amp; two); 
</code></pre>

<p>The operands may be PBV or PBR and not necessarily <code>const</code>. 
The returned value may be of any type, including <code>void</code>. </p>

<p>The <code>const</code> constraint on the returned type will be explained
in a bit. </p>

<p>Note that this particular method of overloading
means that the operator will <em>not</em> have access to the private
members of the class, and so will rely on its public
interface to accomplish its job.</p>

<p>Again, this is a <em>non-member function operator overload</em>.</p>

<p>It is possible to overload operators as member functions,
which we'll also see.</p>

<p>Overloading operators should allow them to function
in the way we expect, i.e. similar to how the built-in
operators function with primitive types. Thus,
an overloaded <code>==</code> operator should return a <code>bool</code>,
which allows its use in <code>if</code> statements. For example:</p>

<pre><code>if (USA == UK)
{
    cerr &lt;&lt; "The Doctor is interfering with the timeline!\n";
}
</code></pre>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-tip-constructors-returning-objects" class="anchor" href="#tip-constructors-returning-objects" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Tip: Constructors Returning Objects</h5>

<p>Constructors can sometimes be thought of
as returning an object. That is, that they
are replaced in the code by the object they create.</p>

<p>Unless, we assign them to something,
these objects are not tied to any named variable,
and so are called <strong>anonymous objects</strong>. They
are full objects though, and can even be 
used as calling objects (i.e., invoke
their public member functions). </p>

<p>This is very useful for operator overloading,
as we might perform internal calculations
for adding two objects together and then input
those internal results into the constructor
to be returned:</p>

<pre><code>// Calculations

return ClassName(finalResult1, finalResult2); 
</code></pre>

<p>This is far cleaner than:</p>

<pre><code>ClassName temp;

// Calculations

temp = ClassName(finalResult1, finalResult2); 

return temp;
</code></pre>

<p>In fact, this might lead to issues if the class
uses dynamic allocation and we didn't overload
the assignment operator correctly (a copy
constructor would not be used, as <code>temp</code> was
already created and initialized when we called
the assignment operator). </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-returning-by-const-value" class="anchor" href="#returning-by-const-value" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Returning by const Value</h4>

<p>The reason we often to elect
to make the value returned by an operator
<code>const</code> is this: automatic error checking
by preventing inadvertent changes to
a returned object. </p>

<p>Consider this: </p>

<pre><code>m3 = (m1 + m2);
</code></pre>

<p><code>(m1 + m2)</code> is a variable of a class type,
but <code>m3</code> is an object of the same clss type. </p>

<p>It's possible to do something like <code>(m1 + m2).input()</code>,
which invokes a member function that changes
member variables. If the operator <code>+</code> returns
a <code>const</code> value though, this will generate an
error. </p>

<p>The default assignment operator will do
a shallow copy: it copies the values of member
variables from one object to another. </p>

<p>This is also why we're returning by value here:
we're copying the anonoymous object created by
the addition operation into m3.</p>

<p>If we returned by reference, we'd have a 
reference to something that might be passing 
out of scope very soon (e.g. as with the 
local variables of an operator
overload definition). </p>

<p>Think about it this way also:
are we generating a value or modifying
an existing one? </p>

<p>Ex: 1 + 2 = 3.</p>

<p>3 is a new value (which can be stored [copied]
into an existing or new variable) but the assigment
operator (which we'll see later) is used
to modify an existing one. </p>

<p>Ex:</p>

<pre><code>fullAmount = baseAmount + 25; 
</code></pre>

<p>If the <code>+</code> operator returned by constant reference,
<code>baseAmount</code> would be changed, even though we likely
intended "fullAmount is equal to baseAmount + 25",
which does NOT imply changing baseAmount; rather,
we just needs value temporarily for a calculation.</p>

<p>So, for the most part, return by const value. </p>

<p><code>=</code> assignment is an exception, as it explicitly
calls for changing an existing object. </p>

<p>Note that if an operator returns
a primitive type, returning it as a <code>const</code>
is pointless, though legal. This is why
the code examples for overloading <code>==</code> 
omitted the <code>const</code> before the <code>bool</code> return-type.</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-overloading-the-negation-operator----" class="anchor" href="#overloading-the-negation-operator----" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Overloading the Negation Operator ( - )</h3>

<p>When used all by itself, the <code>-</code> is
the negation operator, not the subtraction
operator. </p>

<p>It is a <em>unary</em> operator that takes only
<em>one</em> operand. </p>

<p>Consider a class Account that stores
the amount of money in a bank account.</p>

<p>Then, we can overload the negation operator
to support Account-class objects:</p>

<pre><code>class Account
{
    public:
        ...
        Amount(double money);

        // Short definitions can be inline
        double amount() const
        {
            return m_amt;
        }
        ...

    private:
        ...
        double m_amt;
}


// Declaration of negation operator overload
const Account&amp; operator -(const Account&amp; old);

int main()
{
    ...
}

// Negation operator overload implementation
const Account&amp; operator -(const Account&amp; old)
{
    return Account( -old.amount() )
}
</code></pre>

<p>Since this overload happens outside of the class,
we need to use public member functions to
implement the overload. In this case, we also
use the default behavior of the negation operator
with a primitive type. </p>

<p>This is the power of abstraction: we can build 
and build on top of previous work without 
worrying about how it works--so long as it works, haha. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-overloading-operators-as-member-functions" class="anchor" href="#overloading-operators-as-member-functions" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Overloading Operators as Member Functions</h3>

<p>Operators can be overloaded as class member
functions too; this will grant them access to
all private members--not just those of the
calling object, but of any defined class
object too (e.g. the second operand is also
a class object). </p>

<p>With binary operators, there's a syntactic
quirk: there is only one parameter, as the
calling object serves as the first operand. </p>

<p>+, -, <em>, /, = , etc. are all binary operators,
but the the calling object is the first operand;
the operator literally *is a member function</em>!</p>

<p>Ex: <code>account1 + account2</code></p>

<p>Here, <code>account1</code>, the calling object to the member
function operator <code>+</code> is the first operator,
and <code>account2</code> is the second operand. </p>

<p>Note that if we're not changing the calling
object (which is likely if we're returning a
<code>const</code> value), then the whole member function
should be a <em>const member function</em>. </p>

<p>Ex:</p>

<pre><code>class Account
{
    public:
        ...
        const Account operator +(const Account&amp; other) const;
        ...

    private:
        ...
};

    // The following implementation could be
    // in a separate file [indeed, where it should be]

// Here, the calling Account is "self" in a sense
// The other is called "other"
</code></pre>

<p>Don't get confused by the syntax:</p>

<p>Our return-type is a constant Account-type object.</p>

<p>We're overloading the <code>+</code>operator in the scope of
the Account class only.</p>

<p>We implicity consider the calling object to be the
first operand to this binary operator.</p>

<p>We pass the second operand (which also happens
to be of a class type) by reference for efficiency.</p>

<p>This member function operator overload promises
not to modify its calling object, denoted by the
<code>const</code> at the end of its declaration and the first
line of its definition. </p>

<pre><code>// Addition
const Account Account::operator +(const Account&amp; other) const; 
{
    double total = 0;

        // m_amt of the calling object
    total += m_amt;

        // As a non-member, this would have required
        // public accessor function
    total += other.m_amt; 

        // Create and return an anonymous object
    return Account(total);
}
</code></pre>

<p>Overloading operators as member functions is highly
advocated, as it follows the spirit of OOP--and avoids
the messiness of having a bunch of operator overload
declarations and definitions in the source file for main().</p>

<p>Imagine seeing all those for many different classes!</p>

<p>Here's another operator overload implemented as a member
function: </p>

<pre><code>// Subtraction
const Account Account::operator -(const Account&amp; other)
{
    double total = m_amt - other.m_amt;

    return Account(total);
}
</code></pre>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-overloading-the-function-call-operator---" class="anchor" href="#overloading-the-function-call-operator---" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Overloading the Function Call Operator ( () )</h3>

<p>The FCO, a.k.a, the <strong>function application operator</strong> (FAO),
can also be overloaded (which must be done as member function).</p>

<p>This will allow us to create objects that can act like
functions, known as <strong>function-objects</strong>:</p>

<pre><code>class Multiplier 
{
    public:
        Multiplier(int m): multiplier(m) 
        { /* EMPTY */ }

        // Mulitiplier
        int operator()(int x) 
        { return multiplier * x; }
    private:
        int multiplier;
};

int main()
{
    // Create the multiplier (which has a base value)
    Multiplier base_mult(5);

    // Use the multiplier; prints out "20"
    cout &lt;&lt; base_mult(4) &lt;&lt; endl;
}
</code></pre>

<p>Here, a Multiplier class is created.
Its objects can be passed something to multiply
by. This could, of course, be extended to far
more complex cases. What differentiates this
from a normal function then? Why not create
a function <code>int base_mult(int number)</code>?</p>

<p>Because function-objects can save <strong>state</strong>. </p>

<p>For example, <code>base_mult</code> might multiply
by 1 more each time that it is invoked,
thanks to some internal counter that's implemented
as a private data member. This is far
better and cleaner than using static class
variables or--compiler forbid--global variables. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-overloading-the-assignment-operator---" class="anchor" href="#overloading-the-assignment-operator---" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Overloading the Assignment Operator ( = )</h3>

<p>The assignment operator <code>=</code> is a special
overload that is integral to more complex
classes. </p>

<p>Say we have a object that already exists
(i.e. declared and initialized) and we want to 
set it to the value of another object that also already
exists. </p>

<p>In that case, we won't be using the copy constructor
but the assignment operator (=) instead. </p>

<p>Why is this? The copy constructor is a <em>constructor</em>:
it builds <em>new</em> class instances based on existing ones. </p>

<p>It's actually quite clever, taking an existing one as
an argument and then building a new one that is an
<em>independent clone</em> of the old one.</p>

<p>Still, changing an existing instance based on another
existing one is a useful operation, e.g.:</p>

<pre><code>int g = 0; 
int f; 

f = g;
</code></pre>

<p>Defining our own assignment operator
is a necessity when we have a class
that dynamically allocates anything in memory. </p>

<p>See, like with the copy constructor, C++ defines
a default assignment operator for us if we don't 
have one explicitly defined. It'll do a shallow copy,
which is ineffective or even dangerous for anything
that uses the heap or system resources. </p>

<p>Instead, we'll overload the operator as a 
member function, which will allow us to handle all
the private variables that wouldn't be accessible 
to an operator overload outside of the class. </p>

<p>Overloading outside of the class means we would
have to implement everything with public member
functions only. </p>

<p>Friends are an exception.</p>

<p>An example of where an overloaded <code>=</code> is needed:</p>

<pre><code>Circ foo(1, 2, 3);
Circ bar(4, 5, 6);

bar = foo; 
</code></pre>

<p>Primitive types have this functionality built-in.
The old values are stomped over by the new ones. </p>

<p>Our new, programmer-defined types have to have their
own operations defined. We could have it do anything,
e.g. assign the old instances' values to the new. 
But we could launch a nuclear strike afterward too
if we wanted to. This would totally defy the typical
expectations of other developers though. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-syntax-1" class="anchor" href="#syntax-1" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Syntax</h4>

<p>Here's how to do so:</p>

<pre><code>ClassName&amp; operator=(const ClassName &amp;source)
{
    member1 = source.member1;
    member2 = source.member2;

    return (*this);
}
</code></pre>

<p>Note:</p>

<ol>
<li><p>The keyword <code>operator</code> which indicates that we're
redefining how an operator behaves.</p></li>
<li><p>We return a reference to an object of the class type.</p></li>
<li><p>The passed source object is passed by <em>constant</em> reference.</p></li>
<li><p>We return a reference to the changed destination object.</p></li>
</ol>

<p>The assignment operator can be seen as a sort of 
.setEqualTo() member function for an object, which is why it
accepts only one operand here. </p>

<p>Why? The calling object is treated as the first operand. </p>

<p>We could use the new operator as <code>bar.operator=(foo)</code>,
but this would render the overloading operation pointless. 
Instead, we get the natural syntax we're used to: <code>bar = foo</code></p>

<p>Note: the use of <code>=</code> alone does not trigger the overloaded
operator. It's if it's an existing and an existing, rather
than a construction and an initialization, which would
be done with the copy constructor. </p>

<p>void f(Blah b)
{
    ...
}</p>

<pre><code>Blah b1; // Constructor

Blah b2(b1); // CC

Blah b3 = b1; // CC

b3 = b1; // Overloaded assignment

f(b3); // CC
</code></pre>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-with-dynamic-memory" class="anchor" href="#with-dynamic-memory" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>With Dynamic Memory</h4>

<p>When we have dynamic memory allocation with a class,
we'll need an overloaded assignment operator.</p>

<p>Here's what it'll have to do:</p>

<ol>
<li>Free all dynamic memory currently used by the 
target/destination object/instance. 
(We can't afford to have a memory leak)</li>
</ol>

<p>What if the destination instances uses
MORE memory than what the source instance does!</p>

<p>We can't just copy over values, because then
we'd forget about the memory we never deallocated;
our tracking variables (if their values were copied
over from the source instance) wouldn't include them.</p>

<p>So we'd have a MEMORY LEAK.</p>

<p>Thus, DEALLOCATE existing memory before continuing.</p>

<p>Also, the destination instance's original values are
irrelevant, since we're gonna replace them. </p>

<p>Why was this not a problem with copy construction? 
Because we were creating a brand new object, 
which meant there was no memory previously allocated
for it. There's no memory to leak there, though both 
new and old will end up in invalid states if they
accidentally share the same memory.</p>

<p>In the PowerPoint, it should read that b is
destructed before a. It happens in reverse order
of creation. </p>

<ol>
<li><p>Allocate new memory (we need a new pointer to
a new section of memory, so as to not overlap
with the source instance; this is why we deallocated
our old memory) </p></li>
<li><p>Explicitly copy the contents of the source
instance to the destination/target instance. </p></li>
</ol>

<p>Steps 2 &amp; 3 are basically what we did with
the copy constructor. </p>

<ol>
<li>Return a reference to the target instance.</li>
</ol>

<p>Example continued:</p>

<pre><code>ClassName&amp; operator =(const ClassName &amp;source)
{
    member1 = source.member1;
    member2 = source.member2;

    delete member3;

    member3 = new _type;

    *member3 = source-&gt;member3; 

    return (*this);
}
</code></pre>

<hr>

<p>QUESTION:</p>

<p>Why should an overloaded assignment operator
free any dynamic memory currently used by a target class 
instance before allocating new memory and then copying 
over the contents of the source instance?</p>

<p>ANSWERED: See above after #1</p>

<hr>

<p>Can we explicitly call the destructor and the
copy constructor? Possible, apparently, but very
tricky, and would require a language feature
we don't know yet.</p>

<p>Also, deleting the existing members
then the calling the CC would compile,
but create another object, a temporary one,
which disappears immediately after the assignment
operator overload definition. </p>

<p>We will get to a technique that allows us
to pull this off. This allows us to avoid
the duplication of already existing code.</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-multiple-assignments" class="anchor" href="#multiple-assignments" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Multiple Assignments</h4>

<p>The reason we <code>return (*this)</code> is so that we
can do something like the following: </p>

<pre><code>// class Foo has already been declared
// and implemented elsewhere,
// and stores a single unsigned int

Foo first(3), second(4), third(6);

first = second = third;
</code></pre>

<p>Then <code>first</code> and <code>second</code> will have <code>third</code>'s value of 6. </p>

<p>How does this work? The assignment operator
in C++ has a <em>right-to-left associativity</em>,
meaning we move right to left. </p>

<p><code>second</code> is set to <code>third</code>'s value.</p>

<p>The result of this is a modified <code>second</code>, 
which is returned from the (=) operator function,
and used for setting the value of <code>first</code>.</p>

<p>So the expression looks like this as it progresses:</p>

<pre><code>first = second = third;

first = second; // second has third's value(s)

first;
</code></pre>

<p>The <code>return (*this)</code> line is an example of a member function
of a variable returning the whole variable!</p>

<p>Assignment is actually returning a value, the new
value of the lvalue on the rightmost binary assignment:</p>

<pre><code>x = n = k;

x = (n = k); // Parantheses not needed

x = (n) // n has k's value
</code></pre>

<p>It's relay of values from right to left. </p>

<hr>

<p>N.B.: <code>this</code> is a <em>pointer</em> to the calling object;
we dereference it in order to do anything with it.</p>

<p>We follow the <code>this</code> pointer to the calling
object, and then return a reference to it. </p>

<p>We could return a copy, but this is wasteful. 
Pass on the modified paper rather than a copy of it.</p>

<hr>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-anti-aliasing-of-instances" class="anchor" href="#anti-aliasing-of-instances" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Anti-Aliasing of Instances</h4>

<p>There's one problem with our assignment operator
as-is: what if it's invoked upon an object (that
has dynamically-allocated memory in use) to set
it equal to itself?</p>

<p>E.g. <code>x = x</code></p>

<p>"If you don't want to do anything, just write
an empty statement, semicolon."</p>

<p>But we do assign objects to themselves; this
happens when we have a function that takes
arguments and we pass the same objects to it.</p>

<p>It may happen accidentally when stuff nests 
deeply. </p>

<p>This is known as <strong>aliasing</strong>, when two different
references/pointers lead to the same thing, which can
cause unintended issues. </p>

<p>We'll have a problem. As is, it will deallocate, 
then re-allocate, then copy over uninitialized junk
data over itself. <em>We'll have lost our original data</em>,
which can lead to all sorts of unintended consequences.  </p>

<p>The solution is simple: <em>check for self-assignment</em>:</p>

<p>(Our code is valid for when the operator is called
on two different objects) </p>

<p>"The one thing that distinguishes one object from
another is if they're in the same place in memory." </p>

<p>Analogy: Where are persons A and B? They're in the same
place in space-time? Same person. Different places?
