Different persons. </p>

<pre><code>ClassName&amp; operator=(ClassName &amp;source)
{
    if (this == &amp;source) 
        // Source is passed by reference; 
        // we need to compare addresses 
    {
        return (*this); // No changes made
    } 
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>Why return right away? Assignment's goal
is to make the lvalue a clone of the rvalue.
If they're the same instance, this is already
true. Just return the instance. </p>

<p>The following is NOT a correct check:</p>

<pre><code>ClassName&amp; operator=(ClassName &amp;source)
{
    if (*this == source)
    {
        ...
    }
}
</code></pre>

<p>Why? Because what if the source instance just happens
to have the same value(s) as the target instance, but
<em>is</em> a separate, <em>distinct</em> instance? This
doesn't actually check if source and target are 
the same instance; it's actually
checking if they're equivalent instances. </p>

<p>The first check does, as it's <em>comparing memory addresses</em>,
not values.</p>

<p>This also lets us avoid worrying about overloading
the <code>==</code> operator as well (and we'd have to do manual
checking member by member within the overloading definition
for <code>==</code>, we might never realize our mistake unless we had
a class with pointer members and we compared addresses 
instead of values [perhaps by mistake] and realized that
that was it exactly what we needed). </p>

<p>Instead, here we simply compare memory addresses;
different objects should be not at the same place
in memory, unless things have gotten really screwed up. </p>

<p>Aliasing comes up in many places. Any time
we have <em>a function that takes references or pointers</em>
to <em>objects of the same type</em>, we need to check if they're
pointing to the same instance, which is something
that can come up unintentionally deep in some algorithm.</p>

<p>Example:</p>

<pre><code>void transfer(Account&amp; from, Account&amp; to, double amt)
{
    from.debit(amt):
    to.credit(amt);
}

transfer(arr[i], arr[j], 2000);
</code></pre>

<p>What if i and j are equal? We must account
for any situation where we might be acting upon
the same object. This one would have no net effect:
it loses 2000, then loses 2000. BUT, a change in requirements
later on may change our implementation and if we forget
about this harmless aliasing issue, then it can turn harmful.</p>

<pre><code>{
    ...
    if (amt &gt;= 10000)
        fileForm(...);
</code></pre>

<p>Filing unnecessary forms has consequences. What happens
if we transfer money from an account to itself
then we filed a form for no reason, bringing down
the legal consequences. </p>

<p>So, check for aliasing: </p>

<pre><code>{
    ...
    if (&amp;from == &amp;to)
        return;
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>This is something that should be handled at design time,
after we've designed the functions' interfaces in specs.
We need to determine what we should do in some case,
e.g. with aliasing. </p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-story-time" class="anchor" href="#story-time" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Story Time</h5>

<p>This happened with an AI researcher where his dissertation
was a program that discovered mathematical theorems.
He gave it guiding principles (look for stuff that
occurs rarely or frequently) and and got it to try to prove
theorems, and had it identify interesting things. </p>

<p>The program discovered that numbers have factors,
then examined those and eventually prime numbers. </p>

<p>He got hired by a project commissioned by the Navy,
where they set up a game to discover interesting tactics. </p>

<p>In the simulation, one thing that occured would be
to fire one missile from one ship to another ship.</p>

<p>So the program found a situation where firing a missile
on itself was useful: if the enemy ship fired on itself
(at the same time that we fired on it), it would sink faster!
The function didn't account for the aliasing contingency. </p>

<hr>

<p>QUESTION:</p>

<p>Does this overloaded assignment operator work even
when we have nested classes that all use dynamic memory?</p>

<p>Possible self-answer: </p>

<p>Yes, if it correctly handles the class data member
value transfers correctly.</p>

<hr>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-swapping-objects" class="anchor" href="#swapping-objects" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Swapping Objects</h4>

<p>As we learned before, we can't just 
directly exchange variables (a = b; b = a).
We'll lose one variable's values otherwise.</p>

<p>Instead, we need a temporary clone to allow us
to transfer values. It's like Hanoi Towers! </p>

<p>This is trivial once we have our copy 
constructors and assignment operators. This
is slow though, with all sorts of allocation
and deallocation happening. </p>

<p>What if we just swapped the data members
and pointers (to objects) instead? We can do that! 
(Again, we'll need the 3-step temporary
copy value transfer method).</p>

<p>This swap() function would have to be
a member function of the class in order
to work, but this would be blazing fast!</p>

<p>The temporary variables should be automatic variables.</p>

<p>Things get complicated once we have classes
within classes though...</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-avoiding-code-duplication" class="anchor" href="#avoiding-code-duplication" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Avoiding Code Duplication</h4>

<p>Wait, this is how we can avoid having to do
all that work with code that basically 
duplicates the destructor and copy constructor!</p>

<p>THIS IS WHAT I THOUGHT ABOUT BEFORE BUT COULDN'T
FIGURE OUT HOW TO IMPLEMENT!</p>

<pre><code>ClassName&amp; ClassName::operator=(const ClassName&amp; source)
{
    if (this != &amp;source)
    {
        ClassName temp(source); // We use the CC

            // Remember the goal of assignment
            // Swap variables using a temporary value

            // The below is equivalent to this.swap(temp)
            // Remember that class methods implicitly point
            // the `this` pointere to the calling object instance

        swap(temp); // Where calling object is destination
    }
            // Temp is destructed; its scope was inside that
            // `if` conditional block
            // We avoiding duplicating the destructor

    return *this;
}
</code></pre>

<p>This is so much simpler! What Smallberg just showed
us is the <strong>copy-and-swap</strong> style in modern C++ (late 90s onward).</p>

<p>Just make swap() work well!</p>

<p>Also, we don't have to check for self-assignment, the code
would still work, as we'd be copying to a temporary,
swapping values, and the original would just get its original
values back. This is fucking awesome!</p>

<p>Summary of the two approaches: </p>

<p>Traditional assignment operator implementation: </p>

<ol>
<li><p>Deallocate all dynamically allocated memory currently used
by the calling instance. </p></li>
<li><p>Allocate enough memory to hold the source's contents. </p></li>
<li><p>Copy over the memory contents of the source instance
to our newly allocated memory</p></li>
<li><p>Return a reference to the calling instance</p></li>
</ol>

<p>Copy-and-swap approach: </p>

<ol>
<li><p>Create a temporary copy of the source instance using 
the copy constructor. </p></li>
<li><p>Swap the temporary copy's values with our calling
instance's value. This entails swapping variable values, 
including pointers to objects, etc. </p></li>
<li><p>Return a reference to the calling instance. The temporary 
copy went out of scope and was destructed automatically.
We avoid code duplication and unnecessary overhead. </p></li>
</ol>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-the-big-three" class="anchor" href="#the-big-three" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>The Big Three</h3>

<p>The book notes that </p>

<pre><code>"The copy constructor, the = assignment operator, 
and the destructor are called the big three because 
experts say that if you need any of them, you need all three."
(p. 464)
</code></pre>

<p>Why would we need all three? If we have a destructor,
then we likely have something allocated dynamically,
which means that new copies made from old, existing
variables will have to be properly initialized with
a copy constructor, and existing variables being set
to the value of another existing variable will require
an overloaded assignment operator (=). </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-toss" class="anchor" href="#toss" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Toss</h3>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-supplementary-from-forney-notes-1-17-january-2014" class="anchor" href="#supplementary-from-forney-notes-1-17-january-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Supplementary from Forney Notes #1: 17 January 2014</h2>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-initializer-lists-1" class="anchor" href="#initializer-lists-1" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Initializer Lists</h3>

<p>Say we have class: </p>

<pre><code>class Test
{
    public: 

        Test(int number, string name)
        {
            m_member1 = number;
            m_member2 = name;
        }

        int getMember();
        bool isEmpty();

    private:

        int m_member1;
        string m_member2;
};
</code></pre>

<p>Normally, when we construct a new instance
of the class, say <code>Test t1</code>, then the 
<em>data members</em> of the class are declared <em>before</em>
any code in our defined constructor is executed.</p>

<p>This can be seen as:</p>

<pre><code>int main()
{
    ...
    int m_member1;
    string m_member1;

    m_member1 = number;
    m_member2 = name;
}
</code></pre>

<p>That is, a normal constructor that assigns
passed arguments' values to data members is like
declaring the members and then assigning them.</p>

<p>To get the equivalent of initialization at
instantiation, we need an <em>initializer list</em>.
This will pre-empt any default constructors 
for data members of other class-types as well.</p>

<p>We need this for when other classes don't
have a default constructor and need arguments
passed to them, or we want to customize our 
new object at creation. This also improves
performance, as we're avoiding the extra step that
comes from declaring <em>then</em> assigning. Instead,
we do it all in one step. </p>

<p>Syntax:</p>

<pre><code>ClassName(_parameters)
    : member1(parameter1), member2(parameter2), ...
{
    // CONSTRUCTOR BODY
}
</code></pre>

<p>OR:</p>

<pre><code>// Initialization list style
  Survivor::Survivor ():
      m_name("Rick Grimes"),
      m_infected(false),
      m_health(100),
      m_gun(Weapon("Beretta", 30)) 
      {}
</code></pre>

<p>Note: </p>

<ul>
<li>No semicolon after initializer list</li>
<li>Initializer lists will initialize members
in the order in which they are listed in the
class definition, <em>regardless</em> of their order
in the initialization list</li>
<li>Curly braces must still follow for the constructor
definition, even if it is an empty <code>{}</code>
</li>
<li>
<strong>Initializer lists</strong> are the only way to initialize 
<code>constant</code> data members</li>
</ul>

<p>So the rule of thumb is this: 
use an initializer list whenever possible,
and it is especially preferable for 
non-primitive types. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-c-compilation-process" class="anchor" href="#c-compilation-process" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>C++ Compilation Process</h3>

<p>Compiling a C++ program is actually 
a multi-step process: </p>

<ol>
<li><p>Preprocessing </p></li>
<li><p>Compilation [Optimization thrown in here as well]</p></li>
<li><p>Linking</p></li>
</ol>

<p>And we end up with an executable file.</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-preprocessing" class="anchor" href="#preprocessing" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Preprocessing</h4>

<p>Preprocessing deals with <em>preprocessor directives</em>
like <code>#include</code> and <code>#define</code>. The <code>#</code> indicates
a preprocessor directive. </p>

<p><code>#include</code> replaces the file name with the file
contents (or at least the identifiers for later resolution). </p>

<p><code>#define</code> works before compilation,
and replaces a defined identifier in code with whatever
literal we associated with it. </p>

<pre><code>#define LOUD_STRING "YELLING"
#define COOL_INT 42

int main ()
{
    cout &lt;&lt; LOUD_STRING &lt;&lt; endl;
    cout &lt;&lt; COOL_INT &lt;&lt; endl;
}
</code></pre>

<p>What's the difference between <code>const</code> and <code>#define</code>?
The former obeys scope rules; the latter will be
applied globally, even across files (how else
would include guards work?). Note then that <code>#define</code>
should NOT be used for any sort of variable that
needs to be changed throughout the course of a program. </p>

<p>Note also the preprocessed nature of <code>#define</code> means
that we won't take up memory needed for another variable,
which usually shouldn't be something we need to worry about.</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-compilation" class="anchor" href="#compilation" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Compilation</h4>

<p>Now, compilation occurs (which really encompasses
the use of an assembler as well, where we go from 
C++ source code to assembly code to machine code [binary]).</p>

<p>Each source code file is transformed into an <em>object file</em> 
of machine code. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-linking" class="anchor" href="#linking" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Linking</h4>

<p>The <em>linker</em> follows, checking that for every declaration, 
we have a definition. We should never hit anything in code
and go "Wait, what's that?". All the appropriate dependencies
will then be grouped together and made into an executable file.  </p>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-lectures-5--6-17-january-2014--22-january-2014" class="anchor" href="#lectures-5--6-17-january-2014--22-january-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lectures 5 &amp; 6: 17 January 2014 &amp; 22 January 2014</h2>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-review-the-big-three" class="anchor" href="#review-the-big-three" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Review: The Big Three</h3>

<p>[At Smallberg's lecture, which replaced
a standard Friday discussion]</p>

<p>Up until now, we've dealt with language 
issues, e.g. when default 'structors and
operators need to be replaced with our own
custom definitions. </p>

<p>Review: </p>

<pre><code>class Blah
{
    public: 

        Blah(const Blah&amp; other);
            // No other constructors declared
        ...
};
</code></pre>

<p>Questions: </p>

<ol>
<li>Is there anything interesting about this class?</li>
</ol>

<p>Answer: <em>It's impossible to create a Blah object.</em>
Here, we can only create a Blah as a copy of another
Blah. It's chicken and the egg, but with no resolution. 
It's an infinite regression toward nothing. </p>

<p>Can't we do <code>Blah b2(b1)</code>? NO. </p>

<p>"This class is useless!--not this class, CS 32."</p>

<p>Rules: </p>

<ul>
<li><p>A default constructor is provided <em>only</em> when we 
do not declare <em>any</em> constructors for a class 
(including copy constructors).</p></li>
<li><p>A default copy constructor is provided only when we
do not declare a copy constructor.</p></li>
<li><p>A default destructor is provided only when we do not
declare a destructor (which would call the destructors
of other classes for any data members of those class types).
It does nothing for primitive types, however, e.g. a
pointer to a dynamically allocated Zombie pointer. </p></li>
<li><p>If we do not declare an assignment operator for a class,
the compiler will provide one for us. </p></li>
</ul>

<p>This sounds like Miranda Rights for classes, haha. 
"If you cannot afford a lawyer, one will be provided for you."</p>

<p>Anytime a class of ours contains a low-level resource
handle (e.g. a pointer to something dynamically allocated), 
we're gonna need the Big Three: destructor, copy constructor, 
assignment operator. </p>

<p>On the other hand, if our class is built out of other 
class types that are already encapsulated and self-cleaning,
then we don't need to worry about our class's 'structors
and operators. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-linked-lists" class="anchor" href="#linked-lists" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Linked Lists</h3>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-the-problem" class="anchor" href="#the-problem" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>The Problem</h4>

<p>It's time for our first major data structure!</p>

<p>Let's say we have a collection of objects,
and we wanna do stuff with it. </p>

<p>What are our options for doing so?</p>

<p>Originally, our only option for grouping
together like things was a fixed-sized array.</p>

<p>Smallberg just proposed an array that layed out
its elements throughout its indices. Say we had
a group of names represented as std::string's. </p>

<p>We quickly find that inserting, deleting, sorting,
etc. objects is very annoying.</p>

<p>Someone finally answered "Put them all at one end
of the array.". Someone else also suggested having a
counter for the number of the names. Now, that counter
is also the index of the next available element. 
We don't have to search for an available slot before 
adding an item. Searching is also not bad: we can stop
once we know that we've reached the end of the array.</p>

<p>Here's the annoying one: deleting an item. We could
replace it with the empty string, but that would 
throw off the counter. Deleting is made easy,
but complicates other common collection-related
operations. This also assumes that an empty string
is not important for representing something. It'd be
like excluding 3 from the set of all real numbers
for no real reason.</p>

<p>So instead, we want to make sure that the array
is always containing stuff we care about in the early 
part of it. We can shift everything over, which is
needed if order matters. If order does <em>not</em> matter,
than we can just take the last item and replace the
deleted one with that, then decrement the counter. If
the deleted item was at the end, we can just decrement
the counter.</p>

<p>Note though that finding an item takes time,
and preserving order is annoying. And of course,
the major downside is that we're limited in size. </p>

<p>What if we did know the max size needed?  </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-a-house-analogy" class="anchor" href="#a-house-analogy" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>A House Analogy</h4>

<p>Einstein expects a large shipment of animals
but will rent houses one at a time, keeping
track only of the last-rented house, and in
each house, he'll note on the fridge where the next 
rented house is. The last note will say "end of list". </p>

<p>This is necessary because he doesn't know in advance
how many he'll need.</p>

<p>His algo: </p>

<ol>
<li>Rent a new empty house</li>
<li>Put his animal in the empty house</li>
<li>Note where the next house in the list is</li>
<li>Update his chalk board to know where his
last-rented house is</li>
</ol>

<p>Do it with a struct to represent houses: </p>

<pre><code>struct House
{
    string name; // animal we're holding
    House *next; // address of next house
};
</code></pre>

<p>Outside of the struct, we'll have a special
last-rented pointer to know where the last
house is.</p>

<p>The last house identifies as the last one
in the list via <code>nullptr</code> for its fridge note
to the next house: there is no next house. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-a-train-analogy" class="anchor" href="#a-train-analogy" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>A Train Analogy</h4>

<p>[The following are notes written
before, during, and after Lecture 6]</p>

<p>Picture a train. Its cars are linked together.
Each car knows what the next car is. </p>

<p>If we start with knowledge of what the
first car is, and mark the last car (the caboose)
as such (some sort of marker to let us know
that we've reached the end of the train), then
we can just start at the beginning and go from 
car to car until we hit the caboose.</p>

<p>This knowledge of the first pointer is known
as the head pointer. Each train car can
be a class or struct-type object, containing its
data members as well as a pointer to the next
car. This next pointer is how we know where to go,
well, next!</p>

<p>In code, this will look something like this: </p>

<p>// Items in a linked list are called <strong>nodes</strong></p>

<pre><code>// The container for our nodes and their data
struct Car
{
    std::string m_name;
    Car* m_next; 

    // Note: a class cannot have a data member
    // of its own type; that'd be weirdly recursive
};
</code></pre>

<p>This is not a pointer to itself, but a reference
to another Car. </p>

<p>This is a <strong>self-referential data-type</strong>. </p>

<p>To make things easy, the linked list itself
will be held in a class. Here's the sort of 
stuff we can do with it:</p>

<p>Also, the node struct is easily defined
in another header file elsewhere or done
as a private member of the containing class.</p>

<pre><code>#include &lt;string&gt;

// A container for our linked list
class Train 
{
    public: 

        // Create a new linked list Train
        Train();

        // Dive-bomb the current Train
        ~Train()

        // Add nodes/Cars to the Train
        void addCarFront(const std::string&amp; name);
        void addCarMiddle(const std::string&amp; name);
        void addCarRead(const std::string&amp; name);

            // Each of these addNode() functions
            // will have different implementations
            // in order to work correctly!

        // Delete an individual Car, based
        // on its characteristics or its 
        // position in the Train
        void deleteCar(const std::string&amp; name);
        void deleteCar(const int&amp; slotNumber):

        // Find the position of a node
        int findCar(const std::string&amp; name) const;

        // Process the list
        // This could mean doing stuff
        // with node data, ordering nodes
        // (e.g. alphabetizing), etc.
        void print carsList() const;


    private:

        int m_numCars;
        Car* m_headCar; // The head pointer
};
</code></pre>

<p>To know when we've hit the last <em>node</em> in a
linked list, the last node will have a next
pointer that points to <code>nullptr</code>. This means
that it's not meant to go anywhere next,
and so we know that we've reached the end of
the linked list. </p>

<p>Note that EVERY linked list must have at least
a head pointer (if we reversed head and tail,
then we could really call the "tail" the new head).</p>

<p>We won't know where to start with the linked list otherwise. 
We have to have able to start at the head and follow
next pointers to the next node till we've reached the end.</p>

<p>See, that's the only thing linking everything together:
the pointers. The addNode() methods are just dynamically
allocating new nodes in memory. We don't have an array
of nodes (that'd be dumb; we'd be dealing with the limitations
of arrays then, something we probably wanted to avoid by
using a linked list). </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-initialization" class="anchor" href="#initialization" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Initialization</h4>

<p>Initialization of a linked list 
is a <em>necessity</em>. The last thing we want
to do is accidentally follow a head pointer
to what we believe is the start of a linked
list only to discover (perhaps very belatedly)
that it was a dangling pointer. </p>

<p>This happens with the containing class' constructor. </p>

<pre><code>Train()
{
    m_head = nullptr; 
}
</code></pre>

<p>When the head pointer is set to the nullptr,
we know that the linked list is <em>empty</em>. 
A list is empty at creation, so have the constructor
set the head pointer to <code>nullptr</code>. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-insertion" class="anchor" href="#insertion" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Insertion</h4>

<p>Cool, now let's add some nodes! </p>

<p>Note that we can add nodes to
the top, middle, or bototm. </p>

<p>Adding to the top is easiest and fastest
when compared to the other two. Always
try to choose the simplest one that best
suits our goal. Work smart, not hard. </p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-at-the-top" class="anchor" href="#at-the-top" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>At the Top</h5>

<p>Adding to the top has
two possible cases.</p>

<ol>
<li>The linked list already has nodes in it.</li>
<li>The linked list is empty. </li>
</ol>

<p>Reminder of the node struct:</p>

<pre><code>struct Car
{
    std::string m_name; 
    Car* m_next;
};
</code></pre>

<p>To add a new node: </p>

<pre><code>void addCarFront(const std::string&amp; name)
{
    // Create a new Car
    Car* latest = new Car; 

    // Customize the Car
    latest-&gt;m_name = name; 

    // Link new top node to what
    // was previously the top node,
    // which is given by the head pointer

    latest-&gt;m_next = m_head; 
        // m_head is part of Train

    // Update the head pointer to point
    // to the new top of the list
    m_head = latest; 
}
</code></pre>

<p>This will work even if the list is empty!</p>

<p>Without comments:</p>

<pre><code>void addCarFront(const std::string&amp; name)
{
    Car *latest = new Car; 
    latest-&gt;m_name = name; 

    latest-&gt;m_next = m_head; 
    m_head = latest; 
}
</code></pre>

<p>The general algo: </p>

<ol>
<li>Allocate memory</li>
<li>Initialize the node</li>
<li>Link the node to the former start of the lsit</li>
<li>Re-aim the head pointer at the new node</li>
</ol>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-at-the-end" class="anchor" href="#at-the-end" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>At the End</h5>

<p>If we're adding nodes to the end of a list,
we must again consider if it is empty or
filled (perhaps partially). </p>

<pre><code>void addCarRear(const std::string&amp; name)
{
</code></pre>

<p>In the empty case, things are easy:
we can just consider it to be adding
to the top of an empty linked list!
There is no beginning or end, so make
the Beginning:</p>

<pre><code>    if (m_head == nullptr)
        addCarFront(name);
</code></pre>

<p>If the list already has nodes, 
then we need to: </p>

<ol>
<li>Follow the links to the current last node</li>
<li>Allocate a new node using <code>new</code>
</li>
<li>Add data to the new node</li>
<li>Set the <code>next</code> pointer in the new node to <code>nullptr</code> </li>
<li>Update the former last node to point to the new last node</li>
</ol>

<p>The hard part is following those links, something
called <strong>list traversal</strong>.</p>

<p>We're gonna use a loop to go from node to node:</p>

<pre><code>Car* temp; 
temp = m_head; 
// Point to 1st node, thanks to head pointer; start at top of list

while (temp-&gt;next != nullptr) // Go till we hit the current last node
{
    temp = temp-&gt;next; 
    // This changes temp to point to the next node
    // We CANNOT use temp++ (we're not in an array)
    // This means temp will now point to the next node
};
</code></pre>

<p>The advantage of linked lists means we can jump around in memory,
thanks to pointers. If we had kept a counter of the number
of nodes, we could use a for-loop. This current loop
could be used to count the number of items in the list.</p>

<p>An alternative loop syntax (as shown by Smallberg in video lecture):</p>

<pre><code>for (Car *temp = m_head; temp-&gt;next != nullptr; temp = temp-&gt;next)
{
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>However, this is not useful for this function,
as the temp variable will go out of scope and 
disappear. Instead, the first syntax is better here.
The second one is best for, say, a print() function.</p>

<p>We could resolve this by declaring <code>temp</code> before the for-loop.</p>

<p>Once we hit the last node: </p>

<pre><code>Car *latest = new Car; 

latest-&gt;name = name;
latest-&gt;next = nullptr; // This is the new latest node!

temp-&gt;next = latest; 
// Access the next pointer of the old last node 
// Have it point to our new last node
</code></pre>

<p>Full version of adding a node to the end: </p>

<pre><code>void addCarRear(const std::string&amp; name)
{
    // If list is empty, just treat it as adding to the front
    if (m_head == nullptr)
        addCarFront(name);

    // Otherwise, traverse list to last node
    Car *temp = m_head; // Start at top of linked list

    // Keep going till next pointer shows nullptr;
    // This marks the last node as the end
    while(temp-&gt;next != nullptr)
    {
        temp = temp-&gt;next; // Follow next pointer to next node
    }

    // Found last item if here in the code
    // Create new node
    Car *latest = new Car;

    // Customize latest Car and mark it as being the end
    latest-&gt;name = name;
    latest-&gt;next = nullptr;

    // Link old last node to new last node
    temp-&gt;next = latest;
}
</code></pre>

<p>Without comments:</p>

<pre><code>void addCarRear(const std::string&amp; name)
{
    if (m_head == nullptr)
        addCarFront(name);

    Car *temp = m_head; // Start at top of linked list

    while(temp-&gt;next != nullptr)
    {
        temp = temp-&gt;next; // Follow next pointer to next node
    }

    Car *latest = new Car;

    latest-&gt;name = name;
    latest-&gt;next = nullptr;

    temp-&gt;next = latest;
}
</code></pre>

<p>Story from Kim!</p>

<p>She was apparently a Hollywood actress before deciding on a 
CS career. She's been on several TV shows, haha. </p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-in-the-middle" class="anchor" href="#in-the-middle" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>In the Middle</h5>

<p>Inserting in the middle comes up when we have an ordered link list,
e.g. an alphabetized one and insertions need to happen at the right
spots. </p>

<p>The basic algorithm in pseudocode is this: </p>

<pre><code>void addCarMiddle(std::string&amp; name)
{
    if (list == empty)
    {
        addCarFront(name);
    }
    else if (new node belongs at top of list)
    {
        // This happens if the new item is smaller than
        // earlier in the alphabet, etc.
        addCarFront(name);
    }
    else if (new node belongs somewhere in the list)
    {
        // Traverse list till we find node just ABOVE
        // the proper spot

        // Create the node

        // Link the node into position right AFTER the
        // the node ABOVE its proper position
    }
}
</code></pre>

<p>In actual code: </p>

<pre><code>void addCarMiddle(const std::string&amp; name)
{
    // List is empty: new node belongs at top
    if (m_head == nullptr)
    {
        addCarFront(name);
    }
    else if ( // Item belongs at top of list )
    {
        addCarFront(name);
    }
    // Item belongs somewhere in list
    else
    {
        // Traverse list to find node above insertion point
        // Start with head pointer to top of list

        Car *above = m_head;

        // Keep going; last possible place we can go to
        // is last node; if we go there, the new item
        // belongs at the end; use addCarRear() for that

        while (above-&gt;next != nullptr)
        {
            // Code to see if we've found the node
            // just above where our new node should be

            above = above-&gt;next; // Move down a node
        }

        // Allocate and customize new node

        Car *latest = new Car; 
        latest-&gt;name = name;

        // Link new node to the one below and the one above

        // Link to node after new node (the old next node)
        // This MUST be done first, or we lose track
        // of the rest of the list

        latest-&gt;next = above-&gt;next; 

        // Link to above node

        above-&gt;next = latest;
    }
}
</code></pre>

<p>Without comments:</p>

<pre><code>void addCarMiddle(const std::string&amp; name)
{
    if (m_head == nullptr)
    {
        addCarFront(name);
    }
    else if ( // Item belongs at top of list )
    {
        addCarFront(name);
    }

    else
    {
        Car *above = m_head;
        while (above-&gt;next != nullptr)
        {
            above = above-&gt;next;
        }

        Car *latest = new Car; 
        latest-&gt;name = name;

        latest-&gt;next = above-&gt;next; 
        above-&gt;next = latest;
    }
}
</code></pre>

<p>The same example, but with code for alphabetical comparison
of names (this is assuming the list has been alphabetized
consistently): </p>

<pre><code>void addCarMiddle(const std::string&amp; name)
{
    // List is empty: new node belongs at top
    if (m_head == nullptr)
    {
        addCarFront(name);
    }
    // Item belongs at top of list
    // The head pointer gives us the current top item

    else if (name &lt; m_head-&gt;name)
    {
        addCarFront(name);
    }
    // Item belongs somewhere in list
    else 
    {
        // Traverse list to find node above insertion point
        // Start with head pointer to top of list

        Car *above = m_head;

        // Keep going; last possible place we can go to
        // is last node; if we go there, the new item
        // belongs at the end; use addCarRear() for that
        // The code below also works 

        while (above-&gt;next != nullptr)
        {
            // Code to see if we've found the node
            // just above where our new node should be

            // It's possible to string together next-&gt;'s
            // but this is terribly confusing and possibly
            // dangerous, as we're not checking that
            // the pointer is not nullptr

            if (name &gt;= above-&gt;name &amp;&amp; name &lt;= above-&gt;next-&gt;name)
                break;

            // The first check may not be necessary if the
            // list was maintained correctly; this depends
            // on implementation and application. Think it through.

            // Otherwise, keep traversing
            above = above-&gt;next; // Move down a node
        }

        // Allocate and customize new node
        Car *latest = new Car; 
        latest-&gt;name = name;

        // Link new node to the one below and the one above

        // Link to node after new node (the old next node)
        latest-&gt;next = above-&gt;next; 

        // Link to above node
        above-&gt;next = latest;
    }
}
</code></pre>

<p>The same code, but adding via
slot number (with the first slot being 0):</p>

<pre><code>// New parameter for slot number
void addCarMiddle(const std::string&amp; name, int slotNum)
{
    // List is empty: new node belongs at top
    if (m_head == nullptr)
    {
        addCarFront(name);
    }
    // Item belongs at top of list
    else if (slotNum == 0)
    {
        addCarFront(name);
    }
    // Item belongs somewhere in list
    else 
    {
        // Traverse list to find node above insertion point
        // Start with head pointer to top of list
        Car *above = m_head;

        // Keep going; last possible place we can go to
        // is last node; if we go there, the new item
        // belongs at the end; use addCarRear() for that

        while (above-&gt;next != nullptr)
        {
            // Code to see if we've found the node
            // just above where our new node should be

            // Prefix decrement to have it take effect immediately
            // This gets us to a node right above index slotNum

            // Have we gone forward that many times yet? 
            // We could convert all this to consider #1 to 
            // be the first slot number of the list
            // Then, we'd check if --slotNum == 1

            // Note that a prefix decrement is NEEDED
            // (for this implementation); otherwise, we
            // compare slotNum's original value of 2 against
            // 0, not what we intended

            if (--slotNum == 0) 
                break;

            // Otherwise, keep traversing
            above = above-&gt;next; // Move down a node
        }

        // Allocate and customize new node
        Car *latest = new Car; 
        latest-&gt;name = name;

        // Link new node to the one below and the one above

        // Link to node after new node (the old next node)
        latest-&gt;next = above-&gt;next; 

        // Link above node to latest node
        above-&gt;next = latest;
    }
}
</code></pre>

<p>This is like the meeting place for Order of the Phoenix
in the Harry Potter universe! We squeeze in a new house between
the two existing houses.</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-deletion" class="anchor" href="#deletion" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Deletion</h4>

<p>As with insertion, we have to consider
if we're deleting a node at the top
or one somewhere else in the list. </p>

<p>Our code will handle both cases,
but checking for the easier case first
means efficiency.</p>

<p>Note that a <code>return</code> statement that returns nothing
is usable for a function of return-type <code>void</code>.</p>

<pre><code>void deleteCar(const std::string&amp; doomed)
{
    // First: check if the list is empty
    // There's nothing to delete if so

    if (m_head == nullptr)
        return; 

    // Next, see if the first node 
    // is the one to be deleted

    // Ridiculously long cat screeching as it dies

    if (m_head-&gt;name == doomed)
    {
        // Start the deletion process
        // by assigning a temp pointer to the
        // doomed node; don't wanna forget its
        // location

        Car* killMe = m_head; 

        // Have m_head point to the new top node,
        // the one right after the doomed one
        // This way, we don't lose track
        // of the rest of the list

        m_head = killMe-&gt;next;

        // This could be m_head-&gt;next too
        // as m_head == killMe right now

        // This doesn't work if the doomed
        // node is NOT at the top of the list
        // though; then, we need to link
        // the above node to the node after
        // the doomed one

        // It's like building a bridge over
        // a river that suddenly appears,
        // or creating a patch over a hole
        // in a tire or the road

        // Rain down death and destruction
        // upon the doomed node

        delete killMe; 

        // This will NOT work with delete m_head
        // Then we lose the rest of the list!
</code></pre>

<p><code>next</code> is a common identifier for the next pointer.
<code>head</code> and <code>tail</code> are also common. </p>

<p>Do NOT forget to reassign the head pointer BEFORE
deleting the top node.</p>

<pre><code>        // We needed the temporary pointer;
        // we'd have to delete m_head otherwise
        // and we'd lose track of the start of
        // the list
    }
</code></pre>

<p>Next case: the doomed node is an interior one</p>

<pre><code>    // List traversal to find one just above it

    Car* above = m_head
    while (above-&gt;next != nullptr)
    {
        // Found node above the doomed one
        // Is the next node the one we want
        // to delete? 

        if (above-&gt;next-&gt;name == doomed)
            break;

        above = above-&gt;next;
    }

    // Did NOT find the item
    // above points to last node, 
    // whose next is nullptr
</code></pre>

<p>Do NOT forget to account for the
case that the wanted item does not exist!</p>

<pre><code>    if (above-&gt;next == nullptr)
        return;

    // Otherwise, delete, delete!
    // The next one after above is
    // the doomed node

    Car* killMe = above-&gt;next;

    // Link above node to node
    // after the doomed one

    above-&gt;next = killMe-&gt;next;

    // Also possible: above-&gt;next-&gt;next;

    // Delete the target node
    delete killMe;

    // The last four statements
    // work even for the last node
}
</code></pre>

<p>Unencumbered by comments:</p>

<pre><code>void deleteCar(const std::string&amp; doomed)
{
    if (m_head == nullptr)
        return; 

    if (m_head-&gt;name == doomed)
    {   
        Car* killMe = m_head; 
        m_head = killMe-&gt;next;
        delete killMe; 
    }

    Car* above = m_head
    while (above-&gt;next != nullptr)
    {   
        if (above-&gt;next-&gt;name == doomed)
            break;

        above = above-&gt;next;
    }

    if (above-&gt;next = nullptr)
        return;

    Car* killMe = above-&gt;next;
    above-&gt;next = killMe-&gt;next;
    delete killMe;
}
</code></pre>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-processing" class="anchor" href="#processing" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Processing</h4>

<p>To do stuff with a linked list, 
we traverse through it and do
whatever we'd like to do with it, 
e.g. print out its contents,
compute an average, etc. </p>

<p>Syntax:</p>

<pre><code>void printCarsList() const
{
    // Print every item
    // nullptr lets us know we're
    // at the last one

    Car* temp = m_head;
    while (temp-&gt;next != nullptr)
    {
        cout &lt;&lt; temp-&gt;name &lt;&lt; endl;

        // Don't forget to increment!
        temp = temp-&gt;next;
    }
}
</code></pre>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-destruction" class="anchor" href="#destruction" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Destruction</h4>

<p>Now for that destructor!</p>

<p>Every node has something dynamically allocated,
so <code>delete</code> needs to be rained down upon all of them. </p>

<p>We cannot just do this as part of a normal
traversal though; we'd delete the temp each otherwise,
and we'd lost the rest of the list.</p>

<p>Instead, we'll have two temporary pointers,
just as we did with a deletion operation on a single
item: one to point to the doomed node, one to point
to the one right after it. We simply shift both along. </p>

<pre><code>~Train()
{
    Car* temp = m_head;
    Car* nextOne;

    while (temp != nullptr)
    {
        nextOne = temp-&gt;next;
        delete temp;
        temp = nextOne;
    }
}
</code></pre>

<p>On the last run, nextOne will point
to <code>nullptr</code>, the node temp points to
will be deleted, and temp will be set to 
<code>nullptr</code>, thus meeting the loop-terminating condition. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-tail-pointers" class="anchor" href="#tail-pointers" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Tail Pointers</h4>

<p>We have a head pointer that points to the start
of a linked list; why not have one that points
to the end (tail) of one as well!</p>

<p>This will greatly speed up some of our operations. </p>

<p>Updating our previous code: </p>

<pre><code>class Train
{
    ...

    private:

        Car* m_head;
        Car* m_tail;
};

void addCarRear(const std::string&amp; name)
{
    if (m_head == nullptr)
        addCarFront(name);
</code></pre>

<p>addCarFront() will need to be updated
to support adjusting the tail poitner as necessary. </p>

<pre><code>    Car *temp = m_head; // Start at top of linked list

    while(temp-&gt;next != nullptr)
    {
        temp = temp-&gt;next; // Follow next pointer to next node
    }

    Car *latest = new Car;

    latest-&gt;name = name;
    latest-&gt;next = nullptr;

    // Link old last node to new last node
    m_tail-&gt;next = latest; 

    // Update tail pointer to point to new last node
    m_tail = latest; 
}
</code></pre>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-doubly-linked-lists" class="anchor" href="#doubly-linked-lists" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Doubly-Linked Lists</h3>

<p>In a doubly-linked list, every
node has next AND previous pointers. </p>

<pre><code>struct Car
{
    Car* p_next;
    Car* p_prev;
}; 
</code></pre>

<p>The first node's <code>previous</code> must point
to <code>nullptr</code>. The last node's <code>next</code> must point
to <code>nullptr</code>.</p>

<p>Head and tail pointers are usable with a doubly-linked
list. Moving up and down is now very easy.</p>

<p>Now, the implementations of all of our member functions
for the containing class change. With each insertion,
deletion, etc., we'll need to update:</p>

<ol>
<li>The new node's <code>next</code> and <code>previous</code> pointers. </li>
<li>The preceding node's <code>next</code> pointer</li>
<li>The following node's <code>previous</code> pointer</li>
</ol>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-linked-lists-cheat-sheet" class="anchor" href="#linked-lists-cheat-sheet" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Linked Lists Cheat Sheet</h3>

<p>Keep in mind: NEVER access a node
until the pointer to it has been validated
as a valid one. <code>delete</code> on a <code>nullptr</code> has no
effect, but dereferencing it is undefined behavior.</p>

<pre><code>if (ptr != nullptr)
    // Access node data
</code></pre>

<p>Advance ptr: </p>

<pre><code>if (ptr != nullptr)
    ptr = ptr-&gt;next;
</code></pre>

<p>See if ptr points to the last node in a list:</p>

<pre><code>if (ptr != nullptr &amp;&amp; ptr-&gt;next == nullptr)
    // ptr points to last node
</code></pre>

<p>To get the next node's data:</p>

<pre><code>if (ptr != nullptr &amp;&amp; ptr-&gt;next != nullptr)
    // Access next node's data
</code></pre>

<p>To get head node data:</p>

<pre><code>if (m_head != nullptr)
    // Access m_head-&gt;blah
</code></pre>

<p>To check if a list is empty:</p>

<pre><code>if (m_head == nullptr)
    // List is empty
</code></pre>

<p>Note that we have two classes or structs:
one for the nodes, one for the linked list. 
It's easier if a struct is used for the nodes,
but these are kept in the private part of
the containing class. Also, tail &amp; head
pointers can be supplemented with a counter
for the number of objects in the linked
list.</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-linked-lists-vs-arrays" class="anchor" href="#linked-lists-vs-arrays" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Linked Lists vs. Arrays</h3>

<p>Accessing an item in an array is faster;
we can immediately subscript straight to
it.</p>

<p>Inserting or deleting an item 
is much easier with a linked list though. </p>

<p>However, they also take far more effort
to code and are more likely to be buggy.
Don't use a linked list unless it is
necessary. </p>

<p>// Notes: Carey is finally back, but he risks
// losing his voice for a whole another week if 
// he lectures too much today. He has a sub coming
// in to take over the latter part of today.</p>

<p>// He had a really bad laryingitis infection
// and has to use his phone's voice to communicate</p>

<p>// Kim, a former student of his, is here as sub
// Her undergrad was in theater, but she's
// getting a master's in CS, and is working
// at Google starting this fall</p>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-discussion-1-with-forney-24-january-2014" class="anchor" href="#discussion-1-with-forney-24-january-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Discussion #1 with Forney: 24 January 2014</h2>

<p>Construction order for classes with
members of other class types (class composition): </p>

<ol>
<li>Initializer list</li>
<li>Default-construct all members</li>
<li>Move into constructor body </li>
</ol>

<p>From Forney's NoisyClass example:</p>

<p>Note that local variables in a loop-body
go out of scope after each iteration. </p>

<p>C
C</p>

<h1>
<a id="" class="anchor" href="#" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>~</h1>

<p>D
D
(D) - local variable destroyed 
      as it's popped off the stack</p>

<p>Check that last node exists:</p>

<pre><code>if (m_tail != nullptr)
</code></pre>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-lecture-7-26-january-2014" class="anchor" href="#lecture-7-26-january-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lecture 7: 26 January 2014</h2>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-inheritance" class="anchor" href="#inheritance" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Inheritance</h3>

<p>We are blasting forward, and fast! </p>

<p>Inheritance is the idea of forming
new classes using ones that have already
been defined. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-shieldedrobot--student-analogies" class="anchor" href="#shieldedrobot--student-analogies" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>ShieldedRobot &amp; Student Analogies</h3>

<p>Consider of a newly-sentient
being of type Robot. </p>

<p>Side-lecture: </p>

<p>"Do you want to be a passive consumer
or someone who creates stuff that makes
the world a better place?"</p>

<p>Carey is having that midlife crisis
now, haha. </p>

<p>"Otherwise, you'll have an empty life."</p>

<p>Now consider a very similar Robot
that also has a shield, so we'd have a 
ShieldedRobot. </p>

<p>Note that its members are all very similar
and we would waste time recreating all that. </p>

<p>The only difference here is the shield. 
Essentially, a ShieldedRobot is a Robot
with some additional methods/data. </p>

<p>Another example: a Person and a Student.
A Student is a Person--but with a GPA and an ID. </p>

<p><strong>Never duplicate code</strong>. That introduces
the possibility of more bugs, and also wastes time. </p>

<p>So we'll define a <strong>subclass</strong> (ShieldedRobot) and have it
<strong>inherit</strong> (reuse) the functions and data
of a <strong>superclass</strong>. (Robot)</p>

<p>Then, the subclass has the new methods/data, known 
as <strong>specialization</strong>.</p>

<pre><code>class ShieldedRobot "is a kind of" Robot
{
    // Has all the Robot stuff
    // PLUS:

    /// Shield stuff
}
</code></pre>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-terminology" class="anchor" href="#terminology" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Terminology</h4>

<ol>
<li><p>Define the superclass</p></li>
<li><p>Define the subclass, based on the superclass,
adding any new stuff as needed</p></li>
</ol>

<p>A ShieldedRobot will have all the Robot
methods and data too; C++ determines automatically
which function to call. We can overload inside
the subclass.</p>

<p>Anytime we have a relationship,
"A is a type of B", <em>inheritance</em> may be warranted. </p>

<p>In contrast, a Name is part of a Person.
So a Name would be a member of Person. </p>

<p>BUT, a Person is derived from Animal.</p>

<p>We can inherit many, many times:
A CSNerd is a Person is a Mammal is an Animal.</p>

<p>So each specialization is a "Blah" with "additional".</p>

<p>The superclass is also called a <strong>base class</strong>. 
The subclass is also called a <strong>derived class</strong>.</p>

<p>A class can be a derived class of some
other class, but also a base class for other 
classes. E.g., Mammal is a subclass of Animal
but a superclass for Person, Monkey, etc. as well. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-syntax-2" class="anchor" href="#syntax-2" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Syntax</h4>

<pre><code>class ShieldedRobot : public Robot
</code></pre>

<p>This says that a ShieldedRobot
publicly acknowledges that it is a subclass
of a Robot. There is such a thing as
private inheritance, but is usually not needed;
it basically makes it a member. Just use
class composition instead. </p>

<p>This is <em>public inheritance</em>. </p>

<p>What if a subclass doesn't need a superclass'
functionality? We'll have to design around
that, e.g. render the askOutOnaDate()
function of CSNerd an empty one. The Person
does that, not the CSNerd. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-virtual-member-functions" class="anchor" href="#virtual-member-functions" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Virtual Member Functions</h3>

<p>A derived class can also <em>override</em> the existing
functions from the base class. </p>

<p>Student: "Go Bruins!"
NerdyStudent: "I love circuits!"</p>

<p>What if the derived class' function had a different
function signature? Then it's not the same function,
and this isn't overriding. </p>

<p>But if we are overriding, put the <strong>virtual</strong>
keywords in front of both the base class
and the derived class functions (the original
and the replacement). It is technically only needed
in the base class, but good style has it in front
of both. </p>

<p>If a NerdyStudent has its own member variables,
then it must have its own constructor,
initializing only the stuff</p>

<p>If no override, the subclass will use the superclass'
version of the function, e.g. the StudlyStudent will 
say "Go Bruins" too, but the <strong>virtual</strong> will be needed
in Student if NerdyStudent still redefines the catchPhrase() 
function. Put the virtual at the highest level.</p>

<pre><code>class DerivedClass : public SuperClass
{
    public:

        virtual bool catchPhrase();

}
</code></pre>

<p>What happens if <code>virtual</code> is placed in front of all
the base class? It will slow down the program. </p>

<p>If the member functions are defined outside the class,
only put <code>virtual</code> within the class definition. 
Putting it outside of the class decl's is not allowed, </p>

<pre><code>void NerdyStudent::catchPhrase()
{
    cout &lt;&lt; "I love circuits!" &lt;&lt; endl;
}
</code></pre>

<p>If it's inlined inside the subclass, have <code>virtual</code>
in the superclass and the subclass. </p>

<p>ONLY use <code>virtual</code> for functions that we want
to override/redefine. It will only slow down the program
otherwise, though that will NOT produce errors. </p>

<p>Again, <code>virtual</code> is TECHNICALLY ONLY needed in the subclass. </p>

<p>What if we had Robot -&gt; ComedianRobot -&gt; ReallyBadComedianRobot</p>

<p>Where would virtual be needed? Best pratice: in all 3,
EVEN IF only ReallyBadComedianRobot redefined the catchPhrase() 
function.</p>

<p>Can we have inherit from two classes at once? YES!</p>

<pre><code>class ComedianRobot : public Robot, public Comedian
</code></pre>

<p>Java can't do it, but C++ can, but it is generally
frowned upon. </p>

<p>What if inherited from two classes, but both
have redefined talk() functions? Which one is
used? Office hours! </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-inheritance-function-call-rules" class="anchor" href="#inheritance-function-call-rules" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Inheritance Function Call Rules</h4>

<p>Examples: Student and NerdyStudent.</p>

<p>The NerdyStudent has a virtual cheer() member funiton.</p>

<ol>
<li><p>IF you define a function in the base class
<em>and</em> redefine in the derived class then the derived version of the 
function will <em>hide</em> the base clas version of the function.</p></li>
<li><p>Any public functions defined in the base class,
but <em>not redefined</em> in a derived class can be called
normally by all parts of our program. </p></li>
</ol>

<p>Note that virtual member functions may call
the superclass' member functions too BUT:</p>

<ol>
<li>IF your code wants to call a function in the base class
that's been redefined in the derived class,
it must use a class:: prefix. Scope resolution. </li>
</ol>

<p>Note:</p>

<pre><code>void getExcitedAboutCS()
{

    cheer(); 

    // Call NerdyStudent's cheer()
    // If not there, look for a cheer() in Student
    // If not there, look for it in Person(), etc. 

    Student::cheer();
}
</code></pre>

<p>Calls the most derived version of the function that
we can. Example:  ReallyNerdyStudent would use NerdyStudent's
cheer() if it didn't redefine it. If NerdyStudent doesn't have
a cheer(), look in Person, etc. This is if we don't specify
which class' version of the function to use. </p>

<p>Can go up multiple levels in one step: ClassName::function();</p>

<p>To call from main():</p>

<pre><code>object.ClassName::function();
</code></pre>

<p>Again, can go up the hierarchy in one step. </p>

<p>We can have something like:</p>

<pre><code>void talk()
{
    // Modified speech

    Person::talk(); // Do stuff from the base class too
}
</code></pre>

<p>This a common pattern: have base class do some work,
have the derived class do some work. </p>

<p>E.g., </p>

<p>Harry Potter story:</p>

<p>7th book was ordered, but delivered
to work. He had to wait till Monday rather than
Saturday. He runs to the mailroom.</p>

<p>"Do you have a package for me?"</p>

<p>"No."</p>

<p>Same thing Tuesday. And Wednesday. 
Finally, on Thursday. </p>

<p>"It's for my niece."</p>

<p>Someone else: </p>

<p>"And that proves he's a Nerd, not just a Person."
Lol, that was Smallberg!</p>

<p>Boom, toasted Pop Tart time.</p>

<p>"I need a volunteer. Yes, you--if it starts smoking,
<em>tell me</em>."</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-inheritance-and-construction" class="anchor" href="#inheritance-and-construction" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Inheritance and Construction</h4>

<p>Again, ShieldedRobot and Robot.</p>

<p>Rules:</p>

<p>Classes are constructed from superclasses
to subclasses. This makes sense! (Per Jaclyn)
We're building <em>what we need first</em> first, then
we build the rest. </p>

<p>They are <em>destructed</em> from subclasses to superclasses. </p>

<p>Imagine a Robot has a Battery member variable
and the ShieldedRobot has a ShieldGenerator. </p>

<p>The order of construction: </p>

<p>Build Robot's members (i.e. Battery and the primitives)
Build Robot
Build ShieldRobot's members (i.e. ShieldGenerator)
Build ShieldRobot</p>

<p>BaseClass's members, BaseClass, Subclass' members, subclass</p>

<p>Destruction is reversed, from sub to super:</p>

<p>ShieldedRobot, ShieldGenerator, Robot, Battery (as we learned
with class composition). </p>

<p>So it's the same as before, just accounting for the inheritance 
as well. Same as before for if something is derived
from two classes at once too. </p>

<p>In general:</p>

<p>base class members -&gt; base class -&gt; subclass members -&gt; subclass</p>

<p>What about with constructors that require parameters? </p>

<p>Let's have Duck, derived from Animal. </p>

<p>How do we initialize the Animal? With an initializer list
in Duck! </p>

<p>Rule: If a superclass requires parameters during
construction, then you must add an initializer list to
the subclass constructor. </p>

<p>Syntax:</p>

<pre><code>Duck() : Animal(2) // Arbitrarily: all Ducks weigh 2 lbs
{
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>Before Duck is constructed, Animal will be constructed
first. It MUST happen here, in the initializer list.
It CANNOT happen anywhere else. </p>

<p>In the first need for initializer list, with class composition, the
<em>containing class</em> has the initializer list. Here,
the subclass has the initializer list. </p>

<p>Does the order matter in the initializer list? No,
but some compilers may give warnings/errors in some warning modes. 
Also, remember that members are initialized </p>

<p>Can an Animal initialize from a parameter passed to Duck? </p>

<p>Yes!</p>

<pre><code>Animal(int weight, int coolness);
...
Duck(int weight, int coolness) : Animal(weight, coolness)
</code></pre>

<p>Paramters to Duck -&gt; Build Animal members -&gt; Build Animal
-&gt; Build Duck members -&gt; Build Duck</p>

<p>Oh, look a customized duck that has Animal characteristics!</p>

<p>Remember, the base class is always constructed before the derived
class. If Animal were derived from Organism and that needed
constructor parameters? Animal would need an initializer list too. </p>

<p>In general: a class intializes its superclass. 
In memory: an object of a class will have all of its members
plus the superclasses. </p>

<p>We can even customize parameters with expressions like
<code>weight - GLOBAL_CONSTANT</code>, etc. </p>

<p>In general: a subclass' constructor initializes ONLY
the subclass' members, but initializes its superclass,
if needed. Thus, going down the inheritance hierarchy,
our objects can become more and more detailed. </p>

<p>Again, destruction order
within a class: container destructor, member destructors</p>

<p>Could we derive from ? We could, but little point to it. </p>

<p>Before we run constructor/destructor, all the member variables
must be present! The 'structors may need them! </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-inheritance-and-member-visibility" class="anchor" href="#inheritance-and-member-visibility" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Inheritance and Member Visibility</h4>

<p>So, we have Robot and ShieldedRobot. Can a ShieldedRobot
member function access a Robot's private data? </p>

<p>NO! Because private data members are accessible ONLY
to the member functions of the <em>same class</em>. Derived classes
may NOT access the base class' private members. </p>

<p>Call base class' public functions instead. </p>

<p>What if ShieldedRobot had its own coordinate member
data? No problem. But a main() version call of setX()
would call the base class's version of setX(), and do nothing
with the subclass' setX(), unless we said <code>object.SubClass::setX()</code></p>

<p>IF there's no parametrized constructor, you must use
public member functions. </p>

<p>Once more: <strong>Derived classes may NOT access the private members
of their base classes.</strong></p>

<p>All private members of the base class are hidden from the derived
class, and may only be accessed via the public interface of the base
class. </p>

<p>"Proof": All animals have a spleen (private member). 
Can we touch our spleens?</p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-protected-members" class="anchor" href="#protected-members" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Protected Members</h5>

<p>If we want our derived class to access private member <em>functions</em> 
of the base class, use the <code>protected</code> access specifier:</p>

<pre><code>class Robot
{
    ...

    protected:
        void chargeBattery();
}
</code></pre>

<p>What about data members under protected? "Extremely bad style";
we're violating encapsulation! Then, we'd have to worry about changing
a lot of different stuff. </p>

<p>Now anything <em>derived</em> from the Robot class can access chargeBattery().
E.g., main() cannot call the protected member function; only the subclasses
can. </p>

<p>Overriding protected member functions is perfectly possible as well. s</p>

<p>Rules: </p>

<p>Public in base class B:</p>

<ul>
<li><p>Any function in class B can access it</p></li>
<li><p>Any function in classes derived from B can access it</p></li>
<li><p>All classes/functions unrelated to B may access it</p></li>
</ul>

<p>Private:</p>

<ul>
<li><p>Any function in B can access it</p></li>
<li><p>No functions in classes derived can access it</p></li>
<li><p>NO classes/functions unrelated to B can access it</p></li>
</ul>

<p>Protected:</p>

<ul>
<li><p>Any function in class B can access it</p></li>
<li><p>Any function in classes derived from B may access it</p></li>
<li><p>NO classes/functions unrelated to B can access it</p></li>
</ul>

<p>What if one instance of a derived instance is set equal
to another? By default, the copy constructor and the
assignment operator copies base data, then the derived data. 
This is a <em>shallow copy</em>. </p>

<p>BUT if the base or derived classes have dynamically allocated
member data, then both super and subclasses need a redefined
copy constructor and assignment operator. </p>

<p>Look to the PowerPoint. Subclass CC calls the superclass CC first. 
Subclass [=] calls the superclass [=] first.</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-recap" class="anchor" href="#recap" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Recap</h4>

<p>We reuse code in a base class and derive new classes that
are specialized with new behaviors and data. 
E.g., Bat Mobile derived from Car.</p>

<p>Overriding redefines an existing behavior from the base class
with a new behavior in the derived class. </p>

<p>From office hours:</p>

<p>What if have classes A &amp; B,  and B's foo() calls
A's foo, but then we insert a new class in-between 
A &amp; B which also has foo()? </p>

<p>And in B, we said: A::foo()? Is there a way to make it go
up one level automatically? Not as far as we know right here.</p>

<p>But be careful:</p>

<pre><code>virtual void foo()
{
    cout &lt;&lt; "..." &lt;&lt; endl;
    foo(); // RECURSION!
}
</code></pre>

<p>"What's a dummy node? "</p>

<p>In the linked list class,
have a member dummyNode of the node struct type.
The dummy node's value is irrelevant and unimportant. </p>

<p>Then, have the dummyNode's next point point to
the first actual node (the head pointer). </p>

<p>Why do this? With insertion, we'd have to have 
special code just for inserting at the very top.
With a dummy node, we're always inserting between
nodes; at the top, it's inserting between the dummy node
and the previous top node. </p>

<p>With a dummy node, we use its next pointer as the head
pointer; we don't need a head pointer. </p>

<p>How to know when we've hit the end of a circular list? 
If the node's next pointer points to the head pointer. </p>

<p>ONLY modify result after we've done all rule-following.
Double-check all the different cases of the possible
parameter combinations. </p>

<p>"What does <code>inline</code> mean?"</p>

<p>An inline function is one whose body is defined
in the class declaration. The <code>inline</code> keyword
in front of the function would be here: </p>

<p>This is only at the function definition (outside
the class declaration), NOT at its declaration,
but this IN THE HEADER FILE.</p>

<pre><code>inline _return_type ClassName::function() const
</code></pre>

<p>This keyword would mean that the function calls
arereplaced by the function's actual code. 
This is optional as to whether or not it actually
happens, and compiler-dependent.</p>

<p>Normally, a program would jump from function to function. </p>

<pre><code>class Foo
{
    public:

        void bar() { ... } // Automatically inline

};
</code></pre>

<p>Else, do: </p>

<pre><code>class Foo
{
    public: 

        void bar();
};

inline void Foo::bar()
{
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>Inline makes compile-time longer but run-time
shorter. </p>

<p>Smallberg writes all the specs for homework
and projects, except for Projects 3 and 4, which
Carey writes. </p>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-lecture-8-29-january-2014" class="anchor" href="#lecture-8-29-january-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lecture 8: 29 January 2014</h2>

<p>// Candy for Chinese New Year!</p>

<p>Story: Carey was 5 years into 
career with Symantec. He was sent up
to Saratoga to meet with a customer,
someone who had purchased a lot of 
Symantec software. He there to schmooze
the person over dinner. There's
two guys; one is a VP, one is a director,
from an insurance company. The whole evening
he called Bill "Bob". The guy next to him
finally went "His name is Bill, not Bob."</p>

<p>Carey: "Bill, Bob, whatever." 
Ended up apologizing a lot and not getting fired. </p>

<p>Midterm review lecture up. The 5 people who
showed up to office hours this morning all got $20. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-polymorphism" class="anchor" href="#polymorphism" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Polymorphism</h3>

<p>This is not specific to C++, 
but shared across all OOP languages. </p>

<p>Consider a function that takes an object
of some class-type. Can it also accept
objects of sub-classes derived from that base class? </p>

<p>Yes! But this cannot go the other way around; 
e.g. cannot give Person to function expecting Student. </p>

<p>If a function accepts a <em>pointer or reference</em> to a
class-type, then we can also pass any objects of subclass-types!</p>

<p>Why? Because, a sub-class is IS the super-class;
anything a Person can do, a Student can do--and more! </p>

<p>The function <em>will not know</em> that that it's actuallly
working on an object of a derived class type. It just 
treats the object as being of the expected parameter type. </p>

<p>We could determine if the passed argument is of a derived
class type. Saved for office hours. </p>

<p>Any time we use a base pointer or reference to access
a derived object, this is called <strong>polymorphism</strong>.</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-shape-example" class="anchor" href="#shape-example" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Shape Example</h4>

<p>Consider a <code>Shape</code> base class. </p>

<p>It'll have a member function named <code>getArea()</code>,
made a virtual member function for overriding
in classes <code>Square</code> and <code>Circle</code>. </p>

<p>Square's getArea() calculates differently from 
Circle's, a consequence of geometry. </p>

<p>A problem? If we write a price calculator 
for each different shape; that's <em>code duplication</em>! </p>

<p>Instead, do it all in one function: </p>

<pre><code>void printPrice(const Shape&amp; s, const double price)
</code></pre>

<p>C++ will figure out which getArea() to call. 
It figures out the correct virtual function to call,
if it's been redefined. It will always use the most
derived version. This is all true even though
the printPrice() function has no idea
that it may be acting on a derived object. </p>

<p>You cannot use any functions that are not in 
the base class and also virtual. It'll even use
the base class version, if it's the right one. </p>

<p>If we pass by vlue, we'll encounter <em>slicing</em>. </p>

<p>In the base class, have all of the common things
that all the subclasses have in common. </p>

<p>Is there any way to do different things based on
the type of object passed in? Yes. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-inheritance-vs-polymorphism" class="anchor" href="#inheritance-vs-polymorphism" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Inheritance vs. Polymorphism</h4>

<p>Inheritance: we derive one or more classes
from a base/super class. They all <em>inherit</em> a 
common set of functions and may redfine their own
specializaed functions. </p>

<p>Polymorphism: We may use a base pointer or reference 
to access any variable of a derived class type. 
The same function call autamtically casuses
different actions to occur, depending on what type
of variable is being pointed to/referenced. The
referred-to, pointed-to object automatically behaves
in an appropriate manner, without writing <em>special code</em>
for every single different type. </p>

<p>With polymorphism, we can design and implement systems
that are easily extensible.</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-the-importance-of-virtual" class="anchor" href="#the-importance-of-virtual" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>The Importance of <code>Virtual</code>
</h4>

<p>The <code>virtual</code> keyword figures out what class is being
referenced and calls the right function. The
virtual keyword is a <em>necessity</em>. </p>

<p>Inside of the function using the base class type, 
functions specific to derived classes are <em>invisible</em>
to it! </p>

<p>When we forget <code>virtual</code>, C++ no longer looks
for the appropriate version of the function;
instead, it just uses the version found in the 
base class type. This is an insidious logic error!
In Java, everything is virtual by default. </p>

<ol>
<li><p>So, anytime we redefine a function, use <code>virtual</code>
in the base class <em>and</em> the derived classes. 
The functions are <code>virtual</code> from the base class
with the <code>virtual</code> keyword onward. Just put
<code>virtual</code> in the derived classes to be clear. </p></li>
<li><p>ALWAYS use the <code>virtual</code> keyword in destructors
in base <em>and</em> and derived classes. IF there is ANY
chance that a class may be use as a base class,
have <code>virtual</code> in its destructor declaration! </p></li>
<li><p>No such thing as a virtual constructor. (That's
not needed). </p></li>
</ol>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-superclass-and-subclass-pointers" class="anchor" href="#superclass-and-subclass-pointers" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Superclass and subclass Pointers</h4>

<p>Can we point a base class pointer to a
an object of a derived class type? YES,
because a derived object IS of a base class type.</p>

<p><em>In general, we can point a superclass pointer</em>
<em>at a subclassed variable.</em></p>

<p>Just note that the superclass pointer
cannot access any members specific to a subclass. </p>

<p>C++ will dynamically use the right virtual function.</p>

<p>"upcast" means that we point a superclass pointer
at a derived class object. </p>

<p>Always make the destructor in the base class
virtual. Every derived class' destructor is then
automatically virtual. </p>

<p>Whether we use a superclass pointer or subclass
pointer depends on what we need to with that object.</p>

<p>Story time: underprivileged kids from inner city
visit Symantec. </p>

<p>5 standing around Carey in a circle. He completely
repeated all around, completely forgetting a face
in just seconds! </p>

<p>Wait, what about the array of different Shapes?
Doesn't an array require that the memory be contiguous
and the elements be of the same size? Not here, 
because it's an array of <em>pointers</em>! </p>

<p>QUESTION: Can we point a subclassed pointer to a
superclass variable? NO! </p>

<p>While all Squares are a Shape, a Shape is not a square.
The relationship is one-way. </p>

<p>We may <em>never</em> point a derived class pointer/reference
to a base class variable. </p>

<p>Most-Derived</p>

<p>What is the rule for determining the "right"
function? C++ will select the <em>most-derived</em>
virtual function, even if that function is called
from a non-virtual function. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-virtual-destructors" class="anchor" href="#virtual-destructors" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Virtual Destructors</h4>

<p>IF we intend to derive a class from a base class,
that base class MUST have a <em>virtual destructor</em>. </p>

<p>DO NOT FORGET! </p>

<p>If we forget a virtual destructor, </p>

<p>Construction order: </p>

<ol>
<li><p>Memory allocated</p></li>
<li><p>Superclass to subclass. 
-&gt; Members of class then class. </p></li>
</ol>

<p>Note, when we delete an object, C++
sees a death order on a base class type, 
but everything is virtual, so we call the sub class
destructor then the super class destructor.</p>

<p>Destruction order:</p>

<ol>
<li>Subclass to superclass</li>
</ol>

<p>If the pointer were to a subclass pointer, 
both destructors would be called (in the
same order: most-derived to most-basic), because
the subclass has the superclass bits too! 
<code>Virtual</code> is not needed so much then; the problem
is if we have a superclass pointer to a subclassed pointer,
then we need the <code>virtual</code> to call the correct most-derived
destructor.</p>

<p><em>We may not redefine any destructors from a more basic class</em>. </p>

<p>Say we have D derived from B:</p>

<pre><code>B
    m1

D
    m2
</code></pre>

<p>Construction: m1, B, m2, D - most basic (members first) to most derived</p>

<p>Destruction: ~D, ~m2, ~B, ~m1 - most-derived to most-basic</p>

<p>Never use assignment operators with polymorphism. </p>

<p>Technically, lacking the <code>virtual</code> with destructors
is <em>undefined</em> behavior. </p>

<p>Most of the time, C++ calls the base class' destructor,
and <em>never</em> calls the derived class' destructor. It frees
the memory, and we lose the pointers to any dynamic 
members of the derived class. <em>Memory leak</em>! </p>

<p>What if we have non-dynamic memory in the derived class? 
It's still undefined! Just put <code>virtual</code> in the base class! </p>

<p>This is if we have polymorphism; if the pointer
were to the subclass, the subclass destructor would be
called correctly, as well as the superclass destructor. </p>

<p><strong>Destructing a derived object always destructs up the hierarchy!</strong></p>

<p>The compiler will know the inheritance hierarchy and spit
back a compile-time error if we try to point a subclass pointer
at a superclass pointer. Note also that we cannot delete things
twice. Don't mix up pointers! </p>

<p><code>Virtual</code> destructors are only needed with polymorphism,
because then C++ may encounter situations where it's unsure
what needs to be destructed. THAT SAID, have the destructor
be virtual anyway, in case we later modify the code to use
polymorphism anyway. ALWAYS define a virtual destructor. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-vtables" class="anchor" href="#vtables" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Vtables</h4>

<p>When a variable is defined, an invisble "vtable"
is pointing to the proper set of functions to use,
with an entry for every virtual function in the class. 
ONLY virtual functions are found in this table. </p>

<p>If the variable is of a subclass type, its vtable
will have a pointer to its subclass version of the
virtual function. So, every object knows exactly
what version of the function to call. As long as 
the base class has <code>virtual</code>, that function is
virtual from then on.</p>

<p><em>We can have pointers to functions!</em></p>

<p>Note that the vtables are used at run-time,
not compile-time. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-summary" class="anchor" href="#summary" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Summary</h4>

<p>First we figure out what we want to represent
in general. </p>

<p>Then we define a base class that contains functions
common to all of the derived classes. </p>

<p>Then we write our derived classes, creating specialized
versions of each common function. </p>

<p>We can then access derived variables with a base class
pointer or reference. </p>

<p>Finally, we must always have a virtual destructor in our
base class, whether it needs it or not. (Using
inheritance, create a virtual destructor!)</p>

<p>OMG: How many people have asked the exact same question: 
"Do the derived classes need <code>virtual</code> in front their 
function signatures?"</p>

<p>Does it make sense to have Shape objects? No, 
so its functions are really dummy functions. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-pure-virtual-functions" class="anchor" href="#pure-virtual-functions" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Pure Virtual Functions</h3>

<p>We <em>must</em> define functions that are common
to all derived classes in our <em>base class</em>
<em>to use polymorphism</em>. The lame thing is
the <em>dummy functions</em> in the derived classes.
(But without them, we wouldn't be able to 
use polymorphism). </p>
