<p>// LOL, Carey and a student blowing a spider back and forth at each other. </p>

<p>Since they are never used, their implementations could be anthing. 
But it'd be even better if we could remove them altogether. </p>

<p>We define <em>abstract functions</em></p>

<p>Syntax:</p>

<pre><code>virtual _returnType _identifer() = 0; 
</code></pre>

<p>These are called <strong>pure virtual functions</strong>. </p>

<p>Rules. </p>

<ol>
<li>Make a base class function pure virtual if we realize
that the base-class version does not do anything useful. </li>
</ol>

<p>Pure virtual functions can be in subclasses too! </p>

<p>If a base class has a pure virtual function,
then its derived classes <em>must</em> define their own version
of it. </p>

<p>If a class has <em>at least one</em> pure virtual function,
than it is called an <strong>abstract base class</strong> (ABC). 
Not "American Born Chinese", haha. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-abstract-base-classes" class="anchor" href="#abstract-base-classes" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Abstract Base Classes</h3>

<p>Rules: </p>

<ol>
<li>Must either provide code for <em>all</em> pure virtual functions</li>
<li>Or the derived class becomes an abstract base class itself!</li>
</ol>

<p>Some definitions can be omitted, then the derived class 
effectively has that pure virtual function too. </p>

<p>Look up the inheritance hierarchy: does it have defined
versions from previous superclasses, and then fills in the 
rest? If so, that function is considered defined.</p>

<p><em>If a class is an ABC</em>, we <em>CANNOT define any instances of it</em>
but we can define pointers to them. </p>

<p>But, if there is at least one function that is an inherited
pure virtual function, and we don't redefine them, then
the subclass becomes an ABC itself, and we cannot define
any instances of it. </p>

<p>Note: we do not repeat the PVF in the subclass; it
just inherits it; it "virtually" has the pure virtual function.</p>

<p>Why would we use PVFs and ABCs? IT forces the programmer
to implement certain functions to prevent common mistakes,
like forgetting to implement the specialized virtual functions
of subclasses. </p>

<p>If we forget (and the forgotten function is not made pure virtual),
then we call the base class version by mistake. If we did pure virtual,
then we'd get an area, look at it and go ("Wait, that's not an ABC.
Wait, there's supposed to be a virtual function specialiazed to
that class derived from Shape!" Oh, right, I did not implement...")</p>

<p>Pure virtual functions <em>force the programmer to implement</em>
the <em>correct versions in the derived class</em>. </p>

<p>Still, we can use ABC pointers and references; this lets 
us do polymorphism with that base class (and therefore
pass in instances of derived classes). We never use the
ABC member functions; instead, we call the redefined virtual functions
of the derived types. </p>

<p>Example: </p>

<pre><code>void printPrice(Shape&amp; s)
{
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>Note that we <em>cannot pass-by-value with an ABC</em>. That would require
creating a Shape, which is illegal with an ABC. </p>

<p>Also legal, </p>

<pre><code>void printPrice(
</code></pre>

<p>It's almost like, we're able to see through a paper cutout;
it just masks what we see. We see the actual thing we need through
the paper.</p>

<p>In summary, pure virtual functions let us:</p>

<ol>
<li><p>Avoid writing dummy logic</p></li>
<li><p>Force the programmer to implement derived class' functions.</p></li>
</ol>

<p>Oh, and remember, <em>virtual destructor</em> in the base class, <strong>always</strong>. </p>

<p>As before, we can do an ABC pointer or reference to
an instance of a derived class. </p>

<p>But we would not be able to point an ABC pointer to an ABC instance;
because we cannot create any instances of an ABC! This also means
<em>no arrays</em> of an ABC; but we can do an array of Animal <em>pointers</em>. </p>

<p>But we could do ABC pointers to ABC pointers. </p>

<p>There may be constructors for ABCs, but that doesn't mean
that they will not ABCs (we cannot have virtual constructors). </p>

<p>What if we had</p>

<p>Animal - ABC
Insect - ABC (derived from Animal)
Fly - regular
Ant - regular</p>

<pre><code>Fly f;
Ant a;

Animal* p;

p = &amp;a; // Point to 

p = &amp;f;
</code></pre>

<p>Can this be done? Yes. Animal is a superclass to Fly and Ant. 
But we cannot point subclass pointers to superclasses;
not all Animals are Ants. </p>

<p>We could also do:</p>

<pre><code>Insect* i; 

i = &amp;a; 
i = &amp;f; 
</code></pre>

<p>Ant and Fly are derived from Insect. </p>

<p>Subclass destructor will run, then superclass destructor
will run, automatically. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-challenge-diary-class" class="anchor" href="#challenge-diary-class" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Challenge: Diary Class</h3>

<p>// Assume that this class will be used in inheritance
// and that all these functions may be redefined. </p>

<pre><code>#include &lt;string&gt; 

class Diary
{
    public: 
        Diary(const std::string&amp; title) // PBCR
        {
            m_title = title;
        }
        virtual ~Diary()
        {
            // EMPTY
        }
        virtual std::string getTitle() const // Should be const!
        {
            return m_title;
        }
        virtual void writeEntry(const std::string&amp; entry) // Should be PBCR
        {
            m_entries += entry;
        }
        virtual std::string read() const // Should be const!
        {
            return m_entries; // Returns a COPY of m_entries

            // IF we did not want m_entires to be modified
            // then return by constant reference! 
        }

    private: 

        std::string m_title;
        std::string m_entries; 
};
</code></pre>

<p>Arvin got it; </p>

<p>"Do you want the Pop Tart, or what's in the bag?"</p>

<p>In that case, "Leggo my Eggo!" And he even gets syrup!</p>

<p>If the base class function is <code>const</code>, the redefined 
function must be const too!</p>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-challenge" class="anchor" href="#challenge" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Challenge:</h2>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-secret-diary-challenge" class="anchor" href="#secret-diary-challenge" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Secret Diary Challenge</h3>

<pre><code>class SecretDiary : public Diary
    : Diary("TOP-SECRET") // A SecretDiary IS a Diary! 
    // We need to initialize the superclass!
{
    public:

        SecretDiary()
        {
            // EMPTY
        }

        virtual ~SecretDiary()
        {
            // EMPTY
        }

        // Entries are part of the base class
        virtual void writeEntry(const string&amp; entry)
        {
            // DOESN'T WORK!
            // This would modify a copy of m_entries
            Diary::read() += encode(entry); 

            // This WOULD work if Diary's read()
            // returned a REFERENCE to m_entries

            // CANNOT base class private data
            // MUST use the base class public member functions!
            Diary::writeEntry(encode(entry));
        }

        virtual string read() const
        {
            return decode(Diary::read());
        }

    private:
        // DO NOT redefine data! 
        // DO NOT use protected data! 
        // Use PRIVATE data!
};
</code></pre>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-scrambled-diary-challenge" class="anchor" href="#scrambled-diary-challenge" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Scrambled Diary Challenge</h3>

<p>See challenge part 3 in the PowerPoint. </p>

<p>If read() is not virtual in Diary, then
we use Diary's version of read(), rather
than SecretDiary's read(), which uses decode()! </p>

<p>This is why we would get scrambled text! </p>

<a id="user-content-lecture-9-stacks--queues" class="anchor" href="#lecture-9-stacks--queues" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lecture 9: Stacks &amp; Queues</h2>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-lectures-10--11-3-february-2014--5-february-2014" class="anchor" href="#lectures-10--11-3-february-2014--5-february-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lectures 10 &amp; 11: 3 February 2014 &amp; 5 February 2014</h2>

first. Smallberg has a bunch of graduating EE seniors,
apparently. </p>

<p>WE MUST PRACTICE SOLVING RECURSION PROBLEMS!</p>

<p>It's time for what Joel Spolosky called
"the big guns" of coding interview questions:</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-recursion" class="anchor" href="#recursion" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Recursion</h3>

<p>Carey describes it as "one of the most difficult
topics in computer science", but it does let us
do all sorts of stuff. It's apparently his favorite
lecture of the quarter! </p>

<p>Stacks &amp; queues let us solve mazes and write
calculators that can process whole natural 
expressions. Linked lists underpinned stacks
and queues. </p>

<p>Inheritance and polymorphism let us avoid
code duplication and easily extend our existing
code to meet new needs. </p>

<p>And now, recursion will let us do stuff like 
play chess or checkers, solve Sudoku puzzles (in ~15 lines),
and crack codes or ciphers. It is a big, difficult
gun, but wieled well, we'll finally be able to do
all the cool stuff that pop culture promised us! </p>

<p>It'll also let us do mathy stuff
like factorials! </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-thinking-paradigm-behind-recursion" class="anchor" href="#thinking-paradigm-behind-recursion" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Thinking Paradigm Behind Recursion</h4>

<p>Previously, we did problem-solving in a function with 
breaking stuff down into smaller, constituent
problems, solving each of them with sub-functions, 
then gathering the solutions into one for the
overarching problem. </p>

<p>With recursion, we'll solve the sub-problems
by calling the larger function on itself! </p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-lazy-persons-sort" class="anchor" href="#lazy-persons-sort" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>"Lazy Person's Sort"</h5>

<p>We have a pile of index cards with numbers on them. 
We want to sort the cards. Solution: hand off half to
one person, half to another person, have them sort their
respective halves, the recombine the sorted halves. </p>

<p>Problem: too many cards. </p>

<p>Solution: each of the slaves/willing assistants calls
on two other people each themselves. It's a sort of pyramid:</p>

<pre><code>            A
        B       B
      C   C   C   C
     D D D D D D D D
           ...
</code></pre>

<p>Problem: if there is only card left, it cannot
be split up. </p>

<p>Solution: if handed only card, give it right back;
it's already "sorted". </p>

<p>This is known as <strong>merge sort</strong>. </p>

<p>We just saw the power of calling a function on itself
repeatedly to solve sub-problems that eventually solves
the whole problem. </p>

<p>Each broken-up sub-part may not be of the same
size. Just like how a Riemann Sum may not have
a uniform partition size. </p>

<p>So how do we get to the actual merging part
if we keep calling ourself? </p>

<p>Basically, we go down and down till we hit
the base case, then we return back up, merging and
merging little stuff. <em>Merge sort</em> is apparently
one of the most efficient algorithms known to humanity. </p>

<p>Our job with recursion: how can our function use itself
(on increasingly smaller subsets) to get the complete
problem solved? It must be able to work on the full set
or a subset. We must have faith like Indiana Jones crossing
that bridge! </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-the-two-rules-of-recursion" class="anchor" href="#the-two-rules-of-recursion" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>The Two Rules of Recursion</h4>

<p><strong>ONE: Every recursive function must have a stopping condition</strong>. </p>

<p>The stopping condition (base case): The recursive function
is able to solve the simplest, most basic instance of its
problem <em>without using recursion</em>. Instead, it runs once, 
then <em>stops calling itself</em>. (Example: base case for merge sort
is a single element). </p>

<p>Example: </p>

<pre><code>void eatTootsieRollPop(int layers)
{
</code></pre>

<p>// This is the stopping condition
// In the base case of zero or less, 
// we run once (more) and end! </p>

<pre><code>    if (layers &lt;= 0)
    {
        cout &lt;&lt; "We've hit the center!" &lt;&lt; endl;
        return; // Don't forget to end!
    }

    cout &lt;&lt; "Licking!" &lt;&lt; endl;

    eatTootsieRollPop(layers - 1); // Recursion! Calling itself!
}
</code></pre>

<p><em>Without a stopping condition</em>, a recursive function
would <em>run ad infinitum</em>, something we definitely do not
want to have happen. It'd be infinite Inception!</p>

<p>What happens if a recursive function actually does
go on forever? Well, the call stack would overflow
and we'll crash. </p>

<p><strong>TWO: Every recursive function must have a "simplifying step"</strong></p>

<p>Every time that the recursive function calls itself, 
a <em>smaller</em> sub-problem must be passed into, so that we
<em>eventually reach the stopping condition</em> and terminate. </p>

<p>Without this simplifying condition, we'll never reach
that stopping condition. </p>

<p>From previous example: <code>(layers - 1)</code> </p>

<p>// A <em>copied value</em> that is equal to <code>layers - 1</code>
// Layers is not actually modified; <code>layers--</code> would modify it</p>

<p>// PBR vs. PBR is case-dependent; modifying the counter
// would not always work [also, that counter would have
// to come from outside the function; we'd be recreating
// it every time otherwise]</p>

<p>Without that, we would never reach the stopping condition
of <code>if (layers &lt;= 0)</code></p>

<p>Most recursive functions meet this second rule
by <em>splitting their inputs into two</em> with each call, 
or by operating on an input <em>one smaller</em> in size than the last.</p>

<p>Is it better to divide many times? It depends on the problem. 
We'll be able to mathematically analyze this later on. </p>

<p><strong>TWO.5: Recursive functions must only use local variables &amp; parameters</strong></p>

<p>Using globals, statics or class member variables would
be an atrocious idea. This is not <em>always</em> wrong, but almost
all of the time, it is a bad idea. </p>

<p><em>NEVER use a loop</em> within a recursive function 
unless absolutely necessary. </p>

<p>The parameter-passing method is irrelevant (value, reference, pointer)</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-tracing-through-recursion" class="anchor" href="#tracing-through-recursion" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Tracing Through Recursion</h4>

<p>Tracing through a complex recursive function
can be confusing. To alleviate the headaches, 
we can pretend that each recursive call is a call
to a different function that just happens to have the same name. </p>

<p>This helps a lot with tracing the function returns. </p>

<p>On paper, this can be done by writing down 
all the variables and parameters, and then repeatedly folding
and unfolding the paper to avoid confusion over the returned values. </p>

<p><em>Distinguish between the different calls of the same recursive function</em></p>

<p>"How do you lick something with no layers? That could be misconstrued
as something inappropriate."</p>

<p>Does C++ actually copy the function? No, this is just for visualization. </p>

<p>The debugger would show us going into the call again and again;
the variables watcher would show us the current state with the current
call; Visual Studio will let us go up and down the stack to see the different
call states. I'll have to see if Xcode lets us do the same. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-wielding-recursion" class="anchor" href="#wielding-recursion" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Wielding Recursion</h4>

<p>Here is how to write a recursive function: </p>

<ol>
<li><p>Write the function signature</p></li>
<li><p>Show how to use the function</p></li>
<li><p>Add the base case code</p></li>
<li><p>Add the recursive function call</p></li>
<li><p>Write the function's completion logic</p></li>
<li><p>Validate the function</p></li>
</ol>

<p>Example: we want to calculate a factorial. </p>

<p>Step 1: Write the function header</p>

<ul>
<li><p>What arguments need to be passed to the function and how?</p></li>
<li><p>What should the function return? </p></li>
<li><p>If this is a member function, should the function be <code>const</code>?</p></li>
</ul>

<p>Our example will be <code>int factorial(int number)</code></p>

<p>Step 2: Show how to use our function</p>

<ul>
<li><p>Who calls this function, and from where? </p></li>
<li><p>How does it handle an input of size N vs size N - 1? </p></li>
</ul>

<p>Example calls: </p>

<pre><code>int threeItemMenuPossibilites = factorial(3);
int nowTwo = factorial(2);
</code></pre>

<p>In general:</p>

<pre><code>int var1 = factorial(n);    // Gives us n!
int var2 = facotiral(n - 1) // Gives us (n - 1)!
</code></pre>

<p>Step 3: Add the base case code</p>

<ul>
<li><p>Identify the simplest possible inputs</p></li>
<li><p>Add checks for these base cases (there may be multiple!)</p></li>
<li><p>Solve the problem <em>without using recursion</em></p></li>
<li><p>Always consider <em>all</em> possible base cases!</p></li>
</ul>

<p>In our example, 0! would be the base case. 
0!, by the way, is equal to 1. </p>

<pre><code>int factorial(int number)
{
    if (number == 0)
        return 1; 

    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>Step 4: Simplify the input &amp; call ourselves</p>

<ul>
<li><p>Split or modify the input as needed</p></li>
<li><p>Have the function call itself to solve the larger part</p></li>
</ul>

<p>We currently have:</p>

<pre><code>int factorial(int number)
{
    if (number == 0)
        return 1; 

    int N = number; 
    int minusOne = ... // Need (N - 1)!
}
</code></pre>

<p>Oh, right--recursion!</p>

<pre><code>int factorial(int number)
{
    if (number == 0)
        return 1; 

    int N = number; 
    int minusOne = factorial(number - 1);
}
</code></pre>

<p>This is our <em>simplifying code</em>, which here gets
us one closer to the base case with each recursive call. </p>

<p>Step 5: Write the completion logic</p>

<ul>
<li><p>Get the result from the recursive call</p></li>
<li><p>Further refine/process it, as needed</p></li>
<li><p>Return the finished product</p></li>
</ul>

<p>int factorial(int number)
{</p>

<pre><code>if (number == 0)
    return 1; 

int one = number; 
int minusOne = factorial(number - 1);

return (one * minusOne);
</code></pre>

<p>}</p>

<p>Here, we recursively go down the number line
from the input, then hit bottom, and bounce back
up on the multiplicative wave! </p>

<p>It goes: 5 to 4 to 3 to 2 to 1 to 0.</p>

<p>Then it goes 1 * 2 * 3 * 4 * 5!</p>

<p>Step 6: Validate our new function</p>

<ul>
<li><p>Validate the neonate function, <em>always</em></p></li>
<li><p>Try the simplest possible inputs first</p></li>
<li><p>Try increasingly complex/large inputs</p></li>
<li><p>After one recursive call check, we're usually good</p></li>
</ul>

<p>Alternative formulation of the steps (Carey really
does update the slides every year): </p>

<p>Step 1: Write the function header</p>

<ul>
<li><p>What will the function return</p></li>
<li><p>What do we need to pass to the function? </p></li>
</ul>

<p>For the factorial example, we pass
in an unsigned integer, and we return an
unsigned integer. </p>

<p>Step 2: Define the magic function</p>

<ul>
<li><p>Assume that David Smallberg created a 
magic function that always works that
does what we want to do </p></li>
<li><p>It takes the same parameters, and
return the same result-type</p></li>
<li><p>Restriction: It cannot work on problems 
of size N. It CAN work on problems of size
less than N</p></li>
<li><p>Show how to use this magic function to
solve problems of size elss than N</p></li>
</ul>

<p>Imagine we already have a magic function.
Show how we could use it. </p>

<p>Define a magic function, that has teh same paraemters 
but CANNOT wrok on a value of size N, so we can't do 
N! but we can do (N - 1)!</p>

<p>Step 3: Add the base case code</p>

<p>Determine the base case(s) and solve it (them)
<em>without recursion</em>. </p>

<ul>
<li><p>What is the simplest sub-problem(s) the function
can solve <em>without</em> recursion? </p></li>
<li><p>What inputs allow us to immediately return
some result without having the function call itself? </p></li>
<li><p>There may be <em>multiple base cases</em></p></li>
</ul>

<p>Here, 0! = 1. </p>

<p>Step 4: Solve the problem with the magic function</p>

<p>We can't use the magic function to do all the work,
so break the problem into smaller parts:</p>

<p>N! = N * (N - 1)!</p>

<p>A factorial can be redefined as the top number
times the factorial of the top - 1 number, e.g.
5! = 5 * 4! or 5! = 5 * (5 - 1)!</p>

<p>We already have N; no need for the magic function.</p>

<p>So we use David Smallberg's magic funcion on the 
(N - 1)! part.  </p>

<p>Then we combine the results and return it. </p>

<pre><code>int part1 = N; 
int part2 = magicFactorial(N - 1);

return part1 * part2; 
</code></pre>

<p>Step 5: Remove the magic</p>

<p>magicFactorial()
{
    factorial()
}</p>

<p>We just call ourselves!
The sub-problem(s) is/are solved
by our own function. We trust that our function
will work on problems of sizes smaller than N. </p>

<p>The problem: If our function calls itself,
what stops it from calling itself forever? </p>

<p>Answer: the base case code ensures that eventually
we stop calling ourselves. </p>

<p>Step 6: Validate the recursive function</p>

<p>ALWAYS try our new function on the simplest possible
input, then verify it with increasingly larger/more complex
inputs. VERIFY that our implementation works. Typically,
just one larger is enough to verify the function.</p>

<p>This lets us catch our mistakes! </p>

<p>Note that every recusrive call will create new copies
of the local parameters and variables. We use the call
stack for all this. Runaway recursive functions will
cause a stack overflow. </p>

<p>Note also how the recursive calls match up with
the factorial process: </p>

<p>3! = 3 * 2 * 1 </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-static-variables" class="anchor" href="#static-variables" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Static Variables</h4>

<pre><code>void foo()
{
    static int a; // Static primitive types are initialized to 0

    cout &lt;&lt; a;

    a = a = 1;
}
</code></pre>

<p>When we exit the function, the variable's lifetime is till
the end of the program; but its scope is limited to foo().</p>

<p>It has the lifetime, but not the scope of a global. </p>

<p>Try to avoid statics. They are one way to check how often a 
a function is called, but that's just as easily accomplished
with a counter variable (of a longer lifetime and large scope)
passed into the function (by reference or pointer).</p>

<p>Note also that <code>a</code> would not be re-initialized to 0
with each call to foo(). </p>

<p>A static class instance would have its default constructor 
called. Making recursion problems easier is possible
with globals or statics but they should never be necessary. 
Do it the real way; use the full power of recursion! </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-recursion-on-arrays" class="anchor" href="#recursion-on-arrays" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Recursion on Arrays</h3>

<p>// Carey is in a dress shirt and pants; 
// Symantec has a Top 150 people in the company
// meeting in San Francisco today, apparently. 
// Apple does the same thing, but with top ~100</p>

<p>Now, we'll use recursion to get the sum of all
items in an array. </p>

<ol>
<li>Function header</li>
</ol>

<p>We need the array and its size. </p>

<p><strong>Arrays may never be passed by reference</strong></p>

<pre><code>sumArray(int arr*, int n);
</code></pre>

<p>OR: </p>

<pre><code>sumArray(int arr[], int n); 
</code></pre>

<p>We return the sum of all the values. </p>

<pre><code>int sumArray(int arr*, int n);
</code></pre>

<ol>
<li>We have a magic function</li>
</ol>

<p>Note that Carey is just making this up
as a crutch to help us learn recursion.</p>

<p>Assume that the magic function does not
use recursion.  </p>

<p>It looks like this: </p>

<pre><code>int magicSumArray(int arr*, int n);
</code></pre>

<p>Where n is &lt; N. Smallberg put a booby trap in it! </p>

<p>To sum up smaller arrays:</p>

<pre><code>magicSumArray(arr, n - 1); 
</code></pre>

<p>To get last n - 1 elements: <code>(arr + 1, n - 1)</code></p>

<p>This gets us the element at index 1, the second
element of an array that starts its indexing at 0. 
Remember that arr + 1 gets us to the next element,
moving by the type size, not the number of bytes. </p>

<p>To get the first half: <code>(arr, n/2)</code></p>

<p>To get the last half: <code>(arr + n/2, n - n/2)</code></p>

<p>--&gt; Advance to middle half, then iterate
over the remaining items, which means there are
n - n/2 items remaining (this also accounts for
if there are an odd number of elements in the array). </p>

<ol>
<li>Determine base cases</li>
</ol>

<p>What's the base case here? An empty array,
which has a sum of 0. </p>

<p>If there is only one element, then the sum is
just that the one element, the arr[0]. </p>

<p>It is better to have have extra base cases for 
safety, then to not have them. We can always
remove redundancies later on. </p>

<ol>
<li>Use magic</li>
</ol>

<p>We need to break down our problem into smaller
sub-problems. </p>

<p>We can go </p>

<ul>
<li>Front-to-back</li>
</ul>

<p>We process the first N -1 elements, <em>ignoring</em>
the last element. Then we get the last element 
separately, and add up the two quantities. </p>

<pre><code>int front = magicSumArray(arr, n - 1);
int back = arr[n - 1];

return front + back;
</code></pre>

<p>Or </p>

<pre><code>return front + a[n -1]
</code></pre>

<ul>
<li>Back-to-front </li>
</ul>

<p>We process the last n - 1 elements
and ignore the first element. Then later
add the first element. </p>

<pre><code>int rear = magicSumArray(arr + 1, n - 1);
return a[0] + rear; 
</code></pre>

<ul>
<li>Divide and conquer</li>
</ul>

<p>We process the first half, the second half,
and then combine them: </p>

<pre><code>int first = magicSumArray(arr, n/2);
int second = magicSumArray(arr + n/2, n - n/2);

return first + second; 
</code></pre>

<p>Now which one is faster for processing
arrays? Here, all three need to visit all N elements,
(we need to sum them up), so there is no advantage
to any of them, at least here. </p>

<ol>
<li>Remove the magic </li>
</ol>

<p>magicSumArray() really just calls our sumArray(). </p>

<p>We're calling ourselves. </p>

<p>David: "Surprise!" </p>

<ol>
<li>Verify our function </li>
</ol>

<p>Here, if we do front-to-back or
back-to-front, the second check for n == 1
would not be needed. </p>

<p>If we do divide-and-conquer, the n == 1
check would be needed. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-challenge-1" class="anchor" href="#challenge-1" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Challenge</h4>

<pre><code>void printArray(int arr*, int size)
{
    if (size == 0)
        return;

    // IF using divide-and-conquer
    if (size == 1)
        cout &lt;&lt; arr[0] &lt;&lt; endl;

        // If using back-to-front

    // We print the first element
    cout &lt;&lt; arr[0] &lt;&lt; endl;

    // We print the remaining elements
    printArray(arr + 1, size - 1);
}
</code></pre>

<p>John Thompson, new Chairman of the Board
at Microsoft (named yesterday), used to 
be a CEO at Symantec. Carey knows him...</p>

<p>Update to print from bottom to top</p>

<pre><code>void printArray(int arr*, int size)
{

    if (size == 0)
        return;

    // IF using divide-and-conquer
    if (size == 1)
        cout &lt;&lt; arr[size] &lt;&lt; endl;

        // If using back-to-front

    // We print the remaining elements
    printArray(arr + 1, size - 1);

    // We print the first element
    cout &lt;&lt; arr[0] &lt;&lt; endl;
}
</code></pre>

<p>Yes, this does print ou t in reverse,
this is because we are recursive here! </p>

<p>It hits an empty, then propagates backwards. </p>

<p>Each call of the company of the recursive
function sees a different version of the 
array. </p>

<p>Note that the last call passes in a location
in memory past the end of the array, but our 
base case code accounts for the zero case,
so we just return. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-recursion-on-a-linked-list" class="anchor" href="#recursion-on-a-linked-list" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Recursion on a Linked List</h3>

<p>Typically we just go down a linked list. </p>

<p>We'll pass a pointer to a node (could be
a dummy node, or follow the head pointer to the
first node). </p>

<p>We don't need a size value; we can just determine
based on the next pointer. </p>

<p>If it's a circular linked list, we need a special
case check for if we've hit first node again. </p>

<p>On an exam, <em>never assume</em> that an array or a linked
list is not empty. </p>

<p>Function that finds the biggest item in a linked list. </p>

<pre><code>struct Node
{
    int m_val; 
    Node* m_next
};

int biggest(Node* cur); 
</code></pre>

<p>Note that it's hard to know where the middle
of the link list,  unless we maintained a pointer
to it. </p>

<p>We need to act on a subset of the list. </p>

<p>So we can use the array strategies, acting 
on the last N - 1 items, or first N - 1 items,
then checking against the value of the first node
or the last node. </p>

<pre><code>// It's probably gonna be the head pointer, 
// but we could find the biggest part of just
// part of the list 
int biggest(Node* cur)
{
    // [ERROR]: Empty linked list!
    if (cur == nullptr)
    {
        return -1;
    }

    // Only one node in linked list
    if (cur-&gt;next == nullptr)
    {
        return cur-&gt;m_val;
    }

    // The value of the first node
    int candidate1 = cur-&gt;m_val;

    // Otherwise, find biggest in the
    // rest of the list
    int candidate2 = biggest(cur-&gt;next);

    int winner = (candidate1 &gt;= candidate2) ? candidate1 : candidate2;

    return winner;

    // OR: return (candidate1 &gt;= candidate2) ? candidate1 : candidate2;
}
</code></pre>

<p>When we get to the bottom, we compare one item to the n - 1
item, then we compare that winnner against the next value,
and we take that winner and compare against the next value,
and eventually we'll have the biggest value in the whole list.</p>

<p>This is like a rock-paper-scissors tournament, modified
so that it runs linearly (rather than concurrently/in parallel),
and propagates downward to two people (we're creating pairs)
and then we compete back up the pairs (we're assuming ability
is absolute, i.e. no luck, and re-running the tournament
would deterministically give the same result, as here when
we compare numbers). </p>

<p>Carey did a "Biggest gets head, lucky biggest";
he apparently wanted to do this during the linked
list lecture, but Kim was delivering that one, so he
decided that it would have been inappropriate, haha. </p>

<p><strong>We need to do the challenges on our own.</strong></p>

<p>On the earliest position challenge, consider 
passing one of the parameters by reference or pointer. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-be-careful-with-recursion" class="anchor" href="#be-careful-with-recursion" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Be Careful with Recursion</h3>

<p>Recursion is a memory hog. Why? 
Because we're constantly pushing the function's 
variables onto the stack for each call.</p>

<p>There is only copy of the function in the 
machine code, but we do have multiple copies 
of its variables.  </p>

<p>So, if possible, do a problem without recursion,
unless we're just doing it for practice. </p>

<p>If we do N - 1 for a sub-problem, then we'll end up
with N copies of all the variables and we'll eventually
cause a stack overflow. </p>

<p>If we can do stuff with a for-loop, do not do 
it with recursion. Recursion is used on the difficult stuff. 
Don't use it unless our recursive calls don't go too deep. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-binary-search" class="anchor" href="#binary-search" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Binary Search</h3>

<pre><code>search(...)
{
    if (top &gt; bottom)
    {
        // Our two ends have crossed; nothing found
        return -1; 
    }

    IF no words
        DONE

    ELSE IF middleWord == findWord
        RETURN - DONE

    ELSE
    {
        middleWorld = (top + bot) / 2
        IF (findWord == middleWord) // FOUND it!
            RETURN - DONE
        ELSE IF (findWord &lt; middleWord)
            search(first half [exclude the non-matching middle])

        ELSE IF (findWord &gt; middleWord)
            search(second half [exclude the non-matching middle])
    }
}
</code></pre>

<p>To exclude the non-matching middle, just shift the index
or pointer, up/down, as needed. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-recursion-binary-search" class="anchor" href="#recursion-binary-search" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Recursion Binary Search</h3>

<p>Simplify a recursive function for user use
by creating helper functions. Determine what we need
from the parameters passed to the <strong>simple interface</strong>,
a <strong>cover function</strong>. The helper function will actually
do the dirty work. The cover might just call the helper, 
passing the processed parameters. </p>

<p>Some problems will need a lot of different parameters. 
To make things easier, just abstract away! </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-solving-mazes-with-recursion" class="anchor" href="#solving-mazes-with-recursion" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Solving Mazes with Recursion</h3>

<p>Solving a maze with recursion is very similar
to a stack-based solver, as it too uses a stack! </p>

<p>It will be a depth-first search that uses backtracking. </p>

<p>// He first taught this class at 28
// He had hair then, and could blend in the first row
// Stands up, "I'm the professor!" Haha</p>

<p>How have we simplified the problem with this recusive call? 
We stuck bread-crumbs everywhere! We'll never repeatedly check
a location; we're eliminating possibilties in the maze 
as we explore it. We'll be looking a maze with a 
N - 1 open slots each time; we'll either have a solution 
or no solution! </p>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-lecture-12-10-february-2014" class="anchor" href="#lecture-12-10-february-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lecture 12: 10 February 2014</h2>

<p>// Quick hint on #4 of Homework 3</p>

<p>Say we have:</p>

<p>5 10 2 9 0 7 16</p>

<p>We're gonna split using the first number.</p>

<p>We end up with:</p>

<p>2 0 1 5 10 9 7 6</p>

<p>Now, we want smallest to largest (opposite of homework). </p>

<p>We want stuff to the left and to the right to go
in ascending order. Here, 5 is already in the right
spot, thanks to split()! </p>

<p>So, we want to call order() on the subset (on either
side of the 5, using split(), and we'll order() with 
split(). </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-review-recursion" class="anchor" href="#review-recursion" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Review: Recursion</h3>

<hr>

<p><strong>Klinger story</strong>: small graduate computer vision class. </p>

<p>Professor always brought in food like oranges, and hard-boiled
eggs. The professor asked a  question, and some guy gave a response:
"The purple pyramid!" (he normally said nonsense). </p>

<p>The professor: "That's absolutely right!"</p>

<p>Leonardo made a funny face, and he vibrated a table with a tray
of pastries. The tray fell over and sent pastries flying.</p>

<p>"The pastries are flying!" and he ran out of the class. </p>

<p>He still got an A- in the class. </p>

<hr>

<p>In the maze example, we had an <strong>implicit base case</strong>, where we hit
the end of the function after propagating back up, and simply 
hit the end of the function without doing anything. </p>

<p>That allowed for <strong>backtracking</strong> to the last valid good
exploratory position. Cool animation in the PowerPoint!</p>

<p>Notice that it when it hits the solution, it will keep on
looking and explore <em>every</em> possibility in the array, 
despite having the solution.</p>

<p>Look up the <em>Minimax algorithm</em> (minimize/maximize) 
is a recursive algorithm for creating an intelligent computer
AI opponent. It picks the best move for the computer by 
silently trying each legal move and seeing how the game woud
have turned out. It simulates an ideal human player during
this simulation. It examines a big tree of possibilities
against possibilties. </p>

<p>We can tell the algorithm how far ahead we want it to
check (e.g. 3 levels deep). </p>

<p>At the base case level, do a <em>static evaluation</em>, and 
see who's winning, black or white, e.g. with a number, +/-. </p>

<p>Otherwise, repeatedly (every move computer can make)&lt;
then it temporarily changes the board and checks
how a human would respond (What's the worst a human could?)
, then it returns the best move possibility for the computer. </p>

<p>A findBestHumanMove() is very similar to findBestCompMove();
they <strong>co-recur</strong>. This is <strong>co-recursion</strong>. </p>

<p>The computer will remember all the resulting scores
from the possible combination of moves. </p>

<p>So, the difficulty of the computer opponent will be
determined (primarily) by how far ahead it looks and
analyzes. </p>

<p>The best chess programs don't go deep uniformly; 
that takes too much time and too much computational power.
Instead, they throw away branches that make no sense
(e.g. it's obvious this is a good or bad path). The algo
will iteratively go deeper or shallower as needed. 
Then, they'll</p>

<p>Tic-Tac-Toe can be solved perfectly (we can build
an AI that will always win or tie). There's only 9
squares. Chess is too complex to evaluate everything. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-generic-programming" class="anchor" href="#generic-programming" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Generic Programming</h3>

<p>The war on code and functionality duplication. 
We keep learning more ways to make our programs
highly extensible and adaptive to our needs. </p>

<p><strong>Generic programming</strong> means we'll be able to
build stuff (especially algorithms and data structures) 
that can work on many kinds of data, e.g. a linked list
that can serve as a container for Students as well as
ints, etc. Similarly, we want a sort() that can sort
many different things.</p>

<p>This is <strong>code reuse</strong>. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-custom-comparison-operators" class="anchor" href="#custom-comparison-operators" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Custom Comparison Operators</h3>

<p>First, know that we can overload operators like
<code>==</code>, <code>&lt;</code>, <code>&gt;</code>, <code>&lt;=</code>, and <code>&gt;=</code> just like we did 
the assignment operator <code>==</code>. </p>

<p>To overload within a class:</p>

<pre><code>class SomeClass 
{
    public:

        ...
        bool operator&lt;(const SomeClass&amp; other)
        {
            if ( // Compare data members, etc. ) 
                return true;
            else
                return false;
        }

    private:

        ...
};
</code></pre>

<p>Would this work with derived classes? This would compare
only the Dog part, and not the GoldenRetriever() part. </p>

<p>As before, this can happen outside of the class too: </p>

<p>// Note that outside the class, we'll need two operands
// Inside the class, the calling object is treated as the first
// operand; note also that operator overloading requires that
// at least one of the operands be of a class type (though
// we can also have primitives passed in parameters, e.g. compare
// our custom Number class to an int)</p>

<p>// ORDER MATTERS; we have to do Dog vs. Cat, and a separate
// Cat vs. Dog</p>

<p>// We can also compare class types against other class types;
// This would be </p>

<p>// In general, PBCR</p>

<pre><code>bool operator&lt;(const SomeClass&amp; other, const SomeClass&amp; another)
{
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>// NOTE: If the operator is overloaded outside of the class,
// its implementation will ONLY be able to use the class' public
// members</p>

<p>// ALSO: We cannot have more operands than the built-in
// operators expect. </p>

<p>It may be helpful to recall that <code>fido &lt;= spot</code>
is equivalent to <code>fido.operator=(spot)</code></p>

<p><strong>There is NO default comparison operator</strong> for programmer-defined
class types.</p>

<p>This is <em>static binding</em>, meaning we know at compile-time what
sort of variable we have. <em>Dynamic binding</em> means we have to figure
out at run-time what sort of variable we're actually dealing with,
as with polymorphism. </p>

<p>Generic programming is <em>powerful</em>!  With it, we
can write programs far more quickly; it's a great force
multiplier. This is a common paradigm among modern languages.</p>

<p>Here, we get to avoid writing special code for comparing
different objects (which also avoids accidental comparison
using different standards, or comparing using different data members). </p>

<p>Finally: if the objects passed into the overloaded operator are <code>const</code>,
then any member functions we call better be <code>const</code> too. </p>

<p>Prime example: cout's <code>&lt;&lt;</code> is an overloaded operator. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-templates" class="anchor" href="#templates" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Templates</h3>

<p>Previously, we sort of did generic functions with <code>typedef</code>. 
It sucks though, because we're constantly having to change
the typedef declaration whenever we want to change the data type
we use. </p>

<ol>
<li>Declare the template type</li>
<li>Use that type throughout the generic function</li>
</ol>

<p>To have a swap() function that works with any data type,
we can do: </p>

<pre><code>template&lt;typename Blah&gt;
void swap(Blah a, Blah b)
{
    // This assumes that the assignment operator is correctly defined
    Blah temp;
    temp = a; 
    a = b;
    b = temp;
}
</code></pre>

<p>Note that  will only apply to the immediately
following function or class. </p>

<p>We can also write <code>template&lt;class Blah&gt;</code></p>

<p>With this current definition, we expect swap()'s arguments
to be of the same type. Now, it can be any type, but the arguments
must be of the same type. </p>

<p>We <code>typename</code> or <code>class</code> to be succeeded by a generic identifier.</p>

<p>This swap() would work on two arguments of the same subclass;
because they're just items of the same type! We could use a superclass'
assignment operator if we wanted to. </p>

<p>QUESTION:</p>

<p>What if we had <code>template&lt;class Blah&gt;</code> and created a class Blah?
Would that be allowed? I'll try and find out! </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-template-implementation-details" class="anchor" href="#template-implementation-details" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Template Implementation Details</h4>

<p>The entire templated function is put in a header file. 
It CANNOT be split between a header and source file,
e.g. what we did before with a prototype and definition.</p>

<p>At compile-time, the compiler will generate a new version (machine code)
of the function for each new variable type. The compiled
executable will grow in size (little different from if we had
written many different function versions for, but the source 
file would be smaller).</p>

<p>Templates are time-saving and bug-reducing and source-simplifying! </p>

<p>NOTE: We must use the template data type for <em>at least one parameter</em>
or we'll have an error (i.e. we cannot use it for just a return-type). </p>

<p>When we use a templated function that expects two or more parameters
of the same name, e.g. <code>max(Blah x, Blah y)</code>, then we must pass in
two arguments to max() that are of the same type, e.g. two Cats,
two Dogs, but NOT an int and a float. </p>

<p>QUESTION: Could we define a templated comparison operator? 
Would that be a good idea? It seems redundant though. </p>

<p>We can override a templated function with a specialized function
specific to a class type. We'll use the most specialized version
(e.g. one that compares additional data members of a subclass). </p>

<p>Note that any operators we use inside of the generic functions
must be properly overloaded. To speed up that implementation work,
we could use operators within operator overloads, e.g. using
<code>==</code> and <code>&lt;</code> inside of the overload for <code>&lt;=</code></p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-multi-type-templates" class="anchor" href="#multi-type-templates" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Multi-Type Templates</h4>

<p>We can have </p>

<pre><code>template&lt;typename Type1, typename Type2&gt;
void functionName(Type 1 first, Type 2 second)
{
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-generic-classes" class="anchor" href="#generic-classes" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Generic Classes</h4>

<p>We can have generic classes too, ones that can hold any sort
of data. </p>

<p>AGAIN: Everything goes into a header file</p>

<pre><code>template&lt;typename Item&gt;
class SomeClass
{

    public:
        void setVal(Item a)
        {
            m_a = a;
        }

        // Don't redefine the ints here;
        // we don't need to!
        void print()
        {
            for (int i = 0; i &lt; 10; i++)
                cout &lt;&lt; m_a;
        }

    private:

        Item m_a;
};
</code></pre>

<p>Note that we would have to redefine how <code>cout</code>
works for class types. </p>

<p>NOTE: In a class, we do NOT need to use the template data type.
That would be lame to not use it though. Double-check this. </p>

<p>Note that we can only overload the primitive operators;
we cannot create new operators. </p>

<p>To create an instance of a generic class:</p>

<pre><code>SomeClass&lt;typeWeWantToUse&gt; instanceOfGenericClass;

ClassName&lt;int&gt; object1;
</code></pre>

<p>To define member functions externally, note that we use:</p>

<pre><code>template&lt;class Blah&gt;
class Foo
{
    ...
    void bar(Blah a);
    ...
};


template&lt;class Blah&gt;
void Foo&lt;Blah&gt;::bar(Blah a)
{
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p> goes between <code>ClassName</code> and <code>::</code></p>

<p>Check syntax for multi-type templates?</p>

<pre><code>void Foo&lt;Blah, Item&gt;::function(Blah a, Item b)
{
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>Again, this is all in the header file; we're just writing the 
bodies outside of the class definition. If it's inside the class
definition, we don't need to write <code>inline</code> (but if we didn't intend
to inline, define them outside of the class. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-inline-methods" class="anchor" href="#inline-methods" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Inline Methods</h3>

<p>Inline methods are a request to the compiler (compilers
do not have to honor it) to directly embed a function's
call with its body logic (replace the call and jump
with the machine code definition of the function).</p>

<p>Functions declared <em>and defined</em> inside of a class are implicitly
inline. To do so outside of the class: </p>

<pre><code>inline void Foo&lt;Blah&gt;::functionName(Blah b) const
{
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>Again, if this is a templated function, this is ALL in
the header file (we're just defining the function outside of
the class definition, but it's ALL in the header file). </p>

<p>This is some really ugly syntax...</p>

<p>This is faster. Why would we avoid inlining everything? 
We'd be copying that code and duplicating all over the place,
creating a really large binary, and we'll hit diminishing returns
as we use up memory. </p>

<p>BUT: This heavily slows down compilation.</p>

<p>Inlining is a trade-off between compilation time and execution time.</p>

<p>// Carey struggling with his laser-pointer/clicker
// Its battery died. His personal idiosyncrasy is to have
// a working laser. "Class is over!" So funny, haha. </p>

<p>"I have never looked at the real Stack class, because
I have a life."</p>

<p>Hilarious, haha. </p>

<p>Templated classes allow us to build powerful linked lists and
other data structures. Now, our data structures can hold any sort
of data. (Make sure our templated classes work perfectly; then,
we'll never have to rewrite data structure classes)</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-stl" class="anchor" href="#stl" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>STL</h3>

<p>The STL is a set of pre-written (and tested!) classes,
all implemented with templates. </p>

<p>Boom! Time-saver</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-vector" class="anchor" href="#vector" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Vector</h4>

<p>A  is a dynamically-size array. It does NOT have a 
fixed size. They grow/shrink auto-magically/</p>

<pre><code>#include &lt;vector&gt;

using std::vector;

    // To create an empty vector (0 elements)
    vector&lt;string&gt; myFirstVector;

    // OR:
    std::vector&lt;std::string&gt; myFirstVector;
</code></pre>

<p>This requires that the  has a default constructor!
No different from when we created arrays of stuff. If a class
required parameter(s) to its constructor, then we might
have a vector of pointers to instances of that class instead. </p>

<p><strong>All of a vector's initial elements are automatically initialized</strong>.
(using the default constructor)&gt;</p>

<p>All of its items are contiguous in memory. A vector basically 
dynamically allocates an array. When we hit the end, the 
class allocates a new, larger dynamic array, copies over the old
stuff, and deletes the old one. This is a big reason why 
vectors are slower. </p>

<p>Note that if the vector doubles in size when 
it fills up, then the capacity will quickly (out)grow the need.<br>
The vector-growth would be implementation-dependent. Who
knows what the STL does, haha. </p>

<p>Note that an array of a fixed size will always be faster
than a vector. This trade</p>

<p>"I'm a little edgy right now, because I don't have my laser." </p>

<p>Stack and queue are <em>container</em> classes</p>

<p>If we pass a second parameter:</p>

<pre><code>vector&lt;int&gt; vals(3, 4);
</code></pre>

<p>This will create a vector of 3 ints with initial value 4. </p>

<p>We could also do:</p>

<pre><code>vector&lt;Dog&gt; myPuppyFamily(3, "Fido");
</code></pre>

<p>What sort of constructors are needed here? Is a default
constructor still needed? </p>

<p>Vector member functions:</p>

<pre><code>// To add a new item to the end of the vector:

push_back()
</code></pre>

<p>There is NO push_front() function for vectors. </p>

<pre><code>// To read an existing item, use brackets, 
// just like with arrays
// '[]' is now an overloaded operator!

vals[number]
</code></pre>

<p>Bracks can only access existing items; if we go
out-of-bounds, we'll get memory access errors (just
as with arrays; because again, the underlying implementation
is a dynamically-allocated array). </p>

<pre><code>// To read/write the front or back elements
// of the vector:

front()
back()

// To remove the back item of the vector:
pop_back()
</code></pre>

<p>This means that if order matters for us, we need 
to be careful in how we push items to the vector
such that if we need to delete something, we have
that item at the end (we could write an exchange function
that swaps two values inside of the vector). </p>

<p>QUESTION: What parameters would that exchange function take?</p>

<pre><code>// To get the size of the vector (NOT the size of the
// underlying array)
size()
</code></pre>

<p>Keep in mind that ONLY vectors have a <code>.size()</code>,
not arrays</p>

<pre><code>// To know if the vector is empty
empty()
</code></pre>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-summary-of-" class="anchor" href="#summary-of-" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Summary of </h4>

<pre><code>    // To add a new item to the end of the vector:

push_back()

    // To read an existing item, use brackets, 
    // just like with arrays
    // '[]' is now an overloaded operator!

vals[number]

    // To read/write the front or back elements
    // of the vector:

front()
back()

    // To remove the back item of the vector:

pop_back()

    // To get the size of the vector (NOT the size of the
    // underlying array)

size()

    // To know if the vector is empty

empty()
</code></pre>

<p>ANSWER: Why must a templated class be completely in a 
header file?</p>

<p>Because C++ will create specialized versions for every 
time that the templated class is needed. If we have stuff
split up, that would have prevented the compiler from knowing
where the needed templated class is. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-summary-of--1" class="anchor" href="#summary-of--1" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Summary of </h4>

<p>// These notes were taken 12 February 2014, to continue
// from the previous lecture on generic programming
// and STL </p>

<p>STL's  is a linked list for
us to use. It can be a list of anything,
so long as that type has a default constructor. </p>

<p>We can do:</p>

<pre><code>push_back()
pop_back()
front()
back()
size()
empty()
</code></pre>

<p>AND:</p>

<pre><code>push_front()
pop_front()
</code></pre>

<p>Also: Brackets are NOT usable with 
Only usable with  or arrays. </p>

<p>So these are easy for insertion at head or
tail. </p>

<p>When do we use  vs a ? </p>

<p>Well, what if need to add stuff into
the middle? The linked list would be better
for inserting middle stuff, but the vector
would be better for <em>random access</em> throughout. </p>

<p>This comes from the implementation details. </p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-list-iteration" class="anchor" href="#list-iteration" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>List Iteration</h5>

<p>How do we iterate through a list?</p>

<p>Unlike a vector, we don't have <code>[]</code> brackets, 
so we need another way of going through a  </p>

<p><em>None</em> of the other STL containers have an easy
way to go through themselves. </p>

<p>Say we have (works for lists, etc., etc stacks &amp; quques)</p>

<p>We'll use an iterator variable and is like a pointer
but it used only for STL containers. Typically have it
start pointing it at some item, then increment and decrement
to go up/down the container. The iterator can also
<em>read/write</em> the item it points to. </p>

<pre><code>vector&lt;int&gt; myVec; 

// This is an iterator
// specific to a vector 
// of ints

vector&lt;int&gt;::iterator it;

// Here, we create an iterator (scope resolved
// and clarified as being an iterator for a
// vector of ints) named it

// container&lt;data_type&gt;::iterator _name
</code></pre>

<p>To use it:</p>

<pre><code>// To point at first item of container

it = myVec.begin(); 
</code></pre>

<p>// CANNOT do </p>

<pre><code>it = myVec.front();
</code></pre>

<p>front() gives a value; begin() gives the location </p>

<p>The iterator can point it at any other vector of ints here. 
It's like a pointer. </p>

<p>Once the iterator points at a item, we can access
the item's value, we can use:</p>

<pre><code>// '*' operator has been overloaded

*it;
</code></pre>

<p>Here, we're dereferencing an iterator. </p>

<p>To move down one item: </p>

<pre><code>it++; 
</code></pre>

<p>'++' has also been overloaded to get us to the next item. 
With a  we can do <code>+=</code>. </p>

<p>There are no safeguards for ++ here, it will let us
walk off the list if we're not careful. An iterator
is actually its own class associated here with . </p>

<p>To point to the last item in the container? 
end() exists but points <em>just past</em> the last item
in the container. This will let us go through
everything <em>including</em> the last item, letting
us do stuff with the last item. </p>

<p>To point to the last item: </p>

<pre><code>it = myVec.end();

it--; // Decrement once to last item
      // Assumes list empty 
</code></pre>

<p>This lets us loop easily: </p>

<pre><code>while (it != myVec.end())
{
    cout &lt;&lt; *it &lt;&lt; endl; 

    // This assumes we've defined how
    // cout should handle our items in the list

    it++; 
}
</code></pre>

<p>end() could be pointing to a dummy node, for example. </p>

<p>If we have an STL container of another class-type,
we'll need </p>

<pre><code>(*iterator).method(); 
</code></pre>

<p>Why? Because precedence(*) &gt; precedence(.)
So we can use the <code>-&gt;</code> operator as well. </p>

<p>The <code>*</code> and <code>-&gt;</code> operators have been overloaded. 
Iterators can be treated like pointers, but it is
not possible to do something like <code>begin() - end()</code></p>

<p>For EVERY STL container, the data-type held in
the container must have a default constructor. </p>

<p>Can we have derived classes of CSProfessor
and MathProfessor derived from Person. Can we then 
have a  of Persons and then have those items
set equal to a Professor? No, because polymorphism 
is only usable with pointers or references. We could
have a  of Person pointers. </p>

<p>E.g.: </p>

<pre><code>class Base
{
    ...
};

class Derived1 : public Base
{
    ...
};

class Derived2 : public Base
{
    ...
};

list&lt;Base&gt; x; 

Derived1 y;
</code></pre>

<p>Can we do: </p>

<pre><code>x.push_back(y); 
</code></pre>

<p>This would compile but it is not something
we should do. Only put stuff that belongs
into a . </p>

<p>But we do: </p>

<pre><code>list&lt;Base*&gt; x; // List of Base pointers

Derived1 y; 

x.push_back(&amp;y); // ADDRESS-OF y 
</code></pre>

<p>For iterators, we can compare equality
if they point into the same container. Only
 allows for comparing vector's &lt; &gt;, etc. </p>

<p>Try overloading the <code>*</code> operator for our own 
 class. </p>

<p>For a , use an iterator because we wanted
to insert something in the middle. </p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-const-iterators" class="anchor" href="#const-iterators" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Const Iterators</h5>

<p>IF we pass in a container by constant reference,
we CANNOT use a regular iterator. The regular
iterator has the ability to modify the item
it points to. Instead, we must use a <em>const iterator</em>.
We'll see: <code>cannot convert...const_iterator...iterator</code></p>

<pre><code>void tickleNerds(const list&lt;string&gt;&amp; nerds)
{
    list&lt;string&gt;::const_iterator it; 
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>Const iterators let us iterate through stuff,
but we won't be able to modify the stuff its points at.
This enforces the const promise we made.  </p>

<p>begin() returns an iterator to the first item;
end() returns an iterator to a place right below
the last item.</p>

<p>front() and back() return the first and last values.</p>

<p>What is an iterator? It is an object that
knows what it points to and how to go forward or backward.</p>

<p>// Amazing how much Carey's glasses and baldness are reminiscent
// of Steve Jobs</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-summary-of--2" class="anchor" href="#summary-of--2" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Summary of </h4>

<p> lets us associate two related values,
just like what we did with our previous homework. </p>

<p>This one works likke:</p>

<pre><code>map&lt;string,int&gt; nameToPhone; 

nameToPhone["Carey"] = 325239834;
</code></pre>

<p>Note that a map can only associate in a single
direction, e.g. string --&gt; int, not string &lt;--&gt; int</p>

<p>To go the reverse direction, we would need
a separate .</p>

<p>To access a specific element at some position,
use an iterator. </p>

<p>It works by creating a struct (e.g. Pair)
that stores two items, of the types specified
via template when we created the Map.</p>

<p>To map something to many things: </p>

<pre><code>map&lt;string, vector&lt;int&gt; &gt; myMap; 
</code></pre>

<p>To find a previously-added association,
use an iterator. </p>

<pre><code>map&lt;string,int&gt;::iterator it; 
</code></pre>

<p>Then call the find() function; find() can
only by used to find by the first item:</p>

<pre><code>it = nameToAge.find("Carey"); 
// Binary search tree is used
</code></pre>

<p>We can also look at the Pair of values
in the item in the Map pointers to by
the iterator:</p>

<pre><code>it-&gt;first;
it-&gt;second; 
</code></pre>

<p>What if the looked-for item doesn't exist?
It returns an iterator that points to end().</p>

<p>A Map can only map from a single item, e.g. 
the first item cannot be a vector, but the second
item can be. </p>

<pre><code>if (it == nameToAge.end())
    cout &lt;&lt; "Not found!\n"; 
</code></pre>

<p>To iterate through: </p>

<pre><code>for (it = myMap.begin; it != myMap.end(); it++)
{
    ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>Maps always maintain their items in alphabetical 
order and are sorted automatically by the first
parameter, i.e. by the first parameter to map,
and the map will be sorted by blah, e.g. by strings. </p>

<p>Note that we <em>must define an operator <code>&lt;</code> for the left-hand</em>
class/struct! Why? Because maps automatically sort 
stuff that we add to them. If we do not do this,
we will get a syntax error. </p>

<p>Within the map, we compare new Pairs via the first
parameter. How does this all work using just the &lt; operator? </p>

<p>To see if they're equal: !(a &lt; b) &amp;&amp; !(b &lt; a)</p>

<p>The map will is thus able to determine &gt; and ==. </p>

<p>Why do we use Maps? It allows for more encapsulation, 
for associating stuff on the fly, etc. We could always do 
class composition, but that takes a lot of work. </p>

<p>The first parameter to a  MUST be a single item,
but that single item could contain other data structures,
including vectors, etc. We would just need to more work with
the &lt; operator.</p>

<p>If we do something like <code>map&lt;int, Stud&gt;</code>, then Stud
no longer needs a &lt; operator here. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-summary-of--3" class="anchor" href="#summary-of--3" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Summary of </h4>

<p>Create a set: </p>

<pre><code>set&lt;data_type&gt; mySet; 
</code></pre>

<p>A set keeps a set of unique items
and ignores duplicates when inserting. </p>

<pre><code>insert()
erase()
size()
</code></pre>

<p>Are the items ordered? Yes, they are ordered
smaller to bigger, and we would <em>need an &lt; operator</em>
for an item held in a . This allows for ordering,
needed for binary search. Sets are binary search trees. </p>

<p>We can have sets of other data types. </p>

<p>To search through a set: </p>

<pre><code>set&lt;data_type&gt;::iterator it; 

it = mySet.find(...);

if (it == mySet.end()) // Sentinel value
{
    cout &lt;&lt; "Not found!" &lt;&lt; endl;
}
</code></pre>

<p>All items will be alphabetically ordered. </p>

<p>To erase, we can pass in an iterator;
this lets us delete specific items.</p>

<pre><code>it = mySet.find("Doomed"); 

mySet.erase(it); 

// The iterator is now INVALID
// Don't dereference it! 
</code></pre>

<p>This does NOT erase the iterator, only the
item that we point to. For sets, we can erase
by value or by iterator. </p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-iterator-gotchas" class="anchor" href="#iterator-gotchas" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Iterator Gotchas</h5>

<p>If we have an iterator that points into 
a  and we then add/remove items from the
vector, then we <em>must</em> consider the iterator <em>invalid</em>.
Remember: this is <em>undefined behavior</em>.  </p>

<p>This applies to any iterators pointing into the
vector <em>before</em> the add/remove operation(s)&gt; </p>

<p>This comes from how vectors are dynamic arrays and may
be creating a new dynamic array. The old iterator
pointed at the old dynamic array though, which was freed
from memory. </p>

<p>To erase an item in a  (not via pop_back),
do it via an iterator--but then forget about any other
iterator that was pointing into that vector!</p>

<p>QUESTION: Do  ever downsize? Implementation-dependent.
There are multiple versions of the STL. Also,
never subclass any STL stuff. That'd be crazy.</p>

<p>This is not a problem with sets, lists, or maps. 
HOWEVER: IF we erase the item that an iterator points to, 
then the iterator is invalid. Re-assign the iterator. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-stl-algorithms" class="anchor" href="#stl-algorithms" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>STL Algorithms</h4>

<p>There is a separate find() function that can search
most containers and arrays for a value. </p>

<p>set_intersection() finds the intersection of two sets. </p>

<p>To do all this: <code>#include &lt;algorithm&gt;</code></p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-find" class="anchor" href="#find" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>find()</h5>

<p>First argument is an iterator where we want to start
searching. Second argument is an iterator that points
just after where we want to stop searching. 
third argument is what we want to find. </p>

<p>find() returns an iterator to the item. </p>

<p>It could not find it, it will return whatever we passed
in for the second parameter (which might not be the end()
if we've narrowed down the search area). </p>

<p>This is a linear search. </p>

<pre><code>list&lt;string&gt; names; 

a = names.begin(); 
b = names.end();

itr = find(a, b, "Judy"); 
</code></pre>

<p>find() will not look at b; it will only examine
b - 1. </p>

<p>On an array, find will return a pointer
or the value of the second parameter:</p>

<pre><code>int* ptr; 

// Address-of a[4] means we only want to look
// up to a[3]; a[4] could be after the array
ptr = find(&amp;a[0], &amp;a[4], 19);
</code></pre>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-find_if" class="anchor" href="#find_if" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>find_if()</h5>

<p>find_if() will loop through a container/array and
passes each item to a <em>predicate function</em> that we specify. </p>

<p>Again, first item, and place after last item. </p>

<p>The predicate function must return a boolean. </p>

<pre><code>int* ptr;

ptr = find_if(&amp;a[0], &amp;a[4], is_even);
</code></pre>

<p>find_if() will return a pointer to the item
if is_even() returns true and is done. Else, 
it moves onto the next item and passes a[n] into 
the predicate function. </p>

<p>The predicate function must take the item of the type 
in the array or container,and must return a boolean.</p>

<p>This all works by using <em>function pointers</em>. </p>

<p>find_if() lets us locate items meeting specific 
requirements. </p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-sort" class="anchor" href="#sort" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>sort()</h5>

<p>Sort by passing in two iterators/pointers:</p>

<p>first one is the first item, and second one
is just after the last item:</p>

<pre><code>sort(item.begin, item.end()); 
</code></pre>

<p>To sort other items, we'll need a &lt; operator. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-compound-stl-data-structures" class="anchor" href="#compound-stl-data-structures" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Compound STL Data Structures</h4>

<p>If we wanted a list of courses for each UCLA student? </p>

<pre><code>map&lt;string, list&lt;Course&gt; &gt;
</code></pre>

<p>Also, leave a space between the last two &gt; &gt;;
C++ thinks its's something else otherwise. </p>

<p>Again, the operator &lt; is needed if we have 
something that it must compare order.</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-from-office-hours" class="anchor" href="#from-office-hours" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>From Office Hours</h3>

<p>// These notes were from 10 February 2014,
// during office hours</p>

<p>// Hint on countIncludes() </p>

<p>If a1[0] == a2[0], recur
with remainders of the 
two arrays</p>

<p>Later on, if there's another match,
recur with the remainder of a1,
and ALL of a2, to see if there's another permutation</p>

<p>If a1[0] != a2[0], check remainder of a1
against ALl of a2, to see if there's any permutation  </p>

<hr>

<p>What if we want to recursively go through a circular
linked list? </p>

<pre><code>void print(const Node* cur, const Node* head)
{
    // If no nodes in list
    if (cur == nullptr)
    return;
</code></pre>

<p>This base case check ended up being unnecessary</p>

<p>// One node, linked to itself
if (cur-&gt;next == cur)
{
    cout &lt;&lt; cur-&gt;val;
    return;
}</p>

<pre><code>    cout &lt;&lt; cur-&gt;val;

    // Final base case

    if (cur-&gt;next == head)
        retun;

    // RECURSION!

    print(cur-&gt;next, head);
}
</code></pre>

<p>Challenge: What about printing in reverse order? </p>

<p>Also, to take in an STL class-type as a parameter
( is the example here):</p>

<pre><code>using std::vector;

void print(vector&lt;int&gt;&amp; mylittleVector)
{
    for (size_t = myLittleVector.size(); ...)
        ...
}
</code></pre>

<p>Note that we must specify what the vector contains,
and that we use <code>size_t</code> for referring to its size. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-compound-stl-data-structures-finished" class="anchor" href="#compound-stl-data-structures-finished" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Compound STL Data Structures, finished</h3>

<p>// Reminders for midterm: </p>

<p>Always use an initializer list, 
always have a virtual destructor,
and don't access private data of a base class. </p>

<p>We could map between a student and
a list of courses:</p>

<pre><code>// Note the separation between angle brackets
map&lt;string, list&lt;Course&gt; &gt; 

// &lt;&lt; and &gt;&gt; are bitwise shift operators
</code></pre>

<p>So why do create a  rather than create a
Student class that has a  of Courses built-in?
Because: a map has binary search that has binary
search that works in log2(N) time. </p>

<p>Example: </p>

<pre><code>map&lt;string, list&lt;Course&gt; &gt; courseMap;

Course c1("CS 32");

// Put this course into the list associated with that Student
courseMap["Carey Nachenburg"].push_back(c1); 
</code></pre>

<p>This WILL be on the final exam! </p>

<p>Challenge: A compound STL data structure that associates
People with their set of Friends. </p>

<pre><code>// This lame brackets thing is due to be changed with C++14
map&lt;Person, set&lt;Person&gt; &gt;;

// Multiset? 
</code></pre>

<p>// An answer took longer than last time
// "Incorrect!" 
// He's offering a lottery ticket this time around, haha
// "Close, but no cigar."
// "So close!" 
// "That is some ugly stuff, I don't even know..." 
// Uses a student's head as a desk, lol</p>

<p>If we have a  of something, we need a <code>&lt;</code> operator: </p>

<pre><code>bool operator&lt;(const Person&amp; A, const Person&amp; B)
{
    return (a.getName() &lt; b.getName());
}
</code></pre>

<p> and  require a &lt;-operator for any left-hand
template parameter. </p>

<p>Now associate a Person with the group of Courses and the grade
they got for each course:</p>

<pre><code>map&lt;Person, map&lt;string, Course&gt; &gt; myTranscript; // INCORRECT
</code></pre>

<p>Solution: </p>

<pre><code>map&lt;Person, map&lt;Course, string&gt; &gt; myTranscript; 
</code></pre>

<p>We're assuming that Course has a less-than <code>&lt;</code> operator. 
The left-hand data type for any  or  requires
a less-than operator! </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-big-o-notation" class="anchor" href="#big-o-notation" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Big-O Notation</h3>

<p>Now we move on to evaluating the speed of an algorithm. </p>

<p>We want to be able to compare the speed of algorithms.
First, we need to establish a standard for measuring speed. </p>

<p>We could try measuring how long it takes for something to finish,
but this is inconsistent across computers and would involve really 
long waits for things that take forever to finish crunching. </p>

<p>Measuring the run-time of an algorithm is not useful. </p>

<p>What if we measured something via the number of instructions
it takes to solve a problem of a specific, given size?</p>

<p>But what if it differed for larger or small problem sizes? </p>

<p>// "1 billiiion numbers, as from Austin Powers" </p>

<p>So let's do it via function that tells us how many
instructions are used by a function to handle input data
of some size. </p>

<p>"I take f(N) instructions to solve an input of N size."
Where f(N) is a function. </p>

<p>There's Big O, Big Omega, </p>

<p>Best, Worse, and Average case measurements. Big O is
the worst-case method.</p>

<p>Example: algo A takes 5*N^2 instructions for an input of N data</p>

<ol>
<li>We can compare for a given size of input</li>
<li>We can predict the performance of these algos when applied to
less or more data.</li>
</ol>

<p>The Big-O approach measures by the <em>gross number of steps</em> that
is requires for a size N input in the <strong>worst-case scenario</strong>.</p>

<p>We could be specific:</p>

<p>"This algo takes 5N^2 + 3N + 20 instructions to process N items."</p>

<p>With Big-O, we approximate, by ignoring the coefficients and
the lower-order terms. We would simply say, "The Big-O of
this algo is N^2". This a quick, overall impression of algo's
worst-case efficiency. </p>

<p>STORY TIME:</p>

<p>He saw a food fight, back when he was in Sproul Hall, senior year, 
but no one was throwing food, but all of a sudden,
some guys who had been mock-throwing food from the salad
bar began an actual food-fight. </p>

<p>We use simple functions like log(n), n, n^2, nlog(n). </p>

<p>We would say O(n^2) as "O of n squared" or 
"O of nlog2(N)" (it's always assumed to be log base 2 in CS). </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-computing-big-o" class="anchor" href="#computing-big-o" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Computing Big-O</h3>

<p>We need to determine the number of operations
an algo performs, f(n), this is NOT Big O. </p>

<p>Operations: </p>

<ol>
<li>Accessing an item</li>
<li>Evaluating a math expression</li>
<li>Traversing a single link in a linked list, etc. </li>
</ol>

<p>See the PowerPoint for examples. </p>

<p>We initialize, so one operation, we compare
n and i N times, we increment N times, 
we initialize the inner loop counter n times
(because of the outer loop), we compare n^2 comparisons. </p>

<p>If we wanted to compare two algo's with the same Big-O,
we would compare them at the specific data sizes we're
going to deal with, likely.</p>

<p>// York Peppermint Pattys today</p>

<ol>
<li>Determine f(n) [the number of steps required to solve a problem,
in terms of the number of items n]</li>
<li>Keep the most signficant term and toss the rest</li>
<li>Remove any constant multiplier</li>
<li>This is our Big O, "O of blah"</li>
</ol>

<p>// Note that nlog2(n) &gt; c*n for large n</p>

<p>Why do we toss constants? Because we're typically dealing with
far more stark contrasts, e.g. nlog2(n) vs n^4</p>

<p>We're focusing on the <em>most frequently-occuring operations</em>. </p>

<p>So to find Big-O, just find the thing that happens the most often,
and see how many steps it takes, in terms of the size of the
input data. </p>

<p>Assume that we're analyzing for very large N; this makes
the significant term obvious. </p>

<p>When we say O(n^2), "O of n squared", means
"This algo takes roughly n^2 operations to process N items." </p>

<p>For very large data-sets, there is a vast difference between
n^2, nlog2(n), n, etc. There can also be a O(constant),
meaning we always take a certain constant number of steps
for any data size. </p>

<p>It turns out that we cannot sort a set of data in less
than nlog2(n) time.</p>

<p>We're almost always gonna get </p>

<p>log2(N), N, Nlog2(N), N^2, N^3, 2^N, N! [this last one is horrible]</p>

<p>log2(N) is screamingly fast, much like binary search 
(if something is already sorted). </p>

<p>The choice of algorithms makes all the difference in the world.
Big-O time complexity means that we should always look for something
that is fundamentally better. </p>

<p>There is NOT a big difference between 5N^3 and 1.5N^3. </p>

<p>N^3 is terrible though. This is a USC graduate that stopped drinking
so much. A UCLA graduate would find something that does it
in Nlog2(N). Carey cites this difference in approach as the
differnece between CS-educated software engineers and self-taught coders.</p>

<p>We could write something that checks the size of an input
and then uses the best algo for that data size. </p>

<p>Note that for small data-sets, there is little difference between
algos. Use the easiest, least-buggy implementation. BUT for large
data-sets, use the most efficient algo.</p>

<p>"Don't sweat the small stuff." </p>

<p><em>Do not prematurely optimize a program</em>. Optimize a program
once it works.</p>

<p>Big-O's origin for its name? It was originally a capital omicron
and used by number theorists, apparently. </p>

<p>Big means capital, with O meaning order of complexity, apparently. </p>

<a id="user-content-big-o-examples" class="anchor" href="#big-o-examples" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Big-O Examples</h4>

<p>Question: If I have an algo, does a less efficient algo
consume more energy? </p>

<p>Smallberg is here! And he sorta knew why Big O was called
