<p>Anything that divides stuff in half, we're prolly gonna have
a log2(n) in there somewhere. </p>

<p>It's a good thing that I have many of my (2^x)'s memorized
for small-enough x's, haha. </p>

<p>Typically, we're always splitting stuff up in some way, </p>

<p>All logs are proportional to each other, so the log base
doesn't matter that much. </p>

<p>The guy scratched a ticket. He just won $10,000?!
No, Carey trolled him! </p>

<p>"Prizes may be claimed at Yo Mama's..."</p>

<p>OMG, Carey is too funny, haha. </p>

<p>Basically, multiplication is repeated addition. </p>

<p>We repeat something N times, then N times, then N times,
we to get the total: N * k, where k might N again or
log2(N), etc. </p>

<p>What if we had n^3 + n^2? Big-O ignores the lower-order
terms; if we see two loops, just look at the larger one. </p>

<p>To place something at the end of an array? O(1) or O(constant). </p>

<p>"O of some constant" is really, really nice. </p>

<p>"You know what you won? Nothing!" </p>

<p>0 + 1 + 2 + ... + (n-1) is n * (n - 1) / 2</p>

<p>n * n - n / 2 --&gt; n^2 for Big O</p>

<p>Shortcut steps:</p>

<ol>
<li><p>Locate all loops that do NOT run a fixed number of iterations,
and determine their <em>max number of iterations</em></p></li>
<li><p>Turn these loops into loops with a fixed number of iterations,
using their max number of iterations, e.g. replace i with n - 1 --&gt; n.</p></li>
</ol>

<p>Inner loops that compare against their outer loop counter
may run a variable number of items.</p>

<p>Just replace the variable thing with the max number of iterations. </p>

<ol>
<li>Declare your big O. </li>
</ol>

<p>"O of N [blah]" </p>

<p>If we have something in terms of Q, we would say O(Q^4),
"O of Q to the fourth". It makes no sense to define a function
in terms of </p>

<p>If we have:</p>

<pre><code>for ( i &lt; n )
    for ( j &lt; i * i)
        for (k &lt; j)
</code></pre>

<p>Then this is O(n^5), as we had n * n^2 * n^2</p>

<p>O(p^4)</p>

<p><strong>Check what the loops' terminating conditions are</strong></p>

<p>"What would you do if you didn't have fear?"</p>

<pre><code>for (j &lt; N)
    Circ arr[N];
</code></pre>

<p>Here, we create an array of N circles (we call the constructor N times),
N times, so O(n^2). </p>

<p>In the loop, we construct N times, and destruct N times, so that's
2N times. This is assuming that the Circle constructor's is done in O(constant) time</p>

<p>An array of integers would not take this much time though; 
that would be a constant-time operation because all the compiler
would do is allocate memory for the array, but not initialize anything. </p>

<p>What about multi-input algos? They take in two arguments, independent
of each other. </p>

<p>We could end up with a Big-O time complexity of O(n * q). </p>

<p>// QUESTION: What if we had related arguments? Would things just be </p>

<p>Because the inputs are unrelated, one or the other might dominate,
so we could have O(n + q). </p>

<p>Often, we have <code>+</code> when we have independent loops 
(or separate iterations of some sort), 
and <code>*</code> when we have nested loops (or iteration within iteration).</p>

<pre><code>for (i &lt; N)
{
    if (i == N/2)
        for (k &lt; Q)
}
</code></pre>

<p>Big-O time complexity? N + Q, because only once for every 
every value of N do we have i == N/2, which means we only do
an inner loop operation Q times total. So N + Q. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-the-stl-and-big-o" class="anchor" href="#the-stl-and-big-o" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>The STL and Big-O</h3>

<p>To search a : O( log2(N) ) // Binary search; very fast</p>

<p>// To check if we didn't find anything; if mySet.find() == mySet.end()
// end() is a sentinel value</p>

<p>To add to the <em>end</em> of a : O( 1 )</p>

<p>To delete the <em>first</em> value from a vector containing N items: O( N )</p>

<p>Without knowing the Big-O's of the STL classes, we won't know
the Big-O of our own programs that use the STL. </p>

<p>If we write a loop that runs D times that searches within a set holding
N items, then the Big-O of our loop is O( D * log2(N) ), as D
is a variable independent of N. </p>

<p>Look to the PowerPoint for the Big O of the STL. </p>

<a id="user-content-stls-big-o-continued" class="anchor" href="#stls-big-o-continued" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>STL's Big-O continued</h3>

<p>In the average case, a  can insert
and delete from the end in constant-time.
Accessing is also done in constant-time. </p>

<p>When we analyze the time complexity of our own code,
we need to know the Big-O of the STL's operations too,
so that we know what our own code's Big-O will be. </p>

<p>For a  or a , inserting, finding, and deleting can be
done in O(log2(n)). "log n" NOT "n log n". </p>

<p>Note: the final exam will be more analytical than 
programming, more logic puzzles. </p>

<p> and  are all constant-time for top and bottom
stuff. </p>

<p> is O(1) or O(n) for its operations. </p>

<p> is the same, expect for finding an item,
which will be O(x). </p>

<p>And reminder: Big-O is always in terms of the variables
used. "n" is just the generic variable. </p>

<p>STORY TIME: </p>

<p>He was in India, at the Bombay Airport,

<p>Example: </p>

<pre><code>set&lt;int&gt; nums;

for (int i = 0; i &lt; q; i++)
    nums.insert( i );
</code></pre>

<p>Inserting is normally log2(q), but the set begins
empty, and increases in size. For Big-O, evaluate the worst-case,
which is that we need to assume that nums will hold q items.</p>

<p>So each time we insert, it takes log2(q) steps. Then we
repeat this q times to insert q items. </p>

<p>If something is changing size, just assume that it's the biggest
it can be, then evaluate from there on. We're assuming that it
always has the maximum number of items it can have, as Big-O
looks at the <em>worst case</em>. </p>

<p>Carey: "I don't have any napkins, so you can just use your shirt." </p>

<p>For set s, int n:</p>

<pre><code>for (int i = 0; i &lt; n * n; i++)
{
    s.insert(i);
}
</code></pre>

<p>The answer is O(n^2log2(n^2)) or O(n^2log2(n)),
because the exponent inside the log can be taken
out as a constant. </p>

<p>Now, we have a vector&lt; set &gt; v</p>

<p>v with N sets, and each set has Q items.</p>

<p>"Do you want a waffle?"
"I already had breakfast." </p>

<p>Quite a few people actually turning down the
waffles today. xD</p>

<p>What about number of even values in all of v ?</p>

<p>N * Q, because we have to go through every set in 
the vector. There are N sets, with Q items in each. </p>

<p>"You know what that sounds like? Wrong." </p>

<p>"Just Q?! That's worth a prize ticket--that's ballsy."</p>

<p>How about finding the first set with a particular value
and then counting the number of even values in that set? </p>

<p>N * log2(Q) + Q [N or Q may dominate; need to include both terms]</p>

<p>We may have to go through all the sets, and then once
we find the set, we have to go through all the Q items
to count all the evens. </p>

<p>Evens-counting is O(Q) and not O(log2(Q)) because there's
no built-in function for doing so in log2(Q) time; it has
to be done linearly instead with an iterator. </p>

<p>We (+) Q because we only do the even-counting once. </p>

<p>Do NOT use multiplication unless something is done to
<em>every</em> item; use addition when somethingis done to a <em>single</em>
item. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-sorting" class="anchor" href="#sorting" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Sorting</h3>

<p>Sorting is the process of ordering a bunch of items
based on one or more rules, subject to one or more constraints.</p>

<p>We can sort just about anything; the number of items to be sorted
is <em>important</em>; this affects our sort choice. Large data-sets
require efficient sorting algos. </p>

<p>We need to figure out how to sort something, e.g. by
first name, last name, and then GPA. </p>

<p>Constraints: Are the items in RAM or on disk? E.g., if
we're sorting through Facebook's archives, and we need to sort
chunks at a time. Are we dealing with something that can be fit
in memory all at once? </p>

<p>What data container holds the data? </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-rules" class="anchor" href="#rules" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Rules</h4>

<ol>
<li><p>Do NOT choose a sorting algo until we understand
the requirements of a problem.</p></li>
<li><p>Always choose the simplest sorting algo possible
that meets our requirements. </p></li>
</ol>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-selection-sort" class="anchor" href="#selection-sort" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Selection Sort</h3>

<p>This is slow, and not used often in practice, 
but it'll come up in interviews. </p>

<p>Say we have a bunch of books on a self that we 
want to order ascending based on size of the spine. </p>

<p>First, we find the shortest book in the N size group.</p>

<p>We swap this with the first book.
Then, we look at the remaining N - 1 books and select
the shortest, and swap it with the second book. </p>

<p>Continue this until everything is sorted. </p>

<p>There is no n! algo that we would ever want to use. </p>

<p>Time complexity? <strong>O(n^2)</strong></p>

<p>A step is any time we swap or point our finder at a book. </p>

<p>How do we arrive at O(n^2)? We're simplifying by assuming
N swap steps, and then we have N + N -1 + N - 2+ ... 
--&gt; N * (N+1)/2 --&gt; N^2</p>

<p>When is it more or less efficient? What if something 
is already in order or mostly in order? </p>

<p>Selection sort will still be just as slow. It'll 
still go through everything because it doesn't know for sure
that the first item really is the smallest one in the array
of items. </p>

<p>On the final exam, we'll be asked something like:
"What will this array look like after two iterations
of a selection sort?" </p>

<p>Does a selection sort work well for a linked list? 
Sure! We can easily traverse a list, and the pointer-nature
of nodes means we have an easy time swapping values
between stuff. Note that we'd be changing what node we start
at each time, which is easy, as we'd simply have simple 
pointer to the starting node for examination, and then we could
simply follow that pointer to the next node for the next iteration. </p>

<p>Note that recursion is atypical for selection sort. </p>

<p>Is a selection sort <em>stable</em> or <em>unstable</em>?</p>

<p>We have a comedy show for the break.</p>

<p>"Don't say anything inappropriate or take off your clothes." </p>

<p>There are two CS majors competing in the Laugh Bowl this year. 
LaughFactory.com</p>

<p>"Adult friendships. Not like that!"</p>

<p>Impression of Smallberg time. That was hilarious!</p>

<p>Austin was the performer. </p>

toast them! xD</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-stable-vs-unstable-sorting" class="anchor" href="#stable-vs-unstable-sorting" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Stable vs. Unstable Sorting</h3>

<p>This is not mental instability. </p>

<p>N old people line up to buy laxatives.</p>

<p>"Anybody need a definiiton for laxatives?"</p>

<p>The drugstore wants to sort them and serve them
based on urgency. </p>

<p>"Who hasn't pooped in the longest time?" </p>

<p>Then need to choose a sorting algo
that'll do things in a descending manner. </p>

<p>An <em>unstable</em> sorting algo re-orders
the items without taking into account their initial
ordering. E.g., Ebenezer was originally first in line,
but ended up 3rd in line. This is because everyone was
sorted based on the number of days they haven't ordered;
the sort didn't care about the original line order. </p>

<p>"Andrea's my mom by the way. That would be really embarrassing." </p>

<p>A <em>stable</em> sort takes into account the initial order
and maintains the initial order for items that tie based 
on the sorting criteria. </p>

<p>If you forget, just remember the <em>laxatives</em>!</p>

<p>So is a selection sort stable or unstable? 
Stable. We don't swap out the first item unless
we find a value smaller/larger than it later
in the list of stuff (depends on the sorting order). </p>

<p>// I love the GIF intermissions</p>

<p>"The dog is hyponotizing the cat. This is much
funnier than you thought it was."</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-insertion-sort" class="anchor" href="#insertion-sort" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Insertion Sort</h3>

<p>We use this commonly to sort playing cards. </p>

<p>With books: </p>

<p>Focus on the first two books. If the last
book in this set is in the wrong order,
remove it from the sehlf, shfift the books before it right
as needed, and keep comparing the remove book with the shifted
books, and insert it back into the proper slot.</p>

<p>We start with a size of two, and increase our set size,
shifting books as needed. </p>

<p>Start with size s = 2.</p>

<p>While there are still books to sort: </p>

<pre><code>Focus on the first s books

If the last book is out of order

Remove it

Shift the books before it to the right

insert our book into the proper slot

s = s + 1
</code></pre>

<p>Breaking ties depends on our comparison. </p>

<p>For Big-O. We may shift up to one book for round
one. For round two, this might be two books. </p>

<p>During the last round: N - 1 shifts t to find the
right spot (we're assuming insertion is done in 
constant time). </p>

<p>This is roughly <strong>O(N^2)</strong> steps overall.</p>

<p>If all the books are all already in the right order, 
then insertion sort never does any shifting, and will
take O(n) time to figure this out. This is the best
case for insertion sort. </p>

<p>A perfectly mis-ordered set (i.e. reverse of our intended
order), will take N^2 steps. </p>

<p>See the PowerPoint for the implementation code. </p>

<p>Is this a stable sort? This depends on the implementation;
by default, it is generally stable, as it won't move something
unless necessary. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-bubble-sort" class="anchor" href="#bubble-sort" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Bubble Sort</h3>

<p>Everyone loves to make fun of bubble sort. </p>

<p>Start the atop element of the array. </p>

<p>If the first two elements are out of order, 
swap them.</p>

<p>Then advance forward, a[1] and a[2]. Compare them,
if so, swap, then advance again, all the way till
the end of the array. </p>

<p>IN essence: check two items at a time.</p>

<p>If we made any swaps, repeat the whole process. </p>

<p>Example: celebrities sorted based on the number
of times they've been in rehab. </p>

<p>The smaller items "bubble" up toward te top. </p>

<p>Mentioning bubble sort would likely cause the interviewer
to laugh. </p>

<p>Bubble sort has a bad (bubble) "wrap"</p>

<p>During each pass, we compare every element with its successor,
N steps, and we if made any swaps, we may have to repeat this
process, which means in the worst case we'll do
N passes of N bubbles --&gt; N^2</p>

<p>Bubble sort is <strong>O(N^2)</strong></p>

<p>What if the data is already in order? <strong>O(N)</strong> to find out that
the data is already in order. </p>

<p>Ask the interviewer: Does the provided algo terminate
after making no swaps, or does it always go for N^2 times? </p>

<p>Final exam: We'll be given an array partially sorted, and
we'll be asked: "Which sort did this?" </p>

<p>Every sort is different. The results are distinguishable. </p>

<p>Is bubble sort stable? Yes. We don't move stuff unless we
necessary. </p>

<p>"The Lion Sleeps Tonight" --&gt; Sorting dance, haha. </p>

<p>How to tell stuff apart (after one round of sorting): </p>

<p>Selection sort: Is the smallest item in the first slot? </p>

<p>Insertion sort: Is the second item smaller than the first item?</p>

<p>Bubble sort: Are the last two items out of order? </p>

<p>Note that bubble sort will always leave the biggest number
in the last slot after the first iteration. Then on the second
iteration, the two biggest numbers will be there, and so on. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-shell-sort" class="anchor" href="#shell-sort" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Shell Sort</h3>

<p>The Big-O of shell sort is actually unknown.</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-h-shorting" class="anchor" href="#h-shorting" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>h-shorting</h4>

<p>h-sorting underlies shell sorting. </p>

<p>Pick a value for h (some positive whole number)</p>

<p>Compare a[i] and a[i + h];
    if they are out of order
    Swap tem
If we swapped any items, repeat the whole process again</p>

<p>Bubble sort is basically an h-sort with a value of h = 1</p>

<p>A higher h-value does not make h-sort more efficient.
An array that is h-sorted may not be truly sorted (unless h = 1). 
All h-sort does is guarantee that the i item is less
than the i + h item. </p>

<p>So we'd compare first item and fourth item, 
then second item and fith item (h = 3).
Then here, every item is less than the item 3 places
away from it. It would be "3-sorted".</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-shellsort" class="anchor" href="#shellsort" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Shellsort</h4>

<p>Pick a sequence of <em>decreasing</em> h-values ending with
an h-value of 1: e.g. 8, 4, 2, 1</p>

<p>There have been many attempts to figure out the best 
sequence.</p>

<p><strong>It is required that we end with h = 1</strong>. 
This guarantees that stuff actually gets sorted.</p>

<p>How does it work? We h1-sort, then h2-sort, then
h3-sort, etc. </p>

<p>Example: 8-sort, then 4-sort, then 2-sort, then 1-sort
(bubble sort). </p>

<p>Common pattern: exponential-decreasing h-values. </p>

<p>With each h-sort, we're making the array more sorted each
time for the next sorting pass, so the end result is less
than O(n^2) [the Big-O for bubble sort]. </p>

<p>Can this be applied easily to a linked list? 
Sure: Santo had the idea of having two separate pointers. </p>

<p>We simply advance the pointers, with the starting 
difference being the h-value.</p>

<p>"You're now immortalized in my lecture notes." </p>

<p>Shell sort is not a stable sort. "Look at Wikipedia."</p>

<p>Shell sort is estimated to have a time complexity of
~<strong>O(N^1.25)</strong>. It's usually used on low-memory run environments,
as it uses little memory. </p>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-lecture-15-26-february-2014" class="anchor" href="#lecture-15-26-february-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lecture 15: 26 February 2014</h2>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-quicksort" class="anchor" href="#quicksort" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Quicksort</h3>

<p>But first, an STL challenge: </p>

<p>std::map &gt; habitationHistory;</p>

<p>P people, with E former streets. </p>

<p>Finding the names of all people who lives on levering street? 
Assuming we have a set. <em>P * log2(E)</em>. We have to go through
all the people and then check their set of former addresses.</p>

<p>Bill ever lived on Westwood? <em>log2(P) + log2(E)</em>. 
We find the right person, then we check if the that street
is in their history. </p>

<p>Print out every name with each person's streets, in alpha order. 
<em>P * E</em>. We go through every person and then through
all their streets.  or a  is already in order. </p>

<hr>

<p>Okay, on to <strong>quicksort</strong> and *<em>mergesort</em>, 
the divide-and-conquer algorithms. There are known 
as the efficient sorts and are the ones we normally
use in regular software engineering. </p>

<p>In general, they work like this: </p>

<ol>
<li>
<em>Divide</em> the data-set into two groups of roughly equal size</li>
<li>Sort each of these sub-sets (<em>conquer</em>)</li>
<li>Combine the two sorted groups into one large sorted list
(Line up your conquered enemies and make them watch you
listen to you gloat about your victory.)</li>
</ol>

<p>Notice how this is perfect for <em>recursion</em>!</p>

<p>Quicksort works like this (what we did a previous homework
assignment without realizing it): </p>

<ol>
<li>If the array contains only 0 or 1 element, return</li>
<li>Select an arbitrary element P (pivot) from the array (typically the first one)</li>
<li>Move all elements that are <em>less than or equal to P</em> to the left 
of the array and all elements <em>greater than P</em> to the right. 
This is <em>partioning</em> (divide), and is what split() did in our homework. 
Note that the elements on either side are not in order (at this point). 
They're just less than or greater than the pivot value. </li>
<li>Recursively repeat this process on the left sub-array and on the right sub-array
This is the <em>conquer</em> step. Why do operate on the sub-arrays and forget
about the pivot? Because it's already in the right spot! Everything to the
left and to the right is out of order, but the pivot value fits between them.
Note however that the pivot value will not always end up in the middle
once the array is sorted. </li>
</ol>

<p>Note that we could reverse this so that larger ends up on the left, and
and smaller on the left. Note that this sort is <em>unstable</em>, as ties do not
maintain their original order. </p>

<p>In more advanced algos, multiple values are picked randomly, find the median 
element, and then use that (since it's closer to the true median value). </p>

<p>In code: </p>

<pre><code>void quickSort(int array[], int first, int last)
{
    if (last - first &gt;= 1)
    {
        int pivotIndex;

            // Divide!

        pivotindex = partition(array, first, first);

            // Conquer!

        // Left side; don't touch the pivotValue again
        quickSort(array, first, pivotIndex - 1); 

        // Right side; don't touch the pivotValue again
        quickSort(arary, pivotIndex + 1, last); 
    }
}
</code></pre>

<p>See split() in the homework, which modified parameters to let us know
what the partition values were. </p>

<p>partition() will actually complete in O(n) time. Why? Because its
low and high trackers are checked to see if low ever exceeds high,
which would mean we hit the end. </p>

<p>Note also how the pivotValue may not end up in the exact middle. </p>

<hr>

<p>What's the Big-O of quicksort? Well, we go through the array
to partion it, which takes N steps, then we repeat this for each half.
The number of halves? log2(N) [2 ^ what power is equal to the size of the data-set?]</p>

<p>For each iteration, we partition, costing N steps, then we partition 
the halves, N/2 and N/2. Then four arrays, partitioned at a cost of
N/4 + N/4 + N/4 + N/4. N steps! </p>

<p>So, we take N steps each time to partition (as we just showed), 
and we repeat this log2(N) times (the number of halves we can
make till we hit just 1 or 0 items left [our base case]) in a subarray. </p>

<p>So, quicksort's average Big-O is <strong>O(nlog2(n))</strong></p>

<p><em>However</em>, quicksort is very slow for a set of data that is 
already sorted or mostly sorted. Then, the partitioning hardly
moves anything around but still takes N steps anyway. Then, 
when we recursively do this, there is no left or right side. 
The problem reduction is minimal, so the "problem" is solved slowly. </p>

<p>We repeat this process N times (no split), and each time we partition,
we take N times, so it takes N^2 now.</p>

<p>Then, the algo will only lop off item off the side each time,
and then repeat this N items, for N partitions each time.
This problem also comes up with data that is in reverse order.</p>

<p>So quicksort's worst-case Big-O is <strong>O(n^2)</strong></p>

<p>AVOID quicksort for data that is already mostly or completely
sorted, or in (mostly) reverse order (everything gets moved
to one side [no splitting]). </p>

<p>For an array of N items, which has N! possible configurations,
quicksort is nlog2(n) for most of the configurations
(which would be out of order). </p>

<p>Quicksort works well with a doubly-linked list as we can go up
and down easily. It is an <em>unstable</em> sort, because the pivot
pick is rather random, and we might end up moving stuff around. </p>

<p>In terms of memory usage, quicksort's RAM requirements vary
and can be memory-intensive.</p>

<p>Can it be parallelized across multiple cores? Yes: once the
partioning happens, the subsets can be given to different cores,
which can distribute again if we have a lot of cores. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-mergesort" class="anchor" href="#mergesort" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Mergesort</h3>

<p>Merge takes <em>pre-sorted</em> ararays as inputs and outputs a combined
third sorted array. We learned this as the Lazy Person's Sort.</p>

<p>The underlying basic merge algo: </p>

<p>Take the left-most book in both shelves. 
move the the smallest book to the new, third shelf. </p>

<p>In other words: go through both arrays, compare their 
corresponding elements, and copy over the smaller/larger value
into the third result array 
(depending on if we want ascending or descending). </p>

<p>If either array runs, copy over the entire 
remaining contents of the other array (they're guaranteed 
to belong there, since the arrays are pre-sorted). </p>

<p>We can take a single array and split using different
indices trackers (assuming the tracked parts are already
sorted into two parts, and we know where those parts begin). </p>

<p>Once all this is done, we copy over the contents of the
third, temporary array into the original input array. 
Delete the temporary array. </p>

<p>Full mergesort algo: </p>

<ol>
<li>If array has 1 element, base case: return</li>
<li>Split array into two equal section3</li>
<li>Recursively call mergeSort on left half</li>
<li>Recursively call mergeSort on right half</li>
<li>Call basicMerge to combine the resulting halves</li>
</ol>

<p>The base case is 1 and NOT 2, because the two items
might be out of order, which basicMerge would take care of. </p>

<p>// Many people are missing from the class today, haha,
// likely on account of Project 3</p>

<p><strong>Look to the UCSF visualization of sorts</strong></p>

<p>// Holy cow, Carey's calendar is ridiculous! 
// One way to keep programming: do just enough
// to get more money but not be promoted</p>

<hr>

<p>STORY TIME: </p>

<p>Back when Carey was an intern at Symantec, he
was analyzing DOS viruses. The company wasn't as
strict back then. Everything is kept locked up
in a lab. But back then, he was allowed to login
remotely and copy files back and forth to get some
work done. So he had a virus on the computer on 
work computer, on the gateway, and he was about to 
transfer it back, but he wrote "copy [blah]" but
this automatically ran the copy.exe virus on the gateway
computer. He realized this, and tried to manually clean
up the gateway. He does so. His colleague: 
"Dude, you infected the entire network!" </p>

<p>Every computer is running a scan of Norton and every file 
is coming up infected. He fears being fired and going to jail.
He's crying. His boss apparently pulled a joke. A scan found
the infection right away and removed it. They made a joke definition
that would detect every file, and so it made look like everything 
was infected. </p>

<hr>

<p>NOTE: mergeSort works in-place, all within the array,
except for basicMerge() part, which requires a temporary array. </p>

<p>Big-O analysis time: </p>

<p>The maximum number of levels for mergeSort? log2(N). 
What about the merging cost? We merge a total of N items
at the bottom (halves + halves + halves + ...)</p>

<p>At the second-to-bottom level, it also takes N steps. </p>

<p>At every level, we merge N items. </p>

<p>So mergeSort() is always <strong>O(nlog2(n))</strong></p>

<p>Why would we choose quickSort() over mergeSort()? </p>

<p>Because mergeSort() requires allocation of temporary 
containers (in fact, double the memory)!</p>

<p>quickSort() does not require that. </p>

<p>mergeSort() caveats? No, regardless of how items are
arranged. The time and memory cost comes from allocating
temporary containers for basicMerge()</p>

<p>Is this easy for a linked list? Yes, just follow the links, 
swap values as needed. </p>

<p>It is a <em>stable</em> sort. Are there are any special uses for mergeSort()
that other sorts can't handle? Yes: if we have large datasets
split across multiple hard-drives, then mergeSort() is perfect for it,
since reading/writing to HDDs is slow. </p>

<p>It can totally be parallelized across mulitiple cores, though
basicMerge() must be done under a single core. </p>

<p>Look at the cheat sheet! </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-trees" class="anchor" href="#trees" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Trees</h3>

<p>A special linked data structure that has many uses in CS. </p>

<p>It can manipulate hierarchical data, make information 
easily searchable, manipulate sorted lists, simplify 
expression evaluation, and make decisions (decision trees). </p>

<p>Time for some inappropriate. </p>

<p>Go back 32 levels from a person (32 generations),
we'll have ~ 4 billion. So go back far enough, and
it looks like everything is incest, haha. </p>

<p>Treets are made of nodes, with a <em>root pointer</em>,
analogous to the head pointer of lineked lists. 
The <em>root node</em>, pointed to by this root pointer, may have 0 or more 
children nodes. And so on for each of the children nodes. </p>

<p>Trees with two children for each node are called a binary tree.</p>

<p>A tree with no nodes is call an <em>empty tree</em>,
typically a base case. </p>

<p>A node with 0 children is called a <em>leaf node</em>.</p>

<p>If the root has no children, it is a root and a leaf.</p>

<p><em>Trees are linked lists with multiple forward pointers.</em></p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-binary-trees" class="anchor" href="#binary-trees" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Binary Trees</h3>

<p>A tree where every node has at most <em>two children nodes</em>. </p>

<p>With the exception of the root node, every node
has exactly one parent. Every two nodes descending
from the same parent node are called <em>sibling nodes</em>&gt; </p>

<p>An <em>ancestor node</em> is one which lies between the current node
and the root node, including the root. </p>

<p>Amongst the levels, we don't have links: if we did,
that'd be a <em>graph</em>.</p>

<p><em>Subtree</em>: Any node in the tree can be viewed as the root
of a smaller tree, a subtree. We'll use recursion to process
subtrees on either side of a binary tree. </p>

<p>"A binary tree is <em>bi</em>, not that bi"</p>

<p>Left and right sub-trees: If we pick a node from 
our tree, we can identify its left and right sub-trees. </p>

<p><em>Node depth</em> is the deptht of a node is the distance of a node
from the root of the tree, where the root has dept 0. </p>

<p><em>Full binary tree</em>: Every leaf has the same depth and every non-leaf
(not counting the root node)
has exactly two children. N depth, with 2^(n+1) - 1 nodes</p>

<p>Many operations are possible with a tree. We can add items,
delete items, search for something, 
remove a section of a tree (<em>pruning</em>) or add on a section (<em>grafting</em>). </p>

<p>To show that a child does not exist, use a <code>nullptr</code>.</p>

<p>Smallberg: "I deliberately tanked my class so I could get ice cream."</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-binary-tree-traversals" class="anchor" href="#binary-tree-traversals" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Binary Tree Traversals</h3>

<p>Any time we traverse through a tree, we always
<em>start with the root node</em>. </p>

<p>Four ways to do this: </p>

<ol>
<li>Pre-order</li>
<li>In-order</li>
<li>Post-order</li>
<li>Level-order</li>
</ol>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-pre-order-traversal" class="anchor" href="#pre-order-traversal" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Pre-order Traversal</h4>

<p>It's recursive. </p>

<ol>
<li>Process the current node (add, print, etc.)</li>
<li>Process (recursively) the nodes in the left sub-tree</li>
<li>Process (recusrively) the nodes in the right sub-tree</li>
</ol>

<p>Why "pre-order"? Because the first thing we do
is we process in the value in the node <em>first</em>. </p>

<p>Dingleberry man visited to watch the lecture: "Carey on!"</p>

<pre><code>void preOrder(Node* cur)
{
    if (cur == nullptr)
        return; 

    cout &lt;&lt; cur-&gt;value; // Process the current node

    preOrder(cur-&gt;left); // Recusively process left sub-tree
    preOrder(cur-&gt;right); 
}
</code></pre>

<p>Note that the subtree process calls will NOT have knowledge of
the nodes above it. Instead, it's as before with recursion,
when recursively operating on an array meant we only had 
knowledge of our current subset of it. </p>

<p>Going to a leaf's child will give us a nullptr, 
meaning we process the leaves too (check if child pointer is nullptr
would skip leaves). </p>

<p>This is a depth-first search. It can also be done with a stack. </p>

<p>We visit every node ONCE, so traversal is O(N). </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-in-order-traversal" class="anchor" href="#in-order-traversal" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>In-Order Traversal</h4>

<ol>
<li>Process the nodes in the left sub-tree</li>
<li>Process the current node</li>
<li>Process the nodes in the right sub-tree</li>
</ol>

<p>Processing the current node happens <em>in-between</em>
processing the sub-trees. </p>

<p>Notice the order in which stuff gets printed out.</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-post-order-travesal" class="anchor" href="#post-order-travesal" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Post-Order Travesal</h4>

<ol>
<li>Process the nodes in the left sub-tree</li>
<li>Process the nodes in the right sub-tree</li>
<li>Process the current node</li>
</ol>

<p>We always go left-to-right on trees. </p>

<p>In-order used for binary search trees typically. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-level-order-traversal" class="anchor" href="#level-order-traversal" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Level-Order Traversal</h4>

<p>NOT recursive.</p>

<ol>
<li>Use a temp pointer variable and queue of node pointers</li>
<li>Insert root node pointer into the pointer</li>
<li>While the queue is not empty:
A. Dequeue the top node and put it into temp
B. Process the node
C. Add the node's children to queue if they are not nullptr</li>
</ol>

<p>Again, what we did on the second midterm. </p>

<p>We visit each level's nodes, left to right, before going onto 
nodes in the next level. This results from the FIFO nature
of a queue. This one is a breadth-first search. </p>

<p>// Carey just asked us to pump up his BruinWalk score, haha. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-big-o-of-traversals" class="anchor" href="#big-o-of-traversals" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Big-O of Traversals</h4>

<p>Every traversal visits each node up to three times
(hit it, go left, return, go right, return), so they all
take <strong>O(n)</strong>. We don't skip nodes or skip branches. </p>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-lecture-16-3-march-2014" class="anchor" href="#lecture-16-3-march-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lecture 16: 3 March 2014</h2>

<p>// It's already March! </p>

<p>// Project 4 spec's is already partially up
// It's a database-building project
// It's meant for storing and querying data
// It can also order and output data
// It'll be done with binary search trees</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-evaluating-expressions" class="anchor" href="#evaluating-expressions" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Evaluating Expressions</h3>

<p>Previously, we've done evaluation of postfix expressions. </p>

<p>Now, we can do arithmetic expressions with binary trees! </p>

<p>// I wonder about the steps required to get it all into the
// tree though. </p>

<p>CS 132 for compiler-writing, useful for evaluating expressions. 
Data structures come up again with compiler writing too. </p>

<p>Say we have something like this: </p>

<pre><code>        *
    +       -
  5   6   3   1
</code></pre>

<p>We begin with the root pointer and evaluate using
this general algo: </p>

<ol>
<li><p>If the current node is a number, return its value
(This is our base case)</p></li>
<li><p>Recursively evaluate the left sub-tree and get the result</p></li>
<li><p>Recursively evaluate the right sub-tree and get the result</p></li>
<li><p>Apply the operator in the current node to the left and right
results and return that.  </p></li>
</ol>

<p>This is a <em>post-order traversal</em>. </p>

<p>This could of course be generalized to any sort of operator/operation,
operating on many types of values. Our nodes could contain pointers
to functions, or have functions unto themselves, etc. Code is powerful.</p>

<p>This could work for boolean expressions too, where NOT would have a single
child. </p>

<p>Quick bit of code to do this: </p>

<pre><code>template &lt;class Data&gt;
struct Node
{
    Data m_value; 
    Node* m_left; 
    Node* m_right; 
}


Data evaluate(Node* cur)
{
    if (isnum(m_value))
        return m_value; 
</code></pre>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-binary-search-trees" class="anchor" href="#binary-search-trees" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Binary Search Trees</h3>

<p>NOTE: Binary Tree is NOT a Binary Search Tree. </p>

<p>A BT has two children per node. A BST has ordered 
children per node. Read the problem VERY carefully. </p>

<p>BSTs were designed to rule search. </p>

<p>This is made possible by their structure:</p>

<p>For <em>any</em> given node in a BST:</p>

<ol>
<li><p>All nodes in the <em>left</em> sub-tree must be <em>less</em> than 
the current node's value</p></li>
<li><p>All nodes in the <em>right</em> sub-tree must be <em>greater</em> than
the current node's value</p></li>
</ol>

<p>Some special cases allow for &lt;= or &gt;= as well. </p>

<p>Note that BSTs are not always balanced. </p>

<p>Does this make a difference? Yes, it does. We'll see. </p>

<p>Are duplicates allowed? Depends on our needs. </p>

<p>We can choose to always place duplicates on the
left or right side, or have a third field in each
node that has a list of all the duplicates. </p>

<p>We can do many different things with BSTs: </p>

<ul>
<li>Check if it's empty</li>
<li>Search it for a value </li>
<li>Insert an item </li>
<li>Delete an item </li>
<li>Find the height of the tree</li>
<li>Traverse the binary tree (used for many of the above)</li>
<li>Free the memory used by the tree (a necessity!)</li>
</ul>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-searching-a-bst" class="anchor" href="#searching-a-bst" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Searching a BST</h4>

<p>To search a BST: </p>

<ol>
<li><p>Begin at the root node</p></li>
<li><p>Continue forward until we hit a nullptr</p></li>
<li><p>If goal is == current node's value, then found!
We can return true, or a pointer to the node, etc. </p></li>
<li><p>If goal is &lt; current node's value, <em>go left</em></p></li>
<li><p>If goal is &gt; current node's value, <em>go right</em></p></li>
<li><p>If we hit a nullptr, the value was not found in the BST;
indicate that to the caller in some way</p></li>
</ol>

<p>What if there are duplicates? We'd have to keep traversing. 
Why would we have duplicates? Because we have two people with
the same name but they also have other data. </p>

<p>Carey bought all the prizes at Walmart and all the 
"inappropriate prizes" at Ahhz. The STL  is actually using
a BST.  and  also use BSTs. We're learning how
to do this stuff from scratch. </p>

<p>Recursively: </p>

<pre><code>template &lt;class Data&gt;
class Node
{
    Data m_value;
    Node* m_left;
    Node* m_right;
};
</code></pre>

<p>These would be part of a larger BST class. </p>

<pre><code>template &lt;class Data&gt;
bool searchBST(Data value, Node* ptr)
{  
    // Not found
    if (ptr == nullptr) 
        return false;  

    // Found!
    else if (value == ptr-&gt;m_value)
        return true; 

    // Value is less than current
    // Go left
    else if (value &lt; ptr-&gt;m_value)
        return(Search(value, ptr-&gt;left));

    // Don't forget to return the result
    // of a recursive call!

    // Value is greater than current
    // Go right
    else  
        return(Search(value, ptr-&gt;right));
}
</code></pre>

<p>Iteratively: </p>

<pre><code>template &lt;class Data&gt;
bool searchBST(Data value, Node* ptr)
{  
    while (ptr != nullptr)
    {

        // Found!
        if (value == ptr-&gt;m_value)
            return true; 

        // Value is less than current
        // Go left
        else if (value &lt; ptr-&gt;m_value)
            ptr = ptr-&gt;m_left;

        // Value is greater than current
        // Go right
        else  
            ptr = ptr-&gt;m_right;
    }

    // Not found
    return false;
}
</code></pre>

<p>// I just earned some Oreos and a prize ticket
// for answering that the average Big-O
// for an average BST is O(log2(N))</p>

<p>// It is NOT (Nlog2(N)); after all,
// we can traverse in O(N); why the hell
// would searching a binary <em>search</em> tree
// take longer? </p>

<p>In the average case, searching a BST will take 
<strong>O(log2(N))</strong> time, as we eliminate 50% of the possibilities
each time we go left or right. In the worst case (where
no branching off is possible), searching will take <strong>O(N)</strong> time. </p>

<p>The latter comes when we have a badly-formatted BST. </p>

<p>4 billion nodes can be searched in just 32 steps. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-inserting-a-new-value-into-a-bst" class="anchor" href="#inserting-a-new-value-into-a-bst" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Inserting a New Value into a BST</h4>

<p>Insertion is trickier? Why? Because we have to maintain
the tree's very specific structure. The new value must be placed
such that the tree is still a BST afterward. </p>

<p>// Calling our search() function as our first step would be lame
// That's adding extra, unnecessary steps. Just have insert()
// be its own thang</p>

<p>General algo: </p>

<pre><code>IF the tree is empty
{
    Allocate a new node with given value
    Point root pointer to new node
    RETURN
}

Begin at root of tree

// Stopping condition checked iteratively or recursively
WHILE we're not done 
{
    // Value already exists; duplicates not allowed with this one
    IF given value is == current node's value
        RETURN 

    IF given value is &lt; current node's value
    {
        IF there is a left child
            Go left
        ELSE
            Allocate a new node with given value
            Set current node's left pointer to new node
            RETURN
    }

    IF given value is &gt; current node's value
    {
        IF there is a right child
            Go right
        ELSE
            Allocate a new node with given value
            Set current node's right pointer to new node
            RETURN
    }
}
</code></pre>

<p>QUESTION: What if a new node belongs somewhere in-between
the root node and its descendants? </p>

<p>ANSWER: It would end up as a right child node of the intermediate. </p>

<p>As for Big-O? Well, we find the right insertion spot in
O(log2(N)) time (or worst case, O(N) time), and then insertion
is done in constant time. </p>

<p>Note how classic binary search trees just add stuff 
in whatever order it happens to arrive. </p>

<p>And deletion? Deletion is a huge pain with BSTs...</p>

<p>QUESTION: What if we had to delete the root node?! </p>

<p>// Carey is eating dinner with his TAs tonight
// He'll probably grill them intensely, haha</p>

<p>Given a random array of numbers, if you insert
them into a BST, the resulting tree will be rather
bushy, and its depth will be around log2(N). </p>

<p>But if we insert an ordered array of numbers, 
the BST will be a long line of nodes,
a really inefficient structure for a BST. </p>

<p>If our values are largely ordered, 
consider inserting them radiating outward
from the median, randomly, etc. That will 
lead to a much more balanced BST. </p>

<p>So why do we assume log2(N) for BSTs? Most
are gonna meet that. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-finding-the-min--max-values-in-a-bst" class="anchor" href="#finding-the-min--max-values-in-a-bst" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Finding the Min &amp; Max Values in a BST</h4>

<p>This is actually very easy with a BST, thanks
to its structure. </p>

<p>To find a min, just keep going left!</p>

<p>Iteratively:</p>

<pre><code>template &lt;class Data&gt;
Data getMin(Node* ptr)
{
    if (ptr == nullptr)
        return TREE_EMPTY;

    while (ptr-&gt;m_left != nullptr)
        ptr = ptr-&gt;m_left;

    return ptr-&gt;m_value;
}
</code></pre>

<p>Recursively:</p>

<pre><code>template &lt;class Data&gt; 
Data getMin(Node* ptr)
{
    if (ptr == nullptr)
        return TREE_EMPTY;

    if (ptr-&gt;m_left == nullptr)
        return ptr-&gt;m_value;

    return getMin(ptr-&gt;m_left);
}
</code></pre>

<p>To find a max, just keep going right: </p>

<pre><code>template &lt;class Data&gt;
Data getMin(Node* ptr)
{
    if (ptr == nullptr)
        return TREE_EMPTY;

    while (ptr-&gt;m_right != nullptr)
        ptr = ptr-&gt;m_right;

    return ptr-&gt;m_value;
}
</code></pre>

<p>Recursively:</p>

<pre><code>template &lt;class Data&gt; 
Data getMin(Node* ptr)
{
    if (ptr == nullptr)
        return TREE_EMPTY;

    if (ptr-&gt;m_right == nullptr)
        return ptr-&gt;m_value;

    return getMin(ptr-&gt;m_right);
}
</code></pre>

<p>This can be done in O(log2(N)) time. 
Compare this a regular ol' binary tree, 
which will be O(N). </p>

<p>REMEMBER: Check: Is it a BT or a BST? </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-printing-out-a-bst-alphabetically" class="anchor" href="#printing-out-a-bst-alphabetically" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Printing out a BST Alphabetically</h4>

<p>This is simple: </p>

<p>Use the in-order traversal pattern, as this will go
down the left, then the current nodes, then the right, 
which aligns perfectly with alphabetical order. </p>

<pre><code>void printBST(Node* cur)
{
    if (cur == nullptr)
        return; 

    printBST(cur-&gt;m_left);

    cout &lt;&lt; cur-&gt;m_value;

    printBST(cur-&gt;m_right);
}
</code></pre>

<p>This will be done in O(N) time, as we need to visit
all of the items. </p>

<p>This works for printing stuff out in numerical order too!</p>

<p>This is because in-order goes <em>in-order</em>:
the stuff that is less, then the middle, then the bigger. </p>

<p>// Ah, I never could handle the sugariness of Oreos</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-destructing-a-bst" class="anchor" href="#destructing-a-bst" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Destructing a BST</h4>

<p>Even the oldest of trees must die eventually. </p>

<p>But how do we do so? Every node will have to be
destructed because it was likely manually allocated.</p>

<p>Easy: use the post-order traversal pattern. The youngest
die first. </p>

<pre><code>void razeBST(Node* doomed)
{
    if (doomed == nullptr)
        return; 

    // Go for left subtree
    razeBST(doomed-&gt;m_left);

    // Go for right subtree
    razeBST(doomed-&gt;m_right);

    // Delete current node
    delete doomed;
}
</code></pre>

<p>Here, again, Big-O is O(N), as we must visit every node. </p>

<p>This allows us to avoid losing the left and right pointers. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-deletion-in-a-bst" class="anchor" href="#deletion-in-a-bst" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Deletion in a BST</h3>

<p>// 9,000 lines of code! </p>

<hr>

<p>STORY TIME: </p>

<p>The guy who pulled that joke on Carey (with all the 
scans running). </p>

<p>Carey was once an intern and had been working on something 
for a month. He booted up his computer one day: "No hard drive found." </p>

<p>He was thinking about he should have used version control. 
He opens the chassis, and found that his hard drive had been
disconnected. It was a prank. </p>

<p>He built a password sniffer, got his password, stored it
and said it whenever he walked by Sammie.</p>

<p>Sammie was in charge of the compilation and build process. </p>

<p>He came back from a night of partying. Carey wrote a program 
that would tweak all the assembly code to have syntax hours. 
It was only on his computer. It produced thousands and thousands
of errors when Sammie tried to run the build. Carey notes 
that he would be fired for such pranks these days. Symantec
was like a start-up in those days. </p>

<p>Carey: "Don't ask for permission, ask for forgiveness." </p>

<hr>

<p>To delete something from a BST, we need something to take
the place of the deleted node. How do we handle the re-linking? </p>

<p>If we don't handle the deletion properly, we'll break
the unique structure that characterizes a BST. </p>

<p>High-level algo: </p>

<p>Given a value V to delete:</p>

<pre><code>Find the value V in the tree with a standard BST search
{
    Use two pointers: a cur pointer and a parent pointer    

    If the node was found, delete it from the tree, preserving
    the structure
}
</code></pre>

<ol>
<li>
<p>Search for the value V: </p>

<p>Find the parent node of the doomed node</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Deletion has 3 cases:</p>

<ul>
<li>
<p>Node is a leaf node (this is easy)</p>

<ul>
<li>Delete the node, set the parent's child pointer to a nullptr</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>
<p>Our doomed node has only one child</p>

<ul>
<li>The grandchild jumps up to take the part of the child</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><p>Our doomed node has two children </p></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ol>

<p>Case 1: </p>

<pre><code>// Determine if it's the left or right
// child that is doomed    
if (parent-&gt;left == cur)
    parent-&gt;left = nullptr;
else if (parent-&gt;right == cur)
    parent-&gt;right = nullptr;

// Delete the node
delete cur; 
</code></pre>

<p>Special case: What if it's the root node that is doomed? </p>

<p>The parent pointer is nullptr</p>

<p>If the root node is a leaf (there's only one node in the BST), 
delete the current node (the root node) and set the root
pointer to nullptr.</p>

<p>Case 2: </p>

<pre><code>// Get a pointer to cur's only child
Node* grandchild; 

// Since this is case 2, we know that
// there is only one child node

if (cur-&gt;left != nullptr)
    grandchild = cur-&gt;left; 
else
    grandchild = cur-&gt;right; 

// Link parent to grandchild
// This left/right check maintains
// the BST structure

if (parent-&gt;left == cur)
    parent-&gt;left = grandchild;

else if (parent-&gt;right == cur)
    parent-&gt;right = grandchild; 

delete cur; 
</code></pre>

<p>If the doomed node is the root node (with a single child): </p>

<pre><code>// Get a pointer to cur's only child
Node* grandchild; 

// Since this is case 2, we know that
// there is only one child node

if (cur-&gt;left != nullptr)
    grandchild = cur-&gt;left; 
else
    grandchild = cur-&gt;right; 

rootPtr = grandchild;

delete cur; // Where cur == original root node
</code></pre>

<p>Case 3: </p>

<p>We need to select a replacement that will 
still leave the BST consistent (and we can't add anything). 
It's a sort of puzzle.</p>

<p>In general, when deleting a node K, we want to replace K
with either </p>

<ol>
<li><p>K's left subtree's largest-valued child</p></li>
<li><p>K's right subtree's smallest-valued child.</p></li>
</ol>

<p>This works out to preserve the structure. Either one
works.</p>

<p>It is actually two deletions, because we moving 
means we have to delete the moved node too. </p>

<p>Goal: Replace target node with a name that is
<code>&gt;</code> all names in left sub-tree or <code>&lt;</code> all names in right sub-tree</p>

<pre><code>ptr = cur-&gt; left
while (ptr-&gt;right != nullptr)
    ptr = ptr-&gt;right; 

Copy ptr's node's value into temp
Remove the node pointed to by ptr (the replacement) from the tree
    This is a recursive call, either case 1 or 2
    (as it's the rightmost or leftmost node;
    here it's the leftmost node)
Copy temp into cur
</code></pre>

<p>Case 3 deletion requires Case 1 or Case 2 deletion as well! </p>

<p>Then, the deletion will have been completed.</p>

<p>This WILL be on our final exam. Deletions in general will 
cause a classic BST to become more and more unbalanced. </p>

<p>No one uses classic BSTs these days; they just can't maintain
their efficiency. So instead, we'll use a balanced binary search
tree, which is what used in STL's ,  and . </p>

<p>Note that  and  are balanced search trees. </p>

<p>To print stuff out, it simply uses an in-order traversal
to print the items out in order. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-office-hours" class="anchor" href="#office-hours" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Office Hours</h3>

<p>// He's actuallly using the projector in this room today, 
// which is cool</p>

<p>// Jeffrey figured out another algorithm for BST node deletion 
// It heavily increases the depth of the tree though, 
// which decreases the efficiency of all of its operations</p>

<p>The old way of writing function pointers:</p>

<pre><code>insertion_sort(parameter, bool (*comp(...));
</code></pre>

<p>Smallberg wrote it </p>

<pre><code>insertion_sort(parameter, bool comp(...));
</code></pre>

<p>This lets us pass functions to functions. </p>

<p>This allows non-member functions to call upon other functions. </p>

<p>This lets us be more flexible with a function; we can
just change what sub-algo we pass into an algo, and instantly switch
out what it does. Note that the pointer function that we pass into 
had better have the same signature (return-type and parameters)
as we list in the function parameter. </p>

<p>e.g. :</p>

<pre><code>function(int parameter, string parameter, int sub_algo(char parameter1));
</code></pre>

<p>Then the sub_algo() looks like this: </p>

<pre><code>int sub_algo(char parameter1);
</code></pre>

<p>And the function call looks like this:</p>

<pre><code>function(5, "test", sub_algo('k'));
</code></pre>

<p>QUESTION: Can we templatize function pointers? </p>

<p>Duplicates: If it was a single item, then we could just maintain
counters at each node for duplicates. Most BSTs have more data
associated with each node though. </p>

<hr>

<p>Challenge problem: Found an O(N) way to sort an array of 
1 million random numbers whose values range from 1 to 5. </p>

<p>We could just go through and count the numbers of 1's, 2's, etc.,
then go back through the array and stick that many 1's, 2's, etc. in. </p>

<p>This is done in k * N time, where k is a constant (the range 
span, here 1-5). This beats O(nlog2(n)), the best time complexity 
we know of for data-sets where we don't know much about their contents.</p>

<p>With something like the 1 million 1's, 2's, 3's, etc., though,
we know a set of very specific contraints on the data, and can figure
a clever O(n) way to sort them. O(n) should be the lower bound 
to sort the data, as we have to look at all the data. Unless we have
something where data knows about other data and its order, 
O(n) should be the lower bound for sorting. </p>

<p>!nullptr == true</p>

<p>dynamic_cast: the cast will work if the intended result is 
the same thing or something derived from it. </p>

<p>"Are all [blah] are [blah]?" We cannot go up the hierarchy,
e.g. a subclass pointer cannot point to a superclass, so an
attempted dynamic_cast in that direction would not work. </p>
