<h2>
<a id="user-content-lecture-17-6-march-2014" class="anchor" href="#lecture-17-6-march-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lecture 17: 6 March 2014</h2>

<p>// Getting close to exceeding the number of lines
// of notes written for CS 31!
// I wrote 9,348 lines of notes for that class.
// Now, CS 32 will exceed it handily. 
// Strange to think that I've written over 18,000 lines
// of notes for CS</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-huffman-encoding" class="anchor" href="#huffman-encoding" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Huffman Encoding</h3>

<p>Huffman encoding is one data compression technique used
for compressing/decompressing files, the sort of thing
we use to create ZIP archives and what-not. </p>

<p>First, a review of ASCII. </p>

<p>ASCII is the encoding scheme used by most modern computers. 
In this schema, each character is stored in memory as a
number, e.g. 'A' is 65. </p>

<p>This abstraction is merely a nice cover for how everything
is actually stored and handled as binary bits instead. </p>

<p>Huffman encoding works off the fact that many things repeat
sequences of bits. Also, note that this is a <em>lossless</em> compression
method.</p>

<p>Its general algo: </p>

<ol>
<li>Compute the frequency of each character</li>
<li>Build a Huffman tree (a binary tree) based on these frequencies</li>
<li>Use this binary tree to convert the original file's contents 
to a more compressed form</li>
<li>Save the converted (compressed) data to a file</li>
</ol>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-count-frequency" class="anchor" href="#count-frequency" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Count Frequency</h4>

<p>The first step is really like computing a histogram, 
a chart that records how frequently something shows up. </p>

<p>These are common in early elementary:</p>

<pre><code>// The frequency with which the 
// digits 1, 2 &amp; 3 appear in some larger number,
// as one arbitrary example 

        x
x       x
x   x   x
x   x   x

1   2   3
</code></pre>

<p>For Huffman encoding, we'll probably have something like:</p>

<pre><code>CHARACTER      FREQUENCY
---------      ---------

   'U'             5
   'C'             9
   'L'             2
   'A'             4
   '!'             3
</code></pre>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-build-a-huffman-tree" class="anchor" href="#build-a-huffman-tree" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Build a Huffman Tree</h4>

<p>To build a Huffman tree based on those frequencies:</p>

<p>NOTE: A Huffman tree is a <em>binary tree</em>, NOT a BST. </p>

<pre><code>Create a binary tree leaf node for each entry
in the frequency count table, but do NOT
insert any into a tree (yet)

While we have more than one node left: 

    Find the two nodes with lowest frequencies

    Create a new parent node

    Link the parent to each of the children

    Set the parent's total frequency equal to
    the sum of its children's frequencies

    Place the new parent node in our grouping

Now label each left edege with a 0
each right edge with a 1
</code></pre>

<p>The bit encoding for a character is the path of
0's and 1's taken from the root for three to the character
of interest. </p>

<p>Multiple trees are possible (depending on how we
grouped nodes together, as there can be ties), so multiple encoding
schemes are possible. Note how it uses <em>fewer</em> bits for more common
characters. In addition, the encodings have <em>unique prefixes</em>. 
It is unambiguous in this encoding as to what encoding lines up
to what character. This can be done with groups of characters too. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-compression" class="anchor" href="#compression" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Compression</h4>

<p>Now use the binary tree to convert the original file's contents
into a more compressed form, i.e. find the sequence of bits
(1's and 0's) for each char in the message. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-save-the-archive" class="anchor" href="#save-the-archive" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Save the Archive</h4>

<p>Now take this compressed sequence and save it to a file,
one that will be much smaller than the original file. </p>

<p>Of course, we need to attach the encoding scheme used,
or else we won't know what each sequence of bits was
supposed to represent. We need to encode the tree in
a way: each tree was unique, so it's added <em>overhead</em>. </p>

<p>For small enough stuff, that overhead would push
the "compressed" file over the size of the original,
for larger files, we can save 40-80% of disk space! </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-decoding" class="anchor" href="#decoding" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Decoding</h4>

<p>To decode, we: </p>

<ol>
<li><p>Extract the encoding scheme from the compressed file</p></li>
<li><p>Build a Huffman tree (a binary tree based on the encodings)
Follow the 0's and 1's, as they indicate left/right, and
insert a new node there.</p></li>
<li><p>Use this binary tree to convert the compressed file's contents
back to the original characters
Go until we hit a leaf, then output the character located there.</p></li>
<li><p>Save the converted (uncompressed) data to a file</p></li>
</ol>

<p>ZIP files use an even more effective compression. </p>

<p>The best we can do here is compress from 1 byte to a bit. 
If we compress larger characters, we can save even more space. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-balanced-binary-search-trees" class="anchor" href="#balanced-binary-search-trees" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Balanced Binary Search Trees</h3>

<hr>

<p>To insert: O(log2(N))</p>

<p>To search: O(log2(N))</p>

<p>To erase: O(log2(N))</p>

<p>Traversal: O(N)</p>

<p>BBSTs are <em>O(log2(N))</em> for everything except
for traversal. Note how they don't have the worst-case
O(N) possibility, unlike a normal BST. </p>

<hr>

<p>We've noticed that BSTs become less efficient over time
as we insert and delete nodes. Often, this is because
they linearize out and deepen in their depth. Ideally,
we want to make as few visits to nodes as possible to get
to stuff and get stuff done. We don't want a pathological 
trail that takes forever to traverse. We want a bushy Christmas tree. </p>

<p>We want to maintain an <em>even tree</em>.</p>

<p>Definition: A binary tree is <em>perfectly balanced</em> if
for each node, the number of nodes in its <em>left and right subtrees</em>
<em>differ by at most one</em>.</p>

<p>Three popular approaches exist for building a BBST:</p>

<ul>
<li>AVL trees</li>
<li>2-3 trees</li>
<li>Red-black trees</li>
</ul>

<p>We're doing AVL; look up the others sometime. There are many others
too. They shift nodes around as needed to balance the tree.</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-avl-trees" class="anchor" href="#avl-trees" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>AVL Trees</h4>

<p>AVL Trees are BSTs in which the height of the left and right
sub-trees of <em>each node</em> differ by <em>at most 1</em>.</p>

<p>Note the difference in balancing requirements. </p>

<p>They are implemented by assigning each node a 
<em>balance value</em>:</p>

<p>0: the node's right and left-subtrees have the same height
-1: the node's left subtree is 1 higher than the right
1: the node's right subtree is 1 higher than the left</p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-insertion-1" class="anchor" href="#insertion-1" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Insertion</h5>

<p>Case 1:</p>

<p>After we insert a new node into the AVL tree,
<em>all</em> nodes in the tree still have balances of -1, 0, or 1.</p>

<p>In this case:</p>

<ol>
<li>Insert the new node normally (just like in a vanilla BST)</li>
<li>Update the balances of all the parent nodes, from
the new leaf to the root</li>
<li>If all of the balances are still 0, -1, or 1, done!</li>
</ol>

<p>Case 2: We insert a new node, and it causes one or more
balances to become less than -1 or greater than 1</p>

<p>This requires us to <em>rebalance</em> the tree to the range
of acceptable balances. </p>

<p>There are two-subcases: single &amp; double rotation</p>

<p>In <em>single rotation</em>, some nodes are rotated over once. 
In <em>double rotation</em>, two rebalances are required. </p>

<p>There are at most two shifts in an AVL tree. </p>

<p>Note that the STL  and  classes use
BBSTs (though not necessarily AVL trees). </p>

<p>Know that rebalancing (even twice) is <em>O(log2(N))</em>, so 
insertion is still <em>O(log2(N))</em>.</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-the-modulus-operator" class="anchor" href="#the-modulus-operator" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>The Modulus Operator</h3>

<p>Time for Carey's favorite data structure, 
<em>hash tables</em>. </p>

<p>O(log2(N) + L)</p>

<p>Reminder: % operator returns the remainder
of dividing something by something: </p>

<p>1234 % 100 == 34</p>

<p>100 goes into 1234 twelve times, remainder 34</p>

<p>Note an interesting property of finding the remainder
of a division: </p>

<p>E.g. N % 5:</p>

<p>0 % 5 = 0
1 % 5 = 1 (5 doesn't go into 1 at all)
2 % 5 = 2
3 % 5 = 3
4 % 5 = 4
5 % 5 = 0 (clean division)
6 % 5 = 1
7 % 5 = 2
8 % 5 = 3
9 % 5 = 4
10 % 5 = 0
...</p>

<p>Note that modulo of a number returns a range of numbers
guaranteed to be between 0 and that number N - 1, 
so <em>(variable % N) ranges from 0 - (N - 1)</em></p>

<p>To get a specific range: </p>

<pre><code>% range + beginning

range = (max - min)
</code></pre>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-hash-tables" class="anchor" href="#hash-tables" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Hash Tables</h3>

<p>Thus far, the most efficient ADT we know
of thus far is a balanced binary search tree,
O(log2(N)), for everything.</p>

<p>Can we do better? How about the audacious goal
of attaining <em>O(1)</em>? </p>

<p>Let's begin with a pseudo-predecessor to a true
hash table:</p>

<p>What if we wanted to create an ADT that could
store all the UCLA Student IDs (9 digits),
where the indices are the ID numbers. That
would a 1 billion element array!</p>

<p>To add a new ID#: set array[N] = true</p>

<p>We now know that that ID is active
when we go straight to it. Accessing
an element in an array is done in constant-time. </p>

<p>This is insanely fast--but insanely expensive
in terms of memory. We only have ~50,000 students,
but have 1 billion element array. We're wasting
999,950,000 slots just to know if a student ID
is active or not. </p>

<p>So let's reduce the number of slots required.</p>

<p>NachenSmall Corp. dictates that our program must
use 100,000 slots. </p>

<p>We need a mathmatical function that can take in
an ID# and somehow converts it to a unique
slot number between 0 and 99,999 in the new, smaller
array. </p>

<p>Our domain is [0, 1 billion - 1] and the range
is [0, 99,999] for our function.</p>

<p>f(x): [0, 1 billion - 1] -&gt; [0, 99,999]</p>

<p>This will be our <em>hash function</em> for converting between
the original and something smaller and more compact. </p>

<p>To add a new item: </p>

<pre><code>void addItem(int n)
{
    int slot = hashFunc(n);
    m_array[slot] = true; 
}
</code></pre>

<p>Or: <code>m_array[hashFunc(n)] = true;</code></p>

<p>Slots in hash tables are called <em>buckets</em>. </p>

<pre><code>int hashFunc(int idNum)
{
    int bucket = idNum % ARRAY_SIZE;
    return bucket;
}
</code></pre>

<p>This function will return values between 
0 and 99,999, perfect for our new, smaller array. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-hash-collisions" class="anchor" href="#hash-collisions" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Hash Collisions</h3>

<p>Note how we may end up with the same hashes.
This is known as a <strong>collision</strong>, and this is a problem.</p>

<p>For us, this happens when the last five digits
of the ID number are the same.</p>

<p>We have <em>ambiguity</em>: which ID number are we trying
to represent? </p>

<p>A collision is where two or more values both 
"hash" to the same bucket. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-closed-hash-table-with-linear-probing" class="anchor" href="#closed-hash-table-with-linear-probing" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Closed Hash Table with Linear Probing</h3>

<p>To deal with collisions, this is one possibility. </p>

<p>We figure out a target bucket via that hash function.</p>

<p>If the target buck is empty, we store our value there,
but we store the <em>full original value</em>, not the hash.</p>

<p>This prevents ambiguity. If the bucket is occupied,
we scan down until we find the first
empty bucket. Put the value there. </p>

<p>Here, insertion and searching is O(N) in the worst case,
where the table is almost filled-up, and there are
many collisions. </p>

<p>To create a hash table, we 
<em>must consider the size of our intended dataset</em>.</p>

<p>To search, we compute our bucket number with our
hash function, and we <em>probe linearly</em> downward
until we find our value or hit an <em>empty bucket</em>;
the latter case means that the <em>value is not in the hash table</em>.
This will wrap around (thanks to modulo)
to try to insert in the earlier buckets too, if possible.</p>

<p>In general, this approach addresses collision
by putting each value <em>as close as possible</em>
to its intended bucket. We have <em>locality</em>. </p>

<p>And, since we store the original value, there
is no ambiguity. </p>

<p>The hash table is called "closed" because it has
a <em>fixed-size</em>. Example: a night-club has a limited
capacity. Once we run out of empty buckets, we cannot
add new values. We'd have to create a new hash table
and copy over the old values by re-hashing (to accommodate
the new size of the larger hash table). </p>

<p>In code: </p>

<pre><code>struct Bucket
{
    int idNum; // The value we store
    bool used; // Is the bucket empty? 
               // Initialize this to TRUE
}; 
</code></pre>

<p>We could also choose to terminate search
or insertion operations after a certain number of 
tries if we know that our hash table couldn't be that bad.</p>

<p>What about searching and collisions? We stored the
full, original value! We search linearly, 
and in the worst case, check all N buckets to see if
our value is present, wrapping around to the beginning
as needed. </p>

<p>Note that recursion is pretty much never used with 
hash tables. Do it iteratively. </p>

<p>The cool thing about hash tables? We can store 
whatever the hell we want in them. We could have
a comprehensive database, which we can search 
by a particular field, the one that was hashed
to buckets.</p>

<p>We search by the hashed field, and can then grab
the other fields' data. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-deletion-1" class="anchor" href="#deletion-1" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Deletion</h4>

<p>What happens if we just zero-out a particular position? </p>

<p>Our search algorithm will stop prematurely (when we're
looking for a value that has collided with another and
and have put the collisions in later buckets) because
we hit an empty bucket. </p>

<p>The moral: <em>Use closed hash tables where we</em>
<em>never need to delete anything</em>. We are limited
by the fact that we need to account for the hashing.
We'd end up with all sorts of special cases and 
extra complexity. </p>

<p>Examples: dictionary, a list of the dead (no zombies!)</p>

<p>If we delete where a collision, we may prematurely 
abort a search, failing to find the sought-for value. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-open-hash-table" class="anchor" href="#open-hash-table" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Open Hash Table</h3>

<p>Now let's see another way to deal with collisions,
one that avoids a closed hash table's problems
with deletion and size capping. </p>

<p>Now, our buckets will point to a linked list
of values. </p>

<p>To insert: </p>

<ol>
<li><p>Compute a bucket number with our hash function</p></li>
<li><p>Add the new value to the linked list at array[bucket]</p></li>
<li><p>Done!</p></li>
</ol>

<p>Now, it's constant time to get to a bucket, 
but to traverse the linked list, it's O(L). </p>

<p>There is no a priori size limit to the open hash table. 
It'll slow down with too many items and collisions though. </p>

<p>To search: </p>

<ol>
<li><p>Compute bucket # with hash function</p></li>
<li><p>Search the linked list at array[bucket]</p></li>
<li><p>If we hit the end of the linked list,
the item is not in our table!</p></li>
</ol>

<p>To delete: </p>

<ol>
<li>Go to the right bucket using the hash function</li>
<li>Remove the node from the linked list </li>
</ol>

<p>Note that closed hash tables are faster for when
we know our maximum size, and we avoid the dynamic
allocation, deallocation of open hash tables. </p>

<p><strong>If we need to delete, we must use a open hash table.</strong></p>

<p>If we size the hash table large enough, then we avoid 
multiple collisions, and our average Big-O will 
be ~O(1) in practice. O(1) on our test. </p>

<p>In the worst-case (everything is a collision), then
it's O(N). </p>

<p>We could of course store the items in a BST or BBST instead
for even faster operation. That adds complexity though.</p>

<p>Or: a hash table within a hash table!</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-hash-table-efficiency" class="anchor" href="#hash-table-efficiency" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Hash Table Efficiency</h3>

<p>Efficiency depends on </p>

<p>A. Type of hash table
B. How full the hash table is
C. How many collisions are in the hash table</p>

<p>If we have a completely or nearly empty hash table: 
O(1) for everything. (Go to right bucket, insert/search)</p>

<p>We avoid linear probing (closed) or linear traversal (open). </p>

<p>BUT, if our hash table is nearly full: 
it may now be O(N) for insertion and searching. </p>

<p>So our trade-off is between hash table size and efficiency. </p>

<p>The <strong>load</strong> of a hash table is the max number of intended values
divided by the number of buckets to be available. </p>

<p>This is almost always &lt; 1.</p>

<p><strong>L = max/buckets</strong>. </p>

<p>Example: 0.1 = our hash table has 10 more buckets than needed
(we avoid collisions)</p>

<p>To compute the <em>average number of tries</em> needed to insert/find
an item for a <em>closed table with linear probing</em>: </p>

<pre><code>1/2(1 + 1/(1 - L)) for L &lt; 1.0
</code></pre>

<p>For an <em>open hash table</em>:</p>

<pre><code>1 + L/2 for L &lt; 1.0
</code></pre>

<p>There's not too big a difference between the two (they'll be around
~O(1)). </p>

<p>We would obviously try to avoid loading up the hash table
too heavily. </p>

<p>Remember: dynamic size with deletion too? Open hash table. </p>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-lecture-18-10-march-2014" class="anchor" href="#lecture-18-10-march-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lecture 18: 10 March 2014</h2>

<p>// Definitely beat the number of lines of notes for CS 31</p>

<p>Focus for final will be on material that was new
or not covered on the past two midterms. It will
be more analytical. </p>

<p>Review of hash tables load factor. Note that hash
tables are best for when we know how many items
we'll need to handle, max. If we do not know, then 
some other data structure would do better. </p>

<p>Why would we use a closed hash table rather than an
open hash table? Because allocation is slow in comparison 
to the fast, constant-time insertion into a closed hash table. </p>

<p>Also, hash tables are terrible for traversing something in 
order, e.g. the names we manage in our Database for Project 4. </p>

<p>For searching, however, the totally beat everything.</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-sizing-a-hashtable" class="anchor" href="#sizing-a-hashtable" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Sizing a Hashtable</h3>

<p>To size a hashtable appropriately: </p>

<ol>
<li>Determine the max number of items</li>
<li>Determine the average number of searches we want
to find an item in this hash table type (closed or open)</li>
<li>Solve for the load factor</li>
<li>Load factor is max/buckets; solve for number of buckets</li>
</ol>

<p>We're working backward to get the number of buckets. </p>

<p>We're looking for an effective trade-off between 
memory usage and fast searches. It all depends on our
constraints. </p>

<p>Always try to choose a <em>prime</em> number of items.
This allows for a more even distribution and fewer collisions.
It'll divide evenly into fewer numbers. </p>

<p>Example: 2,021 instead of 2,000</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-hashing-non-numbers" class="anchor" href="#hashing-non-numbers" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Hashing Non-Numbers</h3>

<p>What if we want to hash a string? </p>

<p>We need a hash() function that will give us numbers. </p>

<p>Example: </p>

<pre><code>int hash(string&amp; name)
{
    string name; 

    for (int i = 0; i &lt; name.length(); i++)
        total = total + name[i];

    total = total % HASH_TABLE_SIZE;

    return total;
}
</code></pre>

<p>But this just adds up the encoding values for 
the letters, and so this produces the same result
for different strings, e.g. BAT and TAB. </p>

<p>Those are different strings, and we should get
different hashes for them, but this hash function doesn't
do that, so this means we will have a lot of collisions.</p>

<p>Alternatives:</p>

<pre><code>total = total + (i+1) * name[i];
</code></pre>

<p>Note that this could cause overflow problems
where the string.size() * blah &gt; INT_MAX.</p>

<p>There will always be collisions, no matter what our 
hashing scheme is. Hash tables just have to deal with 
that reality. This one does eliminate the problem with
anagrams hashing to the same value though.</p>

<ol>
<li>A hash function must <em>always</em> give the same bucket number
for a given input value. </li>
</ol>

<p>We do NOT want random hashes!</p>

<ol>
<li><p>The hash function should disperse items throughout the 
hash array as randomly as possible. </p></li>
<li><p>When coming up with a new hash function, always
measure how well it disperses items (do experiments). </p></li>
</ol>

<p>Create a graph of the number of items per bucket number. </p>

<pre><code>x
x x x x x
1 2 3 4 5 
</code></pre>

<p>vs.</p>

<p>Try CRC32, CRC64, SHA2, etc., but MD5, etc. are not secure
enough cryptographically. These are open-source hashing functions.</p>

<p>Note that we still have add our own modulo based on our table
size. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-hash-table-vs-bsts" class="anchor" href="#hash-table-vs-bsts" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Hash Table vs. BSTs</h3>

<p>Speed: O(1) vs. O(log2(N))</p>

<p>Simplicity: Simple vs. complex</p>

<p>Max size: Limited (closed HT), 
          or high load impedes performance</p>

<p>vs. the consistency of BSTs under high load,
especially if balanced.</p>

<p>If we want to exapnd a hash table, it's 
like creating a whole new hash table,
though there are incremental expansion schemes. </p>

<p>Space efficiency: HTs can waste space; BSTs use
only as much space as needed</p>

<p>Ordering: HTs are random; BSTs are inherently ordered</p>

<p>Just searching: HT
Traversing or order required in some way: BST</p>

<p>What is the cost of sorting items? Nlog2(N),
even with our fastest sorting algorithms.</p>

<p>To get items in order with a HT, we'd have to sort
fast. </p>

<p>What if we're mostly searching rather than doing 
stuff in order? This favors using a hash table. 
Conversely, if order is of greater importance, use 
a BST. It all depends on the <em>use case</em>. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-tables" class="anchor" href="#tables" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Tables</h3>

<p>Say we want to keep tracks of all our friends
and info on them. </p>

<p>A group of related data is called a <strong>record</strong> 
or a <strong>row of data</strong>. </p>

<p>Each record has a number of <strong>fields</strong> like name, 
age, etc. </p>

<p>A collection of records is called a <em>table</em>. </p>

<p>We may have many records that share some fields, 
but a field with a unique value for every record
is called a <em>key field</em>. </p>

<p>Have a struct/class represent a record, then have a
collection of them be our table (e.g. vector, array, etc.)</p>

<p>// 10,000 lines! </p>

<p>In our Project, we don't happen to have a key field. </p>

<p>A <strong>table</strong> is an ADT onto itself. It is designed 
to hold records conforming to a particular <strong>schema</strong>
that specifies the format of records and their fields.</p>

<p>To search a table by a particular field, we could
do simple linear search, but this is O(N). </p>

<p>Instead, we could sort and do a binary search. 
That'd be O(log2(N)). This is a problem for insertion,
of course, as we have to re-sort every time we insert. 
That's an overheard of a O(Nlog2(N)).</p>

<p>This would also lock in our searching abilities. 
If everything is ordered by name, how would we search by 
name or phone number? Searching by one field would be
O(log2(N)), but everything else would be linear, O(N). </p>

<p>What about storing our records in a binary search tree? 
This avoids re-sorting, but we still are limited to efficient
search by one particular field. </p>

<p>We could create separate BSTs, one organized by one field,
one organized by another field, but this is duplicating
data, wasting space. </p>

<p>Intead, we'll still use a vector to store all our records.</p>

<p>Then, we'll have a secondary data structure that associates
a field with their slot number in the vector. </p>

<p>This secondary structure lets quickly look up a name, and
find out where that person and all the associated data are in the 
records structure. BST-based Maps or MultiMaps are perfect
for associating indexed fields to their slot numbers.</p>

<p>These secondary data structures are called <strong>indices</strong>
(sing. <strong>index</strong>), which use B-Trees and let us know where
data is stored. </p>

<p>However, note that we are still duplicating some data, 
the indexed fields. </p>

<p>Once we know the slot number, a -like structure
is perfect for constant-time access to the particular
item. </p>

<p>Why do we use slot numbers and not pointers to items? 
We can't fit everything in memory (databases are typically
massive). </p>

<p>What happens if we have to delete a record? 
Updating all the indices and such would be a pain.
Instead, we could put a <em>tombstone</em> marker in the place of
deleted item, to let us know that the record is available.</p>

<hr>

<p>// PROJECT 4 NOTES: </p>

<p>For findEqualSuccessor, check current, then check 
the one before it, for predecessor then check current,
and then check one after it. </p>

<p>1, 2, [2.5] 3, 4, 5 
   P        S</p>

<p>Do this iteratively!</p>

<p>STORY TIME</p>

<p>There was a computer program called Wisdom,
that would randomly generate paragraphs, footnotes,
etc., and it could generate a grammatically-correct
paper. So Carey generated the conclusion to his physics
paper using this. Recently, some guys have generated
random research papers and showing how this nonsense 
was getting accepted at conferences. This illustrates
the lameness of academia. </p>

<hr>

<p>We have as many indexes as we need for our
particular table. </p>

<p>Note that a  can use the brackets [] operator.  </p>

<pre><code>m_IDtoSlot[student.number] = slot;
</code></pre>

<p>To insert: </p>

<p>Add the record to the records data structure. 
Update the index data structures as needed
for each of our indexed fields. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-hash-based-indices" class="anchor" href="#hash-based-indices" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Hash-Based Indices</h4>

<p>Of course, we could use hash tables
to search fields and associated hash numbers. </p>

<p>Modern databases use either one, depending on whether
order matters. </p>

<p>Understand how our table will be used, which will
let us decide how to index each field. If we do
later need to output stuff in order, we can
always just run a sort. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content--and-" class="anchor" href="#-and-" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a> and </h3>

<p>This is a hash-based version of a , a "hash-map" (older name). 
There's a hash-based  as well, a "hash-set". </p>

<p>We can use this for our project!</p>

<p>This is inserting items into a hash table, but each
item has data associated with it (or two data, as with
an ). This allows constant-time operations
with everything. Again, decide if we need to output stuff
in order. </p>

<p>These will resize automatically and they allow deletion
as well, suggesting they use a open hash table. </p>

<p>They're "unordered" because HTs are unordered.</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-priority-queue" class="anchor" href="#priority-queue" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Priority Queue</h3>

<p>In a regular queue, FIFO, first-come, first-served. </p>

<p>Instead, each item in a PQ has a <strong>priority rating</strong>.
We always dequeue the item with the highest rating. </p>

<p>They support a new item, get the value if the highest
priority item, remove the highest priority item. </p>

<p>We need to sepcify how to determine the priority
of each item in the queue. e.g. Doctor determining
which injured person to treat first. </p>

<p>We could do this with a BBST, but what if we a had a
limited set of priorities (high, medium, low)? </p>

<p>What's a basic data structure to handle this? </p>

<p>Three linked lists: one for each priority rating.
Think of seating classes for First Class, Business, Coach
for airplanes. </p>

<p>But what if we have a large number of priorities? </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-heaps" class="anchor" href="#heaps" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Heaps</h3>

<p>This is not the heap, as in the memory location. </p>

<p>Instead, a <strong>heap</strong> uses a <strong>complete binary tree</strong>,
useful for priority queues, in which (assuming N depth):</p>

<ul>
<li><p>The top N - 1 levels of the tree are completely
filled with nodes. </p></li>
<li><p>The bottom-most node are as far left as possible, 
with no empty slots between nodes</p></li>
</ul>

<p>These are NOT BSTs! </p>

<p>There are two types of heaps: <strong>minheaps</strong>
and <strong>maxheaps</strong>. </p>

<p>Maxheap:</p>

<ol>
<li>Quickly insert a new item (Olog2(N))</li>
<li>Quickly retrieve the largest item from the heap</li>
</ol>

<p>Minheap: </p>

<ol>
<li>Quickly insert a new item</li>
<li>Quickly retrieve the smallest item from the heap</li>
</ol>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-maxheap" class="anchor" href="#maxheap" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Maxheap</h4>

<p>Maxheap BT follows these rules:</p>

<ol>
<li>The value contained by a node is always &gt;= values of its children</li>
<li>The tree is a COMPLETE binary tree.</li>
</ol>

<p>Minheap would reverse the 1st rule to &lt;=.</p>

<p>This is not a BST, so only the immediate node family 
matters. </p>

<p>Note how the biggest value is <em>guaranteed</em> to be at
the top of the tree. </p>

<p>So peeking at the top value is O(1). </p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-extracting-the-biggest-item" class="anchor" href="#extracting-the-biggest-item" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Extracting the Biggest Item</h5>

<ol>
<li><p>If the tree is empty, return ERROR</p></li>
<li><p>Otherwise, the top item in the tree is the biggest value
Remember it for later</p></li>
<li><p>If the heap has only one node, then delete it and
return the saved value</p></li>
<li><p>Otherwise, copy the value from the right-most 
node in the bottom-most row to the node</p></li>
<li><p>Delete the <em>right-most node in the bottom-most level</em>
(Choosing this node keeps things left-justified, to avoid creating gaps). </p></li>
<li>
<p>Repeatedly swap the just-moved value with the larger
of its two children until its value is greater than
or equal to both of its children (<strong>sifting down</strong>). </p>

<p>If it's already &gt;=, then we never move it. 
In the end, the second-biggest item from before
is now the biggest item, and has been moved to the top, 
maintaining the validity of the maxheap. This lets
us handle any number of priorities, where the maxheap
inserts based on priority rating. </p>
</li>
<li><p>Return the value from earlier to the caller</p></li>
</ol>

<p>This will be on our final exam. Make it easy by drawing
diagrams. </p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-adding-a-node-to-the-maxheap" class="anchor" href="#adding-a-node-to-the-maxheap" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Adding a Node to the Maxheap</h5>

<ol>
<li><p>If the tree is empty, create a new root node and return</p></li>
<li><p>Otherwise, insert the new node in the bottom-most
left-most position (to maintain left justification for 
completeness)</p></li>
<li><p>Compare the new value with its parent valye</p></li>
<li><p>If the new value is greater than the parent's value,
then swap them</p></li>
<li><p>Repeat steps 3-4 until the new value rises to its proper
place, which might be the root node (meaning it was
bigger than the original largest value)&gt; </p></li>
</ol>

<p><strong>Reheapification</strong> is the name for this process.</p>

<p>Big-O is <strong>log2(N)</strong> for insertion/removal.</p>

<h5>
<a id="user-content-array-based-implementation" class="anchor" href="#array-based-implementation" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Array-Based Implementation</h5>

<p>We never actually use a BST with pointers 
for heaps. It's hard to locate the appropriate locations. </p>

<p>Instead, we'll use an array. </p>

<p><strong>We know that each level of our tree has 2x (twice)</strong>
<strong>the number of the nodes of the previous level.</strong></p>

<p>We can think of translating a complete binary tree (CBT)
over to an array, one level at a time.</p>

<p>If we just have our nodes, one level at a time, in
an array, we know where each level starts. This how
the memory heap works. What about sizing the heap?
Either know the size ahead of time, or use a 
or something else that can resize dyanmically. </p>

<p>We use a simple int variable to track how many items
are in our heap. What about finding the children and parent? </p>

<ol>
<li><p>We can always find the root value in <code>heap[0]</code></p></li>
<li><p>The bottom-most, right-most node in <code>heap[count - 1]</code></p></li>
<li><p>We can always find the bottom-most, left-most 
empty spot (to add a new value) in <code>heap[count]</code></p></li>
<li><p>We can add/remove a node by simply setting
<code>heap[count] = value</code> and/or updating the count.</p></li>
</ol>

<p>This is better than a sorted array that we resort
at each insertion. That'd be be O(Nlog2(N)), vs. this,
which is O(log2(N)).</p>

<p>To locate a node's children:</p>

<p>(given the slot number of the parent)</p>

<p><strong>left child(parent) =  2 * parent + 1</strong>
<strong>right child(parant) = 2 * parent + 2</strong></p>

<p>2i + 1, 2i + 2 are the simple, easy formulas,
where i is the slot number of the parent</p>

<p>// This can be derived from manually listing
// the translations</p>

<p>To find the parent node:</p>

<p><strong>parentSlot(childSlot) = (childSlot - 1 ) / 2</strong></p>

<p>(i - 1) / 2 works because of integer division. </p>

<p>Reheapification is done immediately upon insertion,
which we do by comparing to the parent (which we find 
via these formulas). </p>

<p>Summary: </p>

<ul>
<li>Root node at heap[0]</li>
<li>Bottom-right at heap[count - 1]</li>
<li>Botom-left at heap[count]</li>
</ul>

<p>This is all we need to build a heap. </p>

<p>Recommendation: Use a tree, then copy over the values
from the tree back into the array, to make things
easier. </p>

<p>"This is not a heap. This is a heap helper. 
This is not a hamburger, this is a Hamburger Helper." </p>

<h6>
<a id="user-content-extraction-with-an-array-based-heap" class="anchor" href="#extraction-with-an-array-based-heap" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Extraction with an array-based Heap</h6>

<ol>
<li><p>If the count == 0, return ERROR</p></li>
<li><p>Otherwise, heap[0] holds biggest value</p></li>
<li><p>IF count == 1, set count = 0, return the saved node</p></li>
<li><p>Otherwise, copy the value from the rightmost, bottom-most node
into the root node
heap[0] = heap[count - 1]</p></li>
<li><p>Delete the right-most node in the bottom-most
by decrementing our count</p></li>
<li><p>Compare our newly-copied value to its two children
heap[i] vs heap[2 * i + 1] and heap[2 * i + 2]</p></li>
<li><p>Swap if necessary, till we're done or hit the root node</p></li>
</ol>

<h2>
<a id="user-content-lecture-19-12-march-2014" class="anchor" href="#lecture-19-12-march-2014" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Lecture 19: 12 March 2014</h2>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-last-day-of-class" class="anchor" href="#last-day-of-class" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Last Day of Class</h3>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-adding-node-to-an-array-based-maxheap" class="anchor" href="#adding-node-to-an-array-based-maxheap" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Adding Node to an Array-based Maxheap</h3>

<ol>
<li><p>Insert a new node in the bottom-most,
left-most open slot: <code>heap[count] = value</code>
<code>count = count + 1</code></p></li>
<li><p>Compre the value with its parent value.
Swap if out of o</p></li>
<li><p>If the new value is greater than parent,
swap</p></li>
<li><p>Repeat</p></li>
</ol>

<p>Class challenge. "Do I get a prize ticket?"
"I don't think they're useful anymore." </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-big-o-of-heap-insertion" class="anchor" href="#big-o-of-heap-insertion" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Big-O of Heap Insertion</h3>

<p>We need to comparing, and we need to check
up the ancestors, so <strong>O(log2(N))</strong> for insertion.</p>

<p>Extraction: <strong>O(log2(N))</strong></p>

<p>Since we need to compare and swap. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-heapsort" class="anchor" href="#heapsort" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Heapsort</h3>

<p>Heapsort is guaranteed to at least Nlog2(N)),
the naïve one that uses a separate heap.</p>

<p>The real heapsort doesn't use a separate array. 
That naïve implemenation had a lot of overhead. </p>

<p>Instead, we can do everything within the array. </p>

<p>This semi-official is not quite as efficient as the fully
official one, then we'll see the altter. </p>

<ol>
<li>Convert our input array into a maxheap (shuffle around)</li>
<li>While are numbers left in the array
A. Remove the biggest value from the heap
b. Place it in the last open slot of the array</li>
</ol>

<p>To cleverly shuffle around the values: </p>

<p>Start by visualizing our array as a tree. 
Note, this algo operates on the array itself. </p>

<pre><code>for (curNode = lastNode through rootNode) [rightmost, bottommost,
                                           last item in array]
{
    Focus on subtree at curNode

    Think of this subtree as a maxheap

    Keep shifting the top value down until your subtree
    becomes a valid maxheap [use heapification algo
    where we swap with the larger child until the current
    is larger than both of its children] 

    // Move to next Node
}
</code></pre>

<p>Start at the bottom, keep heapfiying from bottom to top.
Check that the maxheap tree is correct. </p>

<p>We've heapified higher and higher subtrees, relying on the 
lower subtrees.</p>

<p>Note that we wasted a lot of time looking at single-node subtrees. </p>

<p>We only need to re-heapify when we reached a subtree with at least
two nodes. To jump to that: </p>

<pre><code>startNode = N/2 - 1; // Lowest one down, right-most with 2 children

for (curNode = startNode thru rootNode)
</code></pre>

<p>Jump to that slot in the array, and corrolate with the tree nodes. </p>

<p>Note that at least half of a complete binary tree has no children,
because of the bottom row. With this, we skip 50% of the reheapification,
(N/2 items), so this lets us increase efficiency. </p>

<p>While there are numbers left in the heap:</p>

<ol>
<li><p>Extract the biggest value from the maxheap and re-heapify
This frees up the last slot in the array, and the heap now
occupies only the first N - 1 slots. </p></li>
<li><p>Put the extracted value into this freed-up slot of the array.</p></li>
</ol>

<p>Next time, we'll extract, leaving the second-to-last slot free. </p>

<p>Decrement the count as part of the extraction; this is how we
know when we're done.</p>

<p>Minheap would give a descending order. </p>

<p>Extraction, reheapification, insertion -&gt; in order! </p>

<p>Converting an array into a maxheap is O(N) because of
how we skip 50% of the nodes. </p>

<p>"I found it on Wikipedia so it must be true." </p>

<p>Extraction and reheapifying is O(log2(N))</p>

<p>So O(N + Nlog2(N)) --&gt; <strong>O(Nlog2(N))</strong></p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-introduction-to-graphs" class="anchor" href="#introduction-to-graphs" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Introduction to Graphs</h3>

<p>A <strong>graph</strong> is an ADT that stores a set of <strong>entities</strong>
and keeps track of the <strong>relationships</strong> between all of them. </p>

<p>Examples: People and relationships, cities and distances.</p>

<p>It might not just be a connection, but a value associated with
that connections. The edge has a value.</p>

<p>Web pages and links between them (Google Page Rank). </p>

<p><strong>Vertices (nodes)</strong> are the people, city, web pages, etc.</p>

<p><strong>Edges (arcs)</strong> connect two vertices to each other. </p>

<p>Edges typically connect two vertices, but self-connections
are also possible, e.g. a bus on a looping route. </p>

<p>There are <strong>directed graphs</strong> where each edge goes from 
one vertex to another in a specific direction. It
goesn't go both ways, e.g. one-way flight.  A--&gt;B</p>

<p>THere are <strong>undirected graph</strong>, where all edges are <em>bi-directional</em>,
where we can go either away along each edge. A--B</p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-adjacency-matrix" class="anchor" href="#adjacency-matrix" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Adjacency Matrix</h4>

<p>Use a double-dimensional array with the size of both dimensions
equal to the number of vertices. </p>

<p>Each element indicates whether or not there is an edge between
vertex i and vertex j. </p>

<p>e.g. bool graph [5][5]; graph[0][3] = true; </p>

<p>This would be a directed graph. Unless, [0][3] and [3][0] are
marked true. [0][0] would indicate a self-connection. </p>

<p>This could be changed to values, and even have different values
depending on which way we go. These are directed edges. </p>

<p>This is called an <strong>adjacency matrix</strong>. </p>

<p>A BST is a form of a directed graph: we can only 
go to the children, and is a directed acylic graph (DAG). </p>

<p>To represent an undirected graph, just bi-directionally connect:
[i][j] == [j][i]</p>

<p>An interesting property around them: </p>

<p>If we multiply the matrix by itself: All the people
are now identified as 2 connnections away from someone 
away. The first one marked those 1 away. Do it again,
and we get 3 away. </p>

<p>"Why does that work?" "I have no idea." </p>

<p>I'll double-check the matrix math after I take 33A. </p>

<p>Facebook and other social networks do not use this
technique; this would involve very large arrays.
This was referenced in the first lecture!</p>

<p>"In some cases, one friend." </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-adjacency-list" class="anchor" href="#adjacency-list" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Adjacency List</h3>

<p>We can use an array of linked lists or a 
vector of linked lists to represent a graph. </p>

<p>If we add a number j to list i, there is an edge
from vertex i to vertex j.</p>

<pre><code>// 0--&gt;3
graph[0].push_back(3); 
</code></pre>

<p>Each list lets us know the connections. </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-list-vs-matrix" class="anchor" href="#list-vs-matrix" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>List vs. Matrix</h3>

<p>Scenario #1: 10 million users where most people
have a few hundred people. Instead of a 10x10 million
array, use an adjacency list instead. </p>

<p>This is called a <strong>sparse graph</strong>, where there are only
a few connections from one part to another part. </p>

<p>Scenario #2: 1,000 cities with airlines from city to 
almost every other city. Use an adjacency matrix. </p>

<p>Low node connectivity (many items) --&gt; use an adjacency list</p>

<p>High node connectivity (few items) --&gt; use an adjacency matrix</p>

<p>Consider the specific use case. It would be faster
to search an adjacency matrix. </p>

<p>We could have an array of hash tables or an array of sets, etc. </p>

<p>Use an adjacency matrix where there are lots of edges
between few vertices (<strong>dense graph</strong>). The number of cells
grows O(N^2). </p>

<p>Use an adjacency list for where there are few edges
and lots of vertices (&gt;10,000), e.g. Facebook. </p>

<p>Individual vertices are connected to few other vertices. </p>

<p>High school dating habits: "We would call those man-whores or sluts." </p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-graph-traversals" class="anchor" href="#graph-traversals" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Graph Traversals</h3>

<p>A tree is a kind of graph. </p>

<p>Depth-first and breadth-first traversals. </p>

<p>Depth-first is like a pre-order traversal. Use recursion! </p>

<p>Breadth-first is like a level-order traversal. Use a queue! </p>

<p>Depth-first keeps moving till it hits a dead end or a 
previous-ly visited vertex, then it backtracks and tries
another path. (Check if previously-visited; no bread crumbs used)</p>

<p>Use a stack or recursion.</p>

<p>Breadth-first keeps exploring in growing concentric circles,
exploring all vertices 1 away, 2 away, etc. </p>

<p>This is like tree traversal and like our maze exploration. </p>

<h4>
<a id="user-content-depth-first-traversals" class="anchor" href="#depth-first-traversals" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Depth-first Traversals</h4>

<pre><code>If already visited
    RETURN 

Mark current as visited
Process

For each edge leaving current
Determine vertex the edge takes us to (direction depends on use case, maybe)
Call depth-first traversal on that vertex
</code></pre>

<p>Real algo doesn't visit already-visited vertices</p>

<h3>
<a id="user-content-breadth-first-traversals" class="anchor" href="#breadth-first-traversals" aria-hidden="true"><span aria-hidden="true" class="octicon octicon-link"></span></a>Breadth-first Traversals</h3>

<p>Process current, then 1 away, then 2 away, then 3 away. Go
out via the mutual friends. This can be done with a queue. </p>

<pre><code>Add start vertex
mark as discovered

While queue not empty
</code></pre>

<p>And that's it! Loud applause! :D</p>
